The Need for a Mukhassis in Choosing Specific Moments
Philosophers argue that a mukhassis is needed to choose a specific moment among equals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that a specifier (mukhassis) is required to choose a specific moment among equal possibilities, and why this challenges the Ash'ari view of divine will.

Core Idea
Without a mukhassis, no choice among equals is possible.

In this passage, Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers against the Ash'ari claim that the divine will can freely choose among equals without a cause. The philosophers argue that times are equal in possibility, so a specifier (mukhassis) is needed to pick one moment over another. This same reasoning applies to qualities like whiteness vs. blackness, motion vs. rest. The objection sets the stage for Ghazali's famous defense of divine will as the ultimate specifier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the philosophers present their argument as a question, challenging the Ash'ari position.
The passage is a single continuous objection.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the principle: 'a thing is not distinguished from its like except by a mukhassis'. Understand why this is presented as self-evident (by necessity).
This is the core of the objection.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples: times, whiteness/blackness, motion/rest. Why are they 'equals'? How does the argument apply to each?
They all illustrate the same logical problem.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The philosophers conclude that without a mukhassis, the world could arise without a specifier. Reflect on why this is considered absurd and why it threatens the Ash'ari theology.
This sets up Ghazali's later response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيم تنكرون على من يترك دعوى الضرورة ويدل عليه من وجه آخر ؟ ! وهو أن الأوقات متساوية في جواز تعلق الإرادة بها ، فما الذي ميز وقتاً معيناً عما قبله وعما بعده ، وليس محالاً أن يكون التقدم والتأخر مراداً ؟ بل في البياض والسواد ، والحركة والسكون ، فإنكم تقولون : يحدث البياض بالإرادة القديمة والمحل قابل للسواد قبوله للبياض ، فلم تعلقت الإرادة القديمة بالبياض دون السواد ؟ وما الذى ميز أحد الممكنين عن الآخر فى تعلق الإرادة به ؟ ونحن بالضرورة نعلم أن الشيء لا يتميز عن مثله إلا بمخصص ، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يحدث العالم - وهو ممكن الوجود ، كما أنه ممكن العدم ـ ويتخصص بجهة دون جهة من غير مخصص.
If it is said: On what basis do you deny someone who abandons the claim of necessity and argues from another angle? Namely, that times are equal in the possibility of the will's relation to them. So what distinguished a specific time from before and after it? And it is not impossible for priority and posteriority to be willed? Rather, also in whiteness and blackness, motion and rest. For you say: Whiteness occurs by the eternal will, and the place is as receptive to blackness as it is to whiteness. So why did the eternal will relate to whiteness rather than blackness? And what distinguished one of the two possibilities from the other in the will's relation to it? We know by necessity that a thing is not distinguished from its like except by a mukhassis (specifier). If that were permissible, it would be permissible for the world—which is possible in existence as it is possible in non-existence—to occur and to be specified with a direction without a mukhassis.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a classical kalam objection from the philosophers. They argue that if we set aside claims of necessity, we must ask: among equal times, what made the will choose this specific moment? Similarly, among equal opposites (whiteness/blackness, motion/rest), what made the eternal will choose one over the other? The principle is that a thing cannot be distinguished from its equal without a mukhassis. If it were possible without a mukhassis, then the world could come into existence without a specifier, which is absurd. Thus the philosophers demand a rational explanation for the specific choice of the will, challenging the Ash'ari position that the divine will is free to choose without a cause. Ghazali does not answer here; the objection is posed for later refutation.

إرادة قديمة
تخصيص
مخصص
تساوي
جواز
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari position denies the need for any specifier; they affirm God's will is the specifier without external cause.
Correction: The Ash'ari do affirm that God's will is the mukhassis, but they deny that it requires a cause or reason beyond itself. The philosophers' objection is that even God's will needs a reason to choose one equal over another, which the Ash'ari reject.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' objection, what is needed to choose a specific time for creation among equal times?
A) A random event.
B) A mukhassis (specifier).
C) The intrinsic nature of the time.
D) The will of the world itself.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that a specifier (mukhassis) is necessary to distinguish among equal possibilities, challenging the Ash'ari view of a free divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the specific moments of your life—the timing of a trial, a delay, a blessing—do you truly believe that God's will, and nothing else, distinguished that moment from all others? Or do you secretly wish for a different time, as if your preference were more appropriate? Today, can you surrender to His timing as the only perfect specifier, even when it seems arbitrary?
?
The philosophers demand a reason why the eternal will chose whiteness over blackness. In your own struggles, do you demand to know why God gave you this trial instead of another? What would it take for you to accept that His will needs no justification outside itself, and that He is absolutely wise in His choice? Will you stop demanding explanations and start trusting His specifier?
?
Consider a decision you face where two options seem equally good. How does this lesson challenge you to rely on God's specification through prayer and seeking guidance (istikharah), rather than relying on your own reasoning? Are you willing to actively ask God to be your mukhassis, and then accept the outcome as His chosen best for you, even if it is not what you expected?

Why Divine Will Must Specify: Ghazali's Defense
Ghazali refutes the objection that divine will cannot explain why one time is chosen over another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that divine will necessarily specifies one of two possibilities without requiring a further cause.

Core Idea
Divine will is the attribute whose very essence is to specify; it needs no further explanation.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a key objection from the philosophers: if times are equal, what makes God's will choose one? He argues that the will is precisely the attribute that distinguishes, and asking why it specifies is like asking why knowledge knows. This lesson unpacks his layered response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the opponent claims times are equal and asks for a specifier. Understand the force of the objection: if times are equal, what determines God's choice?
The objection targets the very idea of a specifying will.
2
Follow Ghazali's refutation
He presents a series of possible replies and shows each leads to absurdity or is unnecessary. Pay attention to how he turns the tables on the objector.
Ghazali uses a dialectical method, entertaining the opponent's moves.
3
Understand the final answer
Will is an attribute whose property is to specify, analogous to knowledge. Just as knowledge necessarily encompasses the known, will necessarily specifies one of two likes.
This is the central insight: will is defined by specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيم تنكرون على من يترك دعوى الضرورة ويدل عليه من وجه آخر ؟ ! وهو أن الأوقات متساوية في جواز تعلق الإرادة بها ، فما الذي ميز وقتاً معيناً عما قبله وعما بعده ، وليس محالاً أن يكون التقدم والتأخر مراداً ؟ ؛ بل في البياض والسواد ، والحركة والسكون ، فإنكم تقولون : يحدث البياض بالإرادة القديمة والمحل قابل للسواد قبوله للبياض ، فلم تعلقت الإرادة القديمة بالبياض دون السواد ؟ وما الذى ميز أحد الممكنين عن الآخر فى تعلق الإرادة به ؟ ونحن بالضرورة نعلم أن الشيء لا يتميز عن مثله إلا بمخصص ، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يحدث العالم - وهو ممكن الوجود ، كما أنه ممكن العدم ـ ويتخصص جانب الوجود المماثل لجانب العدم فى الإمكان ، بغير مخصص وإن قلتم : إن الإرادة خصصت ، فالسؤال على اختصاص الإرادة ، وأنها لم اختصت ؟ . فإن قلتم : القديم لا يقال له لم ؛ فليكن العالم قديماً ولا يطلب لأن صانعه وسببه ، القديم لا يقال فيه : لم ؛ فإن جاز تخصص القديم بالاتفاق بأحد الممكنين فغاية المستبعد أن يقول : العالم مخصوص بهيئات مخصوصة كان يجوز أن يكون على هيئات أخرى بدلا منها ، فيقال : وقع كذلك اتفاقاً ، اختصت الإرادة بوقت دون وقت ، وهيأة دون هيأة ، اتفاقاً ، وإن قلتم : إن هذا السؤال غير لازم ، لأنه وارد على كل ما يريده البارى ، وعائد على كل ما يقدره ، فنقول : لا ، بل هذا السؤال لازم ؛ لأنه عائد في كل وقت وملازم لمن خالفنا على كل تقدير قلنا : إنما وجد العالم حيث وجد ، وعلى الوصف الذي وجد ، وفى المكان الذى وجد ، بالإرادة ؛ والإرادة صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ، ولولا أن هذا شأنها ، لوقع الاكتفاء بالقدرة ، ولكن لما تساوت نسبة القدرة إلى الضدين ولم يكن بد من مخصص يخصص الشيء عن مثله ، فقيل : للقديم – وراء القدرة - صفة من شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، فقول القائل : لم اختصت - الإرادة بأحد المثلين ؟ كقول القائل : لم اقتضى العلم الإحاطة بالمعلوم على ما هو به ؟ فيقال : لأن العلم عبارة عن صفة هذا شأنها ، فكذا الإرادة عبارة عن صفة هذا شأنها ، بل ذاتها تمييز الشيء عن مثله
For if it is said: Why do you reject someone who abandons the claim of necessity and argues from another angle? That is: times are equal in the possibility of the will attaching to them, so what distinguished a particular time from what was before it and after it, while it is not impossible that precedence and delay be willed? Rather, also in whiteness and blackness, motion and rest, for you say: whiteness occurs by the eternal will, and the substrate is as receptive to blackness as it is to whiteness. So why did the eternal will attach to whiteness rather than blackness? And what distinguished one of the two possibilities over the other in the will's attachment? We know by necessity that a thing is not distinguished from its like except by a specifier, and if that were allowed, it would be allowed that the world—while it is possible in existence, just as it is possible in non-existence—be brought about and the side of existence similar to the side of non-existence in possibility become specified without a specifier. And if you say: The will specified, then the question concerns the specification of the will: why did it specify? For if you say: The eternal is not asked 'why', then let the world be eternal and not require a maker and cause, for the eternal is not asked 'why'. For if the specification of the eternal by chance by one of the two possibilities is allowed, then the farthest the objector can go is to say: The world is specified with particular states, while it could have been in states other than them, so it is said: It happened likewise by chance; the will specified a time rather than a time, and a state rather than a state, by chance. And if you say: This question is not necessary, because it applies to everything the Creator wills and returns to everything He determines, we say: No, rather this question is necessary, because it returns in every time and is attendant upon whoever opposes us in every estimation. We say: The world only exists where it exists, in the description it exists, and in the place it exists, by the will; and the will is an attribute whose property is to distinguish a thing from its like. Were it not for this property, power would suffice. But because the relation of power to opposites is equal, and a specifier is inevitably needed to specify a thing from its like, it is said: The eternal—beyond power—has an attribute whose property is to specify a thing from its like. So the statement of the one who says: Why did the will specify one of the two likes? is like the statement of the one who says: Why did knowledge necessitate encompassing the known as it is? It is said: Because knowledge is an attribute whose property is this. So likewise, will is an attribute whose property is this; rather, its very essence is the distinguishing of a thing from its like.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response sequence. The objection begins by asserting that times are equal in their suitability for the attachment of divine will, so why does will choose one time over another? The same applies to attributes like whiteness versus blackness. The opponent claims that without a specifier (mukhasis), it would be possible for the world to exist without a cause. Ghazali then presents a series of hypothetical replies from the opponent: (1) If you say the will specified, then why did it specify? (2) If you say the eternal is not asked 'why', then let the world be eternal and not require a maker. (3) If you allow specification by chance, then the world's specific states could be by chance. (4) If you say the question is not necessary because it applies to everything, Ghazali counters that it is necessary and recurring. Finally, Ghazali gives his answer: The world exists precisely because of the will, which is the attribute whose very function is to distinguish one thing from its like. Without this attribute, power (qudra) alone would suffice, but since power relates equally to opposites, a specifying attribute is needed. He draws an analogy with knowledge: just as knowledge necessarily encompasses the known as it is, so will necessarily specifies one of two likes. The nature of will is to specify; thus asking 'why did it specify' is like asking 'why does knowledge know?'—it is its essential property.

الإرادة (al-irada)
المخصص (al-mukhasis)
المثلين (al-mithlayn)
القدرة (al-qudra)
العلم (al-`ilm)
القديم (al-qadim)
الضرورة (al-darura)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine will is arbitrary or that its specification is unexplained.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes that will is by definition the attribute of specification, so no further cause is needed.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the question 'why did the will specify one of two likes' invalid?
A) Because the will is eternal and cannot be questioned.
B) Because the will is the attribute whose property it is to specify, just as knowledge is the attribute whose property it is to encompass the known.
C) Because the question only applies to created things.
D) Because the will acts by chance and chance needs no explanation.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is the attribute whose essence is to specify one of two likes; asking why it does so is like asking why knowledge knows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you found yourself asking 'why did God will this for me?' How does Ghazali's argument that the very nature of will is to specify—without needing any further reason—challenge your impulse to demand an explanation from God? What would it mean for you to submit to that truth today? Can you name one specific area where you need to stop asking 'why' and simply trust that God's will is the ultimate specifier?
?
When you face a difficult trial, do you truly believe that God's will is the attribute that specifies that exact moment for you? Or do you secretly believe that chance or your own decisions had a role? How does this lesson confront that belief? What concrete step can you take to affirm God's absolute specification in your life this week?

The Divine Will as Distinguisher of Equals
Ghazali defends divine will as a distinguisher of equals against the objection of timelessness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that divine will is a self-sufficient distinguisher of equals, requiring no external motive.

Core Idea
Will distinguishes equals without needing a reason.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a powerful objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of divine will. Opponents argue that if God's will could choose among equals, we would need to ask why it chose one over another. Ghazali responds by showing that the very nature of will is to distinguish, just as knowledge is to know.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the dialectical style: objection, reply, counter.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent asks: if will specified, why did it specify this particular time and state? Find this question in the passage.
The objection is that specification seems arbitrary without a reason.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali first notes that saying 'the eternal is not asked why' is not a sufficient reply, then argues the question is necessary. Then he explains that will is an attribute whose property is to distinguish, just as knowledge encompasses the known.
He uses an analogy: will is like knowledge in being defined by its function.
4
Reflect on the key concept
Consider why Ghazali thinks this refutes the need for a motive. The will itself is the specifier; it does not need something else to specify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جانب الوجود المماثل لجانب العدم فى الإمكان ، بغير مخصص · وإن قلتم : إن الإرادة خصصت ، فالسؤال على اختصاص الإرادة ، وأنها لم اختصت؟ فإن قلتم : القديم لا يقال له لم ؛ فليكن العالم قديماً ولا يطلب لأن صانعه وسببه ، القديم لا يقال فيه : لم ؛ فإن جاز تخصص القديم بالاتفاق بأحد الممكنين فغاية المستبعد أن يقول : العالم مخصوص بهيئات مخصوصة كان يجوز أن يكون على هيئات أخرى بدلا منها ، فيقال : وقع كذلك اتفاقاً ، اختصت الإرادة بوقت دون وقت ، وهيأة دون هيأة ، اتفاقاً ، وإن قلتم : إن هذا السؤال غير لازم ، لأنه وارد على كل ما يريده البارى ، وعائد على كل ما يقدره ، فنقول : لا ، بل هذا السؤال لازم ؛ لأنه عائد في كل وقت وملازم لمن خالفنا على كل تقدير قلنا : إنما وجد العالم حيث وجد ، وعلى الوصف الذي وجد ، وفى المكان الذى وجد ، بالإرادة ؛ والإرادة صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ، ولولا أن هذا شأنها ، لوقع الاكتفاء بالقدرة ، ولكن لما تساوت نسبة القدرة إلى الضدين ولم يكن بد من مخصص يخصص الشيء عن مثله ، فقيل : للقديم – وراء القدرة - صفة من شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، فقول القائل : لم اختصت - الإرادة بأحد المثلين ؟ كقول القائل : لم اقتضى العلم الإحاطة بالمعلوم على ما هو به ؟ فيقال : لأن العلم عبارة عن صفة هذا شأنها ، فكذا الإرادة عبارة عن صفة هذا شأنها ، بل ذاتها تمييز الشيء عن مثله
The side of existence resembling the side of non-existence in possibility, without a specifier. And if you say: the will specified, then the question is about the specification of the will, and why did it specify? For if you say: the eternal is not asked 'why', then let the world be eternal and not require a maker and cause, for the eternal is not asked 'why' concerning it. For if the specification of the eternal by agreement to one of the two possibilities is allowed, then the most extreme of what can be said is: the world is specified with particular states, whereas it could have been in other states instead of them, so it is said: it occurred thus by agreement; the will specified a time rather than a time, and a state rather than a state, by agreement. And if you say: this question is not necessary, because it applies to everything the Creator wills and returns to everything He decrees, we say: no, rather this question is necessary, because it returns in every time and accompanies whoever opposes us on every estimation. We say: the world came into existence where it did, and in the description it did, and in the place it did, by will; and the will is an attribute whose property is to distinguish a thing from its equal. And were this not its property, power would suffice, but since the relation of power to two opposites is equal and there is no alternative to a specifier that specifies a thing from its equal, it is said: the Eternal has, beyond power, an attribute whose property is to specify a thing from its equal. So the statement of the one who says: 'why did the will specify one of the two equals?' is like the statement of the one who says: 'why does knowledge necessitate encompassing the known as it is?' It is said: because knowledge is an expression for an attribute whose property is this; likewise the will is an expression for an attribute whose property is this, rather its very essence is the distinguishing of a thing from its equal.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an argument-defense. Ghazali presents an objection: if the world is eternal and does not need a cause, then why does it exist in this specific form? The opponent says the will specified it, but then why did the will specify that particular time and state? If one says the eternal is not asked 'why', then one could simply say the world is eternal and needs no cause. The opponent says that the specification happened by agreement (ittifāq). Ghazali refutes that the question is necessary (lāzim) because it applies to every act of the Creator. He then explains that the will is an attribute whose function (sha'n) is to distinguish (tamyīz) between equals. Just as knowledge by its nature encompasses the known, so will by its nature distinguishes. This is a standard Ash'ari argument: the will is not just a cause but a specific attribute that chooses between possibilities without any external motive.

Misconception: Specifying between equals requires a motive or reason for the choice.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the will itself, by its very nature, distinguishes without needing an external motive, just as knowledge knows.

The Objection of Distinguishing Equals
Ghazali clarifies that two things are never identical in every respect, responding to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that distinguishing equals is contradictory fails, and how Ghazali uses the concepts of place and time to show that equality is never absolute.

Core Idea
Two things are never identical in every respect—difference in place or time always provides a distinction.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a philosophical objection: if two things are truly equal, any attribute that distinguishes them would be contradictory. He clarifies that in the physical world, two things are never completely identical; they always differ in location or time. This resolves the apparent contradiction and salvages the concept of an attribute that distinguishes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the structure of the objection and response become clear.
Notice the strong opening phrase 'فإن قيل' indicating an objection.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent claims that an attribute whose purpose is to distinguish a thing from its equal is contradictory. Why? Because if two things are equal, they have no distinction; if they are distinguished, they are not equal.
This is a logical dilemma.
3
Examine Ghazali's counterexamples
Ghazali gives two examples: two black objects in different places, and two black objects in the same place but different times. In each case, there is a distinction (place or time) that prevents absolute equality.
These examples show that equality is never absolute in real objects.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that when we say two things are 'equals,' we mean they share a specific property, not that they are identical in every respect. If place and time were the same, they would not be two distinct things at all.
Thus, distinguishing between things that are equal in some respects is perfectly intelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إثبات صفة شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله غير معقول ، بل هو متناقض ؛ فإن كونه مثلاً معناه أنه لا تميز له ؛ وكونه مميزاً معناه أنه ليس مثلا ؛ ولا ينبغى أن يظن أن السوادين فى محلين متماثلان من كل وجه ، لأن هذا في محل ، وذاك فى محل آخر ، وهذا يوجب التمييز ، ولا السوادين في وقتين في محل واحد متماثلان مطلقاً ، لأن هذا فارق ذلك فى الوقت ، فكيف يساويه من كل وجه ؟ ! وإذا قلنا : السوادان مثلان ، عنينا به فى السوادية مضافاً إليهما على الخصوص لا على الإطلاق ، و إلا فلو اتحد المحل والزمان ولم يبق تغاير ، لم يعقل سوادان ، ولا عقلت الاثنينية أصلا
For if it is said: establishing an attribute whose property is to distinguish a thing from its equal is unintelligible, rather it is contradictory; because its being an equal means that it has no distinction, and its being distinguished means that it is not an equal. And it should not be thought that two blacks in two places are exactly alike in every respect, because this one is in a place and that one is in another place, and that necessitates distinction. Nor are two blacks in two times in one place absolutely alike, because this one differs from that one in time; so how can it equal it in every respect?! And when we say: 'two blacks are equals', we mean by that with respect to blackness attributed to them specifically, not absolutely. Otherwise, if place and time were unified and no difference remained, two blacks would not be conceivable, nor would duality be conceivable at all.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a clarification. The opponent argues that an attribute whose property is to distinguish a thing from its equal is contradictory because if two things are truly equal, they are indistinguishable; if they are distinguishable, they are not equal. Ghazali responds by clarifying that in reality, two things cannot be identical in every respect because they differ in place or time. He uses the example of two black objects in different places: they are not exactly alike because their locations differ. Similarly, two black objects in the same place but at different times differ in time. When we say they are 'equals' (mithlān), we mean they share a specific property (such as blackness) but not absolute sameness. If they were identical in all respects (same place and time), they would not be two distinct things, and duality itself would be inconceivable. Thus, the concept of 'distinguishing a thing from its equal' is not contradictory because in practice, there is always some distinction (like place or time) that prevents absolute equality.

Key Terms
مثل
تمييز
سواد
اثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two things can be absolutely identical in every respect.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that in reality, any two distinct things differ in place or time, so absolute equality is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, are two blacks in different places exactly alike in every respect?
A) Yes, they are exactly alike
B) No, because their places differ
C) No, because their times differ
D) Yes, because both are black

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the objection by showing that no two things are absolutely identical; difference in place or time always provides a basis for distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily judgments, do you assume that two people or situations are 'exactly the same' without considering their distinct contexts? How might this lesson's emphasis on inherent differences in place and time reshape your fairness in evaluating others?
?
You claim that something is 'just like' something else—but have you really examined the hidden distinctions of circumstance or timing? What would it cost you to admit that no two things are ever identical in every respect?
?
If even two black objects can never be absolutely equal, what does that say about your comparisons between yourself and others? Will you stop using false equivalences as an excuse for envy or complacency?

Divine Will and the Objection from Human Experience
Ghazali defends divine will against an objection based on human experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that divine will is not bound by human limitations and that rational proof supports its reality.

Core Idea
Divine will is not bound by human limitations; reason affirms what analogy denies.

This passage addresses a key objection: how can God have a will that distinguishes between identical things? The objector argues it's inconceivable based on human experience. Ghazali refutes this by showing the fallacy of comparing divine attributes to human ones, and insists that rational proof overrides analogical thinking. He also notes that the term 'will' is used by permission of revelation, not literal equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the illustrative example of the thirsty person.
2
Understand the illustration of human will
Focus on the example of two identical cups of water. Why does Ghazali say a human cannot choose between them without an external factor? This shows the limitation of will as we experience it.
This sets up the contrast with divine will.
3
Identify the objection and Ghazali's response
The objector says distinguishing equals is inconceivable. Ghazali asks: is that known necessarily or by speculation? Neither. He then compares it to the affirmation of an attribute that is neither inside nor outside the world, which is accepted by rational proof.
Ghazali turns the tables: if reason can affirm something unimaginable, it can affirm divine will.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation
Note that Ghazali says we use 'will' by permission of revelation, and if the name doesn't fit, another can be used. This shows the priority of rational proof and scripture over linguistic analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحقق هذا أن لفظ الإرادة مستعار من إرادتنا ، ولا يتصور منا أن نميز بالإرادة الشيء عن مثله ، بل لو كان بين يدى العطشان قدحان الماء من يتساويان من كل وجه بالإضافة إلى غرضه ، لم يمكن أن يأخذ أحدهما ، بل إنما يأخذ ما يراه أحسن ، أو أخف ، أو أقرب إلى جانب يمينه إن كانت عادته تحريك اليمين ، أو سبب من هذه الأسباب إما خفى وإما جلى ، وإلا فلا يتصور تمييز الشيء عن مثله بحال والاعتراض من وجهين : الأول : أن قولكم : إن هذا لا يتصور ، عرفتموه ضرورة أو نظراً ؟ ! ولا يمكن دعوى واحد منهما ، وتمثيلكم بإرادتنا مقايسة فاسدة ، تضاهى المقايسة في العلم ، وعلم الله يفارق علمنا في أمور كثيرة ، فلم تبعد المفارقة في الإرادة بل هو كقول القائل : ذات موجودة لا خارج العالم ولا داخله ، ولا متصلاً ولا منفصلاً ، لا يعقل ، لأنا لا نعقله في حقنا ، قيل : هذا عمل توهمك ، وأما دليل العقل فقد ساق العقلاء إلى التصديق بذلك ، فبم تنكرون على من يقول : دليل العقل ساق إلى إثبات صفة الله تعالى من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ؟ فإن لم يطابقها اسم الإرادة ، فلتسم باسم آخر ، فلا مشاحة في الأسماء وإنما أطلقناها نحن بإذن الشرع ، وإلا فالإرادة موضوعة فى اللغة لتعيين ما فيه غرض
This is confirmed by the fact that the word 'will' is metaphorical from our will, and it is inconceivable of us that we distinguish a thing from its equal by will. Rather, if before a thirsty person there are two cups of water that are equal from every aspect relative to his purpose, he could not take one of them; rather he only takes what he sees as better, or lighter, or closer to his right side if his habit is to move the right hand, or some cause among these causes either hidden or apparent; otherwise, distinguishing a thing from its equal is not conceivable at all. And the objection is two-fold: The first: that your statement 'this is inconceivable' - did you come to know it necessarily or by speculation? Neither claim is possible. And your analogy with our will is a corrupt comparison, similar to the comparison concerning knowledge; and God's knowledge differs from our knowledge in many matters, so why is difference unlikely in the case of will? Rather it is like the statement of one who says: an existing essence that is neither outside the world nor inside it, neither connected nor disconnected, is inconceivable because we do not conceive it in ourselves. It is said: this is the work of your imagination, but as for the proof of reason, the rational people have been led by it to affirm that. So how can you reject one who says: the proof of reason has led to affirming an attribute of God whose property is to distinguish a thing from its equal? If the name 'will' does not correspond to it, let it be called by another name, for there is no dispute over names, and we only use it by permission of revelation. Otherwise, 'will' in language is used for the determination of what contains a purpose.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a defence of the concept of divine will against objections based on human experience. Ghazali begins by acknowledging that the term 'will' (al-irāda) is metaphorical (musta'ār) when used for God, drawn from human will. He illustrates that for humans, distinguishing between two exactly similar options is impossible without some external factor, such as a slight difference or habit. This shows the limitation of human will. The objector then raises a two-fold objection; the first is presented here. The objector asks: how do you know that the concept of an attribute that distinguishes equals is inconceivable for God? Is it by necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) or speculative reasoning (naẓar)? Neither is possible. The analogy with human will is a false comparison (muqāyasa fāsida), just as one might wrongly compare God's knowledge to human knowledge. Ghazali counters by comparing it to the acceptance of an attribute that is neither inside nor outside the world—something unimaginable but affirmed by rational proof. Similarly, rational proof leads to affirming an attribute of God that distinguishes equals, even if the term 'will' seems inadequate. If the name is inappropriate, another name can be used; Muslims only use 'will' by permission of revelation (shar'), while its linguistic meaning implies purpose (gharaḍ).

Misconception: That divine will must operate like human will, so it cannot choose between identical alternatives.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this is a false analogy; God's attributes are not limited by human experience, and rational proof can affirm what seems inconceivable to us.

Ghazali shows that rational arguments can affirm divine attributes even when they seem unimaginable by human standards, and the term 'will' is used by permission of revelation, not literal equivalence.

?

The Impossibility of Distinguishing Identicals in Divine Will
Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection that distinguishing identicals is inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that divine will cannot distinguish between two perfectly identical things, and why they consider it contradictory.

Core Idea
Distinguishing identicals is impossible—even in human experience, an external factor must tip the scale.

This passage presents the first major objection against the divine attribute of will in the Tahafut. The philosophers argue that the very concept of a will that distinguishes between two perfectly identical things is incoherent. They support this with logical reasoning and an analogy from human psychology. Understanding this objection is crucial because Ghazali will later respond to it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is the philosophers' objection, not Ghazali's own view.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage opens with the claim that positing an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like is unintelligible. Identify why they say it is contradictory.
The contradiction lies in the definition of 'like' versus 'distinguisher'.
3
Analyze the example of two blacks
The philosophers argue that what we call 'likes' (e.g., two black objects) are not identical in every respect due to differences in place or time. If they were identical in every way, duality would be inconceivable.
This step shows that true identity is rare and undermines the notion of identicals.
4
Examine the human analogy
The passage ends with the example of a thirsty person with two identical cups of water. Without an external factor (like beauty or habit), choice is impossible. This supports the impossibility of distinguishing identicals.
This analogy is used to argue that divine will, if it were like human will, could not distinguish identicals either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إثبات صفة شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله غير معقول ، بل هو متناقض ؛ فإن كونه مثلاً معناه أنه لا تميز له ؛ وكونه مميزاً معناه أنه ليس مثلا ؛ ولا ينبغى أن يظن أن السوادين فى محلين متماثلان من كل وجه ، لأن هذا في محل ، وذاك فى محل آخر ، وهذا يوجب التمييز ، ولا السوادين في وقتين في محل واحد متماثلان مطلقاً ، لأن هذا فارق ذلك فى الوقت ، فكيف يساويه من كل وجه ؟ ! وإذا قلنا : السوادان مثلان ، عنينا به فى السوادية مضافاً إليهما على الخصوص لا على الإطلاق ، و إلا فلو اتحد المحل والزمان ولم يبق تغاير ، لم يعقل سوادان ، ولا عقلت الاثنينية أصلا يحقق هذا أن لفظ الإرادة مستعار من إرادتنا ، ولا يتصور منا أن نميز بالإرادة الشيء عن مثله ، بل لو كان بين يدى العطشان قدحان الماء من يتساويان من كل وجه بالإضافة إلى غرضه ، لم يمكن أن يأخذ أحدهما ، بل إنما يأخذ ما يراه أحسن ، أو أخف ، أو أقرب إلى جانب يمينه إن كانت عادته تحريك اليمين ، ، أو سبب من هذه الأسباب إما خفى وإما جلى ، وإلا فلا يتصور تمييز الشيء عن مثله بحال
For it is said: The affirmation of an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like is unintelligible, rather it is contradictory; for its being a like means that it has no distinction; and its being a distinguisher means that it is not a like. And it should not be thought that the two blacks in two places are identical in every respect, because this is in a place and that is in another place, and this necessitates distinction. Nor are the two blacks in two times in one place absolutely identical, because this differs from that in time, so how can it equal it in every respect? And when we say 'the two blacks are likes,' we mean thereby in blackness, applied to them specifically not absolutely; otherwise, if place and time were united and no otherness remained, two blacks would not be conceived, nor would duality be conceived at all. This is corroborated by the fact that the term 'will' (al-irāda) is borrowed from our will, and it is not conceivable from us that we distinguish by will a thing from its like; indeed, if before a thirsty person there were two cups of water equal in every respect in relation to his purpose, he could not take one of them; rather he only takes what he deems better, or lighter, or closer to his right side if his habit is to move the right, or some cause among these causes either hidden or manifest; otherwise, the distinction of a thing from its like is not conceivable at all.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' initial objection against the divine attribute of will (al-irāda). The passage begins by stating the objection: positing an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its identical is unintelligible and contradictory. The reason given is that being a 'like' (mithl) implies the absence of distinction, while being a 'distinguisher' implies the opposite. The argument then clarifies that two black objects in different places or times are not identical in every respect because of spatial and temporal differences. True identity would require complete unity of place and time, but then duality of subject is inconceivable. Next, the philosophers expand on this by noting that the term 'will' is borrowed from human experience, where distinguishing between two identical things without an external differentiating factor is impossible. The example of a thirsty person with two identical cups of water illustrates that some factor (like beauty, lightness, or habit) always intervenes; otherwise, no choice would be made. Thus, they conclude, distinguishing a thing from its like is impossible in the human case, and by extension in the divine case.

Key Terms
تمييز الشيء عن مثله
إرادة
المثل
السوادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali agrees with the philosophers' objection.
Correction: In fact, Ghazali presents this objection only to refute it in the following passages. He does not endorse the impossibility of divine will distinguishing identicals.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' objection, what is the problem with positing an attribute that distinguishes a thing from its like?
A) Such an attribute is impossible because it would require the thing to be both like and unlike simultaneously.
B) Such an attribute would be unnecessary because all things are already distinct.
C) Such an attribute would conflict with God's simplicity.
D) Such an attribute would imply that God's will is anthropomorphic.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that divine will cannot distinguish between identicals because such an act is rationally impossible, as shown by the human example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face two seemingly equal options—like choosing between two jobs, two courses of action, or even two trivial purchases—do you honestly acknowledge that there is always some subtle factor (preference, history, fear) tipping the scale? Or do you pretend they are identical to avoid admitting your own bias? What would it mean for your self-knowledge if you could trace that hidden factor?
?
The philosophers claim that distinguishing identicals is rationally impossible, even for God. But if God's will is not bound by human limitation, how does your own habit of claiming 'I have no reason' for your choices reveal a lack of trust in God's wisdom? Will you now examine the real reasons behind what you thought was arbitrary?
?
This passage shows that even the philosophers agree that some cause—hidden or manifest—must underlie every distinction. If you believe God's will is always with wisdom, then what does your complaint or confusion about God's decrees reveal about your refusal to accept that His wisdom may be hidden from you? Will you submit even when you see no reason?

First Counter-Objection: The Possibility of Specification Without External Factors
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that distinguishing identicals is impossible through epistemological critique and the two-date example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first counter-objection against the philosophers' claim that distinguishing identicals is impossible, and how the two-date example demonstrates the necessity of an attribute of specification.

Core Idea
A person can choose between identical dates, proving an attribute of specification beyond external factors.

Ghazali challenges the very basis of the philosophers' claim by asking whether they know it by necessity or proof. He then offers a powerful thought experiment: two identical dates before a desiring person who can take only one. If all external factors are equal, the person still chooses — this reveals an attribute of specification that explains voluntary action. This passage is a cornerstone of Ghazali's defense of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Follow the epistemological challenge
Ghazali opens by questioning how the philosophers know their claim. Identify the two modes of knowledge he mentions: necessity (ḍarūra) and proof (naẓar). Ask yourself: can they claim either?
Notice that Ghazali forces the philosophers to defend their epistemology.
2
Examine the analogy to human will
Ghazali says the philosophers' analogy to human will is a corrupt comparison. Understand why divine attributes differ from human attributes. Reflect on how God's knowledge differs from ours.
The point is that dissimilarity in one attribute (knowledge) suggests dissimilarity in another (will) is plausible.
3
Engage with the two-date example
Visualize two identical dates before a desiring person who cannot take both. The person takes one inevitably, by an attribute that specifies one from its like. Note that all external factors can be removed.
This is the core thought experiment that Ghazali uses to prove his point.
4
Understand the dilemma
Ghazali presents the philosophers with two alternatives: either they deny that such equality is possible (foolish) or they admit that the man would remain forever perplexed (false by necessity). Conclude that an attribute of specification is necessary.
This shows that even in human voluntary action, such an attribute exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض من وجهين : • الأول : أن قولكم : إن هذا لا يتصور ، عرفتموه ضرورة أو نظراً ؟ ! ولا يمكن دعوى واحد منهما ، وتمثيلكم بإرادتنا مقايسة فاسدة ، تضاهى المقايسة في العلم ، وعلم الله يفارق علمنا في أمور كثيرة ، فلم تبعد المفارقة في الإرادة بل هو كقول القائل : ذات موجودة لا خارج العالم ولا داخله ، ولا متصلاً ولا منفصلاً ، لا يعقل ، لأنا لا نعقله في حقنا ، قيل : هذا عمل توهمك ، وأما دليل العقل فقد ساق العقلاء إلى التصديق بذلك ، فبم تنكرون على من يقول : دليل العقل ساق إلى إثبات صفة الله تعالى من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ؟ فإن لم يطابقها اسم الإرادة ، فلتسم باسم آخر ، فلا مشاحة في الأسماء وإنما أطلقناها نحن بإذن الشرع ، وإلا فالإرادة موضوعة فى اللغة لتعيين ما فيه غرض ولا غرض فى حق الله ، وإنما المقصود المعنى دون اللفظ ، على أنه في حقنا لا نسلم أن ذلك (1) غير متصور ، فإنا نفرض تمرتين متساويتين بين يدى المتشوف إليهما العاجز عن تناولهما جميعاً ، فإنه يأخذ إحداهما لا محالة ، بصفة شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، وكل ما ذكرتموه من المخصصات من من الحسن أو القرب أو تيسر الأخذ ، فإنا نقدر على فرض انتفائه ويبقى إمكان الأخذ ، فأنتم بين أمرين : إما أن تقولوا : إنه لا يتصور التساوى بالإضافة إلى أغراضه قط ، وهو حماقة وفرضه ممكن ، وإما أن تقولوا : التساوى إذا فرض ، بقى الرجل المتشوف أبداً متحيراً ، ينظر إليهما فلا يأخذ إحداهما بمجرد الإرادة والاختيار المنفك عن الغرض ، وهو أيضاً محال يعلم بطلانه ضرورة ، فإذن لابد لكل ناظر شاهداً أو غائباً في تحقيق الفعل الاختيارى من إثبات صفة شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله
And the objection is from two aspects: • The first: That you say, 'This is not conceivable' – do you know it by necessity or by proof? Neither one can be claimed. And your analogy to our will is a corrupt comparison, resembling the comparison regarding knowledge, while God's knowledge differs from our knowledge in many matters; so why would difference in will be far? Rather it is like the saying of one who says: 'An existent entity neither outside the world nor inside it, neither connected nor disconnected – it is not intelligible because we do not conceive it in our own case.' It is said: 'This is the work of your imagination; as for the proof of reason, the rational have been led by it to affirm that.' So what do you deny to one who says: 'The proof of reason has led to affirming an attribute of God, the Exalted, whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like'? If the name 'will' (al-irāda) does not correspond to it, then let it be called by another name; there is no dispute about names. We merely apply it by permission of the Law; otherwise, 'will' in language is set for determining what involves a purpose (gharaḍ), and there is no purpose in God's right; rather the intended is the meaning not the word. Moreover, regarding ourselves we do not concede that this is inconceivable; for we suppose two identical dates before someone desiring them, unable to take both; then he takes one of them inevitably, by an attribute whose function is specification of a thing from its like. And everything you mentioned of specifying factors, like beauty, closeness, or ease of taking – we can suppose their absence, and the possibility of taking remains. So you are between two positions: either you say that equality in relation to his purposes is never conceivable – which is foolish, and its supposition possible; or you say that if equality is supposed, the desiring man would remain forever perplexed, looking at them and not taking one of them merely by will and choice free from purpose – and this also is impossible, known to be false by necessity. Thus, necessarily, for every observer, present or absent, in actualizing voluntary action, there must be affirming an attribute whose function is specification of a thing from its like.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's first counter-objection (al-wajh al-awwal) against the philosophers' claim that distinguishing identicals is impossible. Ghazali begins by challenging the epistemological basis: do the philosophers know this by necessity (ḍarūra) or by proof (naẓar)? Neither can be claimed. He then attacks their analogy to human will as a corrupt comparison, because God's attributes differ from human attributes in many ways, just as divine knowledge differs. He compares their reasoning to that of someone who denies an existent that is neither inside nor outside the world, simply because it is inconceivable to humans – a failure of imagination, not reason. Ghazali then asserts that rational proof (dalīl al-ʿaql) has led to affirming an attribute of specification. The name is secondary; even if 'will' is inappropriate, another name suffices, and they use 'will' by permission of the Law. Next, he refutes the philosophical claim about human limitation by providing a thought experiment: two identical dates before a person who desires them but cannot take both. The person will take one inevitably, by an attribute of specification. The external factors (beauty, closeness, etc.) can be imagined absent, yet the choice still occurs. Ghazali presents the philosophers with a dilemma: either they deny the possibility of such equality entirely (which is foolish) or they admit that under equality the man would remain forever perplexed (which is also false by necessity). Therefore, anyone explaining voluntary action – human or divine – must affirm an attribute that specifies one thing from its identical.

نظر
مقايسة
تخصيص
اختيار
تمييز الشيء عن مثله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ghazali denies the possibility of genuine equality in human choice, but he actually affirms it to prove that a quality of specification exists.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says we can suppose two identical dates, and the person still chooses, proving an attribute of specification exists even in human voluntary action.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main argument with the two identical dates example?
A) Humans cannot choose between truly identical objects, so divine will is unnecessary.
B) Humans do choose between objects even if all external factors are equal, proving an attribute of specification.
C) The dates are never truly identical because of hidden differences.
D) The choice between identical dates is illusory and requires divine intervention.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's two-date example shows that voluntary action requires an attribute that specifies one thing from its like, refuting the philosophers' claim of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision where you felt genuinely torn between two equally good options. What was the moment of 'specification' that made you choose one? Did you recognize an inner attribute beyond external reasons? How does that awareness change your understanding of your will and its connection to the Divine?
?
Ghazali says that even when all external factors are equal, you still inevitably choose. Can you honestly recall a time when your choice had no apparent cause yet felt decisive? What does that reveal about the state of your heart and your accountability before God for that choice?
?
If every voluntary action requires an attribute that specifies, then your everyday choices are not trivial. In your next moment of hesitation, will you pause to ask: 'What is specifying this choice – my desire for the world, or my desire for God?' How can you train your soul to let the attribute of taṣkhīṣ be guided by taqwā?

The Objection on Possibility and Power
Objection: possibility conditions divine power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that possibility is a necessary condition for being within divine power.

Core Idea
Only what is possible can be within God's power.

The philosophers raise a further objection against Ghazali's spatial analogy. They argue that not everything imaginable is possible; divine wisdom may fix certain limits. Therefore, God's power does not extend to impossible things, such as creating the world larger or smaller. This passage presents the objection, setting the stage for Ghazali's response in the following pages.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once. It is a single sentence containing the philosophers' claim. Notice the structure: 'If it is said: ...'
This is a self-contained objection.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the objection into its premises and conclusion. The philosophers state a general principle (what is not possible is not within power) and apply it to the specific case of the world's size.
The objection uses a syllogistic form.
3
Consider what is missing
Note that this passage only contains the objection. Ghazali's answer is not included here and will appear later. Think about how you might respond if you were Ghazali.
This prepares you for the upcoming response.

This passage is a further objection (فإن قيل) from the philosophers against Ghazali's spatial analogy. They argue that not everything imaginable is possible; in particular, the world being larger or smaller than it actually is may not be possible (because it might violate the actual order or divine wisdom). Therefore, God's power does not extend to impossible things. This objection challenges the assumption in Ghazali's analogy that God could have created the sphere larger or smaller. The response to this objection is not present in the given text, as the window ends here. The argument is incomplete.

The philosophers ground divine power in possibility, arguing that what is impossible falls outside the scope of omnipotence.

Attributing Non-Existence to Divine Power
Ghazali concludes that contingent non-existence is ascribable to divine power like existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that contingent non-existence, though not a thing, can be attributed to divine power just as existence can, and why this does not differ when the event is willed by an eternal will.

Core Idea
Non-existence, though not a thing, is still attributable to divine power.

In this concluding passage, Ghazali ties together his argument about the eternal will and the scope of divine power. He addresses a potential objection: how can non-existence, which is not a thing, be an act of God? His answer is subtle but powerful, showing that what matters is the intelligibility of the occurrence, not its ontological status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the concluding phrase 'And God knows best.'
2
Identify the key claim about non-existence
Find where Ghazali describes removal of an attribute as 'pure non-existence that has occurred.' This is the central point.
3
Trace the logical move
Follow how Ghazali moves from describing removal to concluding that such non-existence, though not a thing, is intelligible and attributable to divine power.
Focus on the phrase 'and that whose occurrence in itself is intelligible, even if it is not a thing, is intelligible to be attributed to the power of the powerful agent.'
4
Consider the implication for the eternal will
Reflect on the final conclusion: under an eternal will, existence and non-existence are symmetric as objects of divine power.

its opposite; and if they are absent, its meaning is the removal of existence without entailing an opposite, so its removal is an expression of pure non-existence that has occurred; thus the occurrence of accidental non-existence is intelligible; and that whose occurrence in itself is intelligible, even if it is not a thing, is intelligible to be attributed to the power of the powerful agent. So it becomes clear by this that whenever the occurrence of an event by an eternal will is conceived, the situation does not differ between what occurs being non-existence or existence. Commentary
This passage concludes a preceding argument about the possibility of attributing non-existence to divine power. Ghazali explains that when an attribute is removed, it is not replaced by its opposite, but rather pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ) sets in. This non-existence, though not a thing, is still intelligible and can be rationally attributed to the power of an agent. The key point is that if an event occurs by an eternal will, it makes no difference whether that event is the coming into being of existence or the bringing about of non-existence—both are equally within the scope of divine power. Ghazali ends with 'And God knows best,' signaling the closure of this argument.

The First Objection: Existence vs. Possibility
Ghazali's first objection: the dilemma of existence vs. possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection against the philosophers' claim that 'possibility of existence' is a distinct meaning that generates multiplicity in the first effect, and how he turns their own logic against them.

Core Idea
If possibility is distinct from existence, then necessity is also distinct—implying multiplicity in God.

In this passage, Imam Ghazali launches his first objection against the philosophers' argument that the first intellect contains three distinct meanings (possibility, existence, quiddity). He challenges the very foundation: is 'possibility of existence' identical to existence or something else? By exposing a dilemma, he aims to show that the philosophers' own reasoning would force them to admit multiplicity in the First Principle, which they deny.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dilemma
Read the opening question: Is 'being possible of existence' identical to existence or other than it? Notice how Ghazali sets up a dichotomy.
This is the pivot of the entire objection.
2
Follow the two branches
Trace what happens if possibility is identical to existence (no multiplicity) and if it is distinct (then the same applies to necessity in God).
Ghazali uses the opponents' own claim to generate an unwanted conclusion.
3
Examine the dialectical exchanges
Notice how Ghazali anticipates and refutes possible replies: e.g., 'necessity is identical to existence' is countered by 'then possibility is also identical'.
The argument proceeds back and forth.
4
Grasp the final argument about true unity
Read Ghazali's concluding point: true unity is where both affirmation and negation of a property are impossible. Since one can say 'exists but not necessary', necessity and existence are distinct.
This undermines the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : هو أنا نقول : ادعيتم أن أحد معاني الكثرة في المعلول الأول أنه ممكن الوجود ، فنقول : كونه ممكن الوجود ، عين وجوده أم غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه ، فلا تنشأ عنه كثرة ، وإن كان غيره ، فهلا قلتم : في المبدأ الأول كثرة ، لأنه موجود وهو مع ذلك واجب الوجود ، فوجوب الوجود غير نفس الوجود ، فليجز صدور المختلفات منه ، لهذه الكثرة ؛ فإن قيل : لا معنى لوجوب الوجود إلا الوجود ، قلنا : فلا معنى لإمكان الوجود إلا الوجود ، فإن قلتم : يمكن يعرف كونه موجوداً ، ولا يعرف كونه ممكناً ، فهو غيره ، قلنا : فكذا واجب الوجود ، يمكن أن يعرف وجوده ، ولا يعرف وجوبه ، إلا بعد دليل آخر ، فليكن غيره ؛ وبالجملة الوجود أمر عام ، ينقسم إلى واجب وإلى ممكن ، فإن كان فصل أحد القسمين زائداً على العام ، فكذا الفصل الثانى ؛ ولا فرق .
فإن قيل : إمكان الوجود له من ذاته ، ووجوده من غيره ، فكيف يكون ذاته ، وماله من غيره، واحداً ؟ ! 
قلنا : وكيف يكون وجوب الوجود هو عين الوجود ؟ ! ويمكن أن ينفى وجوب الوجود، ويثبت الوجود ؟ والواحد الحق من كل وجه هو الذى لا يتسع للنفى والإثبات أصلا ، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه موجود ، وليس بموجود ! أو واجب الوجود ، وليس بواجب الوجود ؛ ويمكن أن يقال ؛ موجود ، وليس بواجب الوجود ، كما يمكن أن يقال : موجود ، وليس بممكن الوجود ، وإنما تعرف الوحدة بهذا ، فلا يستقيم تقدير ذلك في الأول ، إن صح ما ذكروه من أن إمكان الوجود غير الوجود الممكن
The first: We say: You claimed that one of the meanings of multiplicity in the first effect is that it is possible of existence. We say: Is its being possible of existence identical to its existence or other than it? If it is identical, then no multiplicity arises from it; but if it is other, then why do you not say that in the first principle there is multiplicity, because it exists and at the same time is necessary of existence, so necessity of existence is other than existence itself? Then let the different things proceed from it due to this multiplicity. If it is said: Necessity of existence has no meaning other than existence, we say: Then possibility of existence has no meaning other than existence. If you say: It is possible to know that it exists without knowing that it is possible, so possibility is other than existence, we say: Likewise, the necessary of existence can be known to exist without knowing its necessity, except through another proof, so let necessity be other than existence. In summary, existence is a common thing that divides into necessary and possible. If the differentia of one of the two divisions is additional to the common, then likewise the second differentia; there is no difference. If it is said: Possibility of existence belongs to it from itself, and its existence from another, so how can its essence and that which is from another be one? We say: And how can necessity of existence be identical to existence? It is possible to deny necessity of existence while affirming existence. The truly one in every aspect is that which does not admit of negation and affirmation at all, for it cannot be said that it is existent and not existent, or necessary of existence and not necessary of existence. But it can be said: existent and not necessary of existence, just as it can be said: existent and not possible of existence. Oneness is known by this. So such an estimation cannot stand in the first, if what they claimed that possibility of existence is other than possible existence is true.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's first objection against the triplicity argument. He begins by attacking the claim that 'possibility of existence' is one of the three meanings producing multiplicity in the first intellect. He asks: is possibility identical to existence or distinct? If identical, it cannot generate multiplicity. If distinct, then the same reasoning would apply to the First Principle (God), which has both existence and necessity of existence (distinct meanings), thus multiplicity would also be generated from the First—which they deny. The objection proceeds through a dialectic: if the opponent says necessity is identical to existence, then possibility is also identical. If the opponent says possibility is known separately, the same applies to necessity. Ghazali concludes that existence is a common genus divided into necessary and possible; if one differentia is additional, so is the other with no difference. Then the opponent brings a new argument: possibility is from the essence, existence from another, so they are distinct. Ghazali replies by questioning how necessity could be identical to existence if one can be negated while the other affirmed. True unity, he argues, is where both affirmation and negation of a property are impossible; but here, one can say something exists but is not necessary, so they are distinct. Hence the philosophers' own reasoning fails.

Key Terms
إمكان الوجود
وجوب الوجود
الوجود العام
الممكن
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies any distinction between existence and its modalities (necessity/possibility).
Correction: Ghazali does not deny the conceptual distinction; rather, he shows that if the philosophers accept it for the first effect, they must also accept it for God, leading to multiplicity.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's central point in the first objection?
A) Possibility and existence are always identical
B) The philosophers' distinction between existence and possibility implies multiplicity in the First Principle
C) The first intellect has no real aspects
D) Necessity of existence is the same as existence

Key Takeaway
Ghazali exposes an inconsistency: if possibility is distinct from existence, then necessity must also be distinct, forcing multiplicity in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah is 'Wajib al-Wujud', do you ever mentally separate His existence from His necessity? How does Ghazali's argument challenge you to ensure your thoughts about Allah's oneness are free from any form of multiplicity?
?
The philosophers relied on logical reasoning and fell into contradiction. In your own study of aqeedah, what safeguards do you have against your intellect leading you to conclusions that undermine Allah's absolute unity?
?
If you were to explain to someone that 'Allah is One' and that His attributes are not separate entities, how would this lesson shape your explanation? Are you personally convinced that you can hold that belief without internal inconsistency?

Ghazali's Argument that Necessity is Additional to Existence
Ghazali argues that necessity is additional to existence, refuting an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the necessity of existence is a distinct attribute from existence itself, and how this introduces multiplicity in the First.

Core Idea
Necessity is not identical to existence; it is an added differentia.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali confronts the philosophers' claim that the Necessary Existent cannot have any multiplicity. He argues that the very attribute of necessity introduces a distinction from existence, thereby allowing for multiplicity. The passage employs logical reasoning and analogical refutation to establish his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument.
Pay attention to the objection and Ghazali's response.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Notice that Ghazali begins by asserting that necessity (wujub) is other than existence (wujud), thus introducing multiplicity.
3
Follow the objection and response
The objection claims necessity is nothing but existence. Ghazali refutes by analogy with possibility (imkan): if possibility is different from existence, then necessity must also be different.
He uses a parallel argument.
4
Understand the final objection and conclusion
The opponent argues that possibility belongs to essence while existence comes from another. Ghazali counters that if necessity were identical to existence, one could not negate necessity while affirming existence, but this is possible, proving distinction.
He concludes that the truly one does not admit such separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كثرة، لأنه موجود وهو مع ذلك واجب الوجود، فوجوب الوجود غير نفس الوجود، فليجز صدور المختلفات منه، لهذه الكثرة؛ فإن قيل: لا معنى لوجوب الوجود إلا الوجود، قلنا: فلا معنى لإمكان الوجود إلا الوجود، فإن قلتم: يمكن يعرف كونه موجوداً، ولا يعرف كونه ممكناً، فهو غيره، قلنا: فكذا واجب الوجود، يمكن أن يعرف وجوده، ولا يعرف وجوبه، إلا بعد دليل آخر، فليكن غيره؛ وبالجملة الوجود أمر عام، ينقسم إلى واجب وإلى ممكن، فإن كان فصل أحد القسمين زائداً على العام، فكذا الفصل الثاني؛ ولا فرق. فإن قيل: إمكان الوجود له من ذاته، ووجوده من غيره، فكيف يكون ذاته، وماله من غيره، واحداً؟ قلنا: وكيف يكون وجوب الوجود هو عين الوجود؟! ويمكن أن ينفى وجوب الوجود، ويثبت الوجود؟ والواحد الحق من كل وجه هو الذي لا يتسع للنفي والإثبات أصلاً، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال: إنه موجود، وليس بموجود! أو واجب الوجود، وليس بواجب الوجود؛ ويمكن أن يقال: موجود، وليس بواجب الوجود، كما يمكن أن يقال: موجود، وليس بممكن الوجود، وإنما تعرف الوحدة بهذا، فلا يستقيم تقدير ذلك في الأول، إن صح ما ذكروه من أن إمكان الوجود غير الوجود الممكن.
Multiplicity, because it exists and yet is necessary of existence, so necessity of existence is other than existence itself, so let the emanation of different things from it be permissible due to this multiplicity. If it is said: There is no meaning to necessity of existence except existence, we say: Then there is no meaning to possibility of existence except existence. If you say: It can be known as existing, but not known as possible, so it is other, we say: Similarly, the necessary of existence can be known as existence, but not known as its necessity except after another proof, so let it be other. In summary, existence is a general thing divided into necessary and possible. If the differentia of one of the two divisions is additional to the general, then likewise the second differentia; there is no difference. If it is said: Possibility of existence belongs to it from its essence, and its existence from another, so how can its essence and what it has from another be one? We say: How can necessity of existence be identical to existence? And can necessity of existence be negated while existence is affirmed? The truly one in every respect is that which does not admit negation and affirmation at all, for it cannot be said: It exists and does not exist, or necessary of existence and not necessary of existence. But it can be said: existing and not necessary of existence, just as it can be said: existing and not possible of existence. Unity is only known by this, so it is not correct to suppose that for the First, if what they mentioned is correct that possibility of existence is other than possible existence.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an argument presented by Ghazali in the context of the philosophers' claim that the Necessary Existent (God) cannot have multiplicity. Ghazali here argues that necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) is additional to existence (wujud) itself, which introduces multiplicity. He begins by stating that multiplicity arises because the entity exists and yet is necessary, so necessity is not identical to existence. Then he anticipates an objection: that necessity is nothing but existence. He refutes this by analogy with possibility, arguing that if possibility is different from existence, then necessity must also be different. The objection shifts: possibility belongs to the essence, existence comes from another, so how can they be one? Ghazali responds that if necessity were identical to existence, then one could not negate necessity while affirming existence, but clearly one can say something exists but is not necessary. Therefore, the truly one does not admit such separation. Thus, the First has multiplicity.

Correction: Ghazali shows that necessity is a distinct attribute (differentia) from existence, as evidenced by the logical possibility of negating necessity while affirming existence.

Second Objection: God's Intellect and Multiplicity
Ghazali challenges whether God's intellect is identical to His essence, leading to multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second objection against the philosophers' claim that God's intellect is identical to His essence, and how this leads to multiplicity in either the First or the effect.

Core Idea
If God's intellect equals His essence, then He must be multiple or the universe must be one.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a sharp dilemma: either God's intellect is identical to His essence, or it is other. Each option seems to force multiplicity onto the First Principle. He then turns the philosophers' own reasoning against them by applying it to the first intellect (the effect). This lesson walks through the logic of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage opens with a binary question: is God's intellect identical to His essence or other? Note the two possible answers and their immediate consequences.
Ghazali uses this to set up a logical trap.
3
Trace Ghazali's counter
Ghazali anticipates the philosophers' claim that self-intellection necessarily includes knowing oneself as a principle. He then applies the same logic to the effect (the first intellect). Follow how he parallels the arguments.
The key move is to show that if the philosophers' reasoning holds, the effect should also have no multiplicity.
4
Understand the implication
If the effect has multiplicity (as the philosophers claim), then by parity of reasoning, the First must also have multiplicity. Conversely, if the First has no multiplicity, the effect cannot have it either. This undermines the philosophers' system.
Ghazali concludes that the claim of absolute oneness is abandoned.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: what does this objection reveal about the coherence of the philosophers' emanation scheme? How does it challenge their view of God as a simple intellect?
This connects to broader issues of divine simplicity and multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثاني: هو أن نقول عقله مبدؤه، عين وجوده، وعين عقله نفسه، أم غيره؟ فإن كان عينه فلا كثرة في ذاته، إلا في العبارة عن ذاته، وإن كان غيره فهذه الكثرة موجودة في الأول، فإنه يعقل ذاته، ويعقل غيره، فإن زعموا أن عقله ذاته عين ذاته، ولا يعقل ذاته، ما لم يعقل أنه مبدأ لغيره فإن العقل يطابق المعقول، فيكون راجعاً إلى ذاته؛ فنقول: والمعلول، عقله ذاته عين ذاته، فإنه عقل بجوهره، فيعقل نفسه، والعقل والعاقل والمعقول منه أيضاً واحد؛ ثم إذا كان عقله ذاته عين ذاته، فليعقل ذاته معلولاً لعلة، فإنه كذلك، والعقل يطابق المعقول، فيرجع الكل إلى ذاته، فلا كثرة إذن، وإن كانت هذه كثرة فهي موجودة في الأول، فلتصدر منه المختلفات. ولنترك دعوى وحدانيته من كل وجه.
The second objection: we say: His intellect is His principle, identical to His existence, and identical to His intellect of Himself, or other than it? If it is identical, then there is no multiplicity in His essence except in expression about His essence. If it is other, then this multiplicity exists in the First, for He intellects His essence and intellects another. If they claim that His intellect of His essence is identical to His essence, and He does not intellect His essence unless He intellects that He is a principle for another, for the intellect corresponds to the intelligible, so it returns to His essence; we say: And the effect, its intellection of its essence is identical to its essence, for it intellects by its substance, so it intellects itself, and the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible from it are also one. Then if its intellection of its essence is identical to its essence, let it intellect its essence as an effect of a cause, for it is so, and the intellect corresponds to the intelligible, so everything returns to its essence, so there is no multiplicity then. If this is multiplicity, then it exists in the First, so let the different things emanate from Him. And let us abandon the claim of His oneness from every aspect.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This is a second objection presented by Ghazali. It examines whether God's intellect ('aql) is identical to His essence or other. If identical, there is no multiplicity except in expression. If other, then multiplicity arises because He intellects both self and others. The philosophers might claim that His intellection of self is identical to essence and necessarily includes intellection of being a principle for others, but Ghazali turns this against them by applying the same logic to the effect (the first intellect). If the effect's intellection of self is identical to its essence, then it should intellect itself as an effect, and all returns to its essence, thus no multiplicity. But the philosophers claim the effect has multiplicity (it intellects itself, its cause, its effects). So either the effect also has no multiplicity (contradiction) or the multiplicity in the effect implies multiplicity in the First. Thus, the claim of absolute oneness is abandoned.

Key Terms
عقل (intellect)
عاقل (intelligent)
معقول (intelligible)
مبدأ (principle)
معلول (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's intellection of Himself cannot be a source of multiplicity.
Correction: Ghazali shows that if self-intellection includes intellection of being a principle (which involves other), then multiplicity is already present in the divine self-intellection.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali conclude if the philosophers admit multiplicity in the effect but deny it in the First?
A) The First must be multiple
B) The effect is not multiple
C) The First is not a cause
D) The philosophers are consistent

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second objection forces the philosophers to admit either that the First has multiplicity or that the effect has no multiplicity, undermining their claim of absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as similar to your own—limited, sequential, and changing? How does Ghazali's objection expose the danger of this anthropomorphism? What concrete step will you take this week to remind yourself that Allah's knowledge is entirely unlike creation?
?
The philosophers claimed that God's oneness is compatible with His being the source of many things. In your own life, where do you claim to rely solely on Allah, but in practice depend on people, wealth, or plans? Where is your tawhid compromised? Will you identify one area and actively surrender it to Allah today?
?
Ghazali uses a logical argument to unmask inconsistency. When you encounter a religious truth you cannot fully grasp with your reason, do you humbly submit or insist on your own understanding? What is one attribute of Allah that puzzles you, and how will you train yourself to accept it as taught by revelation rather than philosophical reasoning?
