The First Knows All: Ibn Sina's Rejection of the Original View
Ghazali presents the objection and the first aspect of his answer, citing Ibn Sina's abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of Ghazali's refutation: that even the philosophers themselves abandoned the claim that God only knows Himself, and instead affirmed universal knowledge.

Core Idea
Philosophy's own evolution shows God's knowledge must be universal, not self-limited.

Ghazali begins his refutation by noting a key fact: the philosopher Ibn Sina himself abandoned the view that God only knows Himself, finding it too ugly. Instead, Ibn Sina and other verifiers argued that God knows all things universally. This shift within the philosophical school itself undermines the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Read the Arabic passage containing the objection and the first aspect of Ghazali's answer. Notice the claim that Ibn Sina and others abandoned the original view.
The objection states that God only intellects Himself, but Ghazali immediately counters with a historical fact.
2
Examine the alternative view
Identify the alternative view attributed to Ibn Sina: that God knows all existents universally, not particularly. Understand the distinction between universal and particular knowledge.
Universal knowledge means knowing the essences and principles, not contingent particulars.
3
Understand why the original view was deemed ugly
Read the explanation of why the original view was considered ugly: it leads to an absurdity where the effect (first intellect) is nobler than the cause because it knows more things.
This sets up the reductio that will be detailed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأول لا يعقل إلا ذاته، وعقله ذاته هو عين ذاته، فالعقل والعاقل والمعقول واحد ولا يعقل غيره. فالجواب من وجهين: أحدهما: أن هذا المذهب لشناعته هجره ابن سينا وسائر المحققين، وزعموا أن الأول يعقل نفسه مبدأ لفيضان ما يفيض منه، ويعقل الموجودات كلها، بأنواعها عقلاً كلياً، لا جزئياً، إذ استقبحوا قول القائل: المبدأ الأول لا يصدر منه إلا عقل واحد، ثم لا يعقل ما يصدر منه، ومعلوله عقل، ويفيض منه: عقل، ونفس فلك، وجرم فلك، ويعقل نفسه ومعلولاته الثلاثة، وعلته ومبدؤه، فيكون المعلول أشرف من حيث إن العلة، ما فاض منها إلا واحد، وقد فاض من هذا ثلاثة أمور، والأول ما عقل إلا نفسه، وهذا عقل نفسه، ونفس المبدأ الأول، ونفس المعلولات؛ ومن قنع أن يكون قوله في الله تعالى راجعاً إلى هذه الرتبة، فقد جعله أحقر من كل موجود: يعقل نفسه، ويعقل غيره؛ فإن ما يعقل غيره ويعقل نفسه أشرف منزلة منه، إذا كان هو لا يعقل إلا نفسه. فقد انتهى بهم التعمق في التعظيم، إلى أن أبطلوا كل ما يفهم من العظمة، وقربوا حاله تعالى من حال الميت، الذي لا خبر له بما جرى في العالم، إلا أنه فارق الميت في شعوره بنفسه فقط، وهكذا يفعل الله سبحانه بالزائغين عن والناكِبين عن طريق الهدى، المنكرين لقوله تعالى: (ما أشهدتهم خلق السموات والأرض ولا خلق أنفسهم) الظانين بالله ظن السوء المعتقدين أن أمور الربوبية، تستولى على كنهها القوى البشرية، المغرورين بعقولهم، زاعمين أن فيها مندوحة عن تقليد الرسل - صلوات الله عليهم وسلامه - وأتباعهم - رضوان الله عليهم.
If it is said: The First only intellects His essence, and His intellection of His essence is identical to His essence, so the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible are one, and He does not intellect another. The answer is from two aspects: The first: that Ibn Sina and the rest of the verifiers have abandoned this doctrine due to its ugliness, and they claimed that the First intellects Himself as a principle for the emanation of what emanates from Him, and intellects all existents, in their types, universally, not particularly, for they deemed ugly the statement: the First Principle only emanates one intellect, then does not intellect what emanates from Him, while His effect is an intellect, and emanates from it: an intellect, a soul of a sphere, and a body of a sphere, and it intellects itself and its three effects, and its cause and principle, so the effect becomes nobler because from the cause only one emanated, while from this three things emanated, and the First only intellected Himself, while this intellects itself, the soul of the First Principle, and the souls of the effects. And whoever is content to reduce his statement about God to this rank has made Him lower than every existent: He intellects Himself and intellects another; for that which intellects another and intellects itself is nobler in rank than He, if He only intellects Himself. Their profundity in glorification has led them to nullify everything understood of greatness, and they brought the state of the Exalted One close to the state of a dead person who has no news of what occurs in the world, except that he differs from the dead in his perception of himself alone. And thus does God do to those who deviate from the path of guidance, who deny His saying: 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth nor the creation of themselves', who think evil of God, believing that human powers can master the realities of lordship, deluded by their intellects, claiming that they are independent of following the Messengers - blessings and peace of God upon them - and their followers - may God be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the objection that the First only knows Himself, and Ghazali's two-part refutation. The first part cites that Ibn Sina and other verifiers have abandoned this view, and they now claim that God knows all things universally. This shows that the original view is not even accepted by its proponents. The second part is a reductio ad absurdum: if the First only knows Himself, then the effect (the first intellect) is superior because it knows itself, its cause, and its effects. This makes the cause lower than the effect, which is absurd. Furthermore, such a God is like a dead person who only perceives himself, which contradicts true divine greatness. Ghazali concludes with a warning against those who rely solely on intellect and reject revelation, citing a Quranic verse.

Key Terms
عقل كلي (universal intellect)
عقل جزئي (particular intellect)
مبدأ (principle)
معلول (effect)
فيضان (emanation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing only oneself is a sign of divine perfection.
Correction: Ghazali shows that even philosophers find this view deficient because it makes God less noble than His creation.

Checkpoint
Why did Ibn Sina abandon the view that the First only knows Himself?
A) Because it contradicts revelation
B) Because it is ugly and leads to absurdity
C) Because he wanted to follow Aristotle
D) Because he could not prove it

Key Takeaway
The first aspect of Ghazali's refutation shows that even leading philosophers rejected the view that God only knows Himself, affirming universal knowledge instead.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's knowledge is universal and detailed, or do you act as if some parts of your life are hidden from Him? What area of your life do you need to bring into the light today?
?
Ibn Sina abandoned a deficient view of God because it was 'ugly' (شناعة). Have you ever rejected a comfortable but inadequate belief about God because it dishonored Him? What belief are you still holding onto that diminishes His glory?
?
The passage shows that an effect cannot be nobler than its cause. Do you sometimes attribute more power, wisdom, or control to creation—people, systems, or your own abilities—than to the Creator? How can you consciously realign your trust and awe toward Him alone?

The Absurdity of a God Who Only Knows Himself
Ghazali presents the second aspect of his answer, a reductio showing the absurd consequence of the original view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument: if God only knows Himself, then He is lower in nobility than His creation, and this reduces Him to a dead state — a conclusion that refutes the original claim.

Core Idea
A God who only knows Himself is like a dead person, which is absurd for the Creator.

Ghazali now drives his refutation home with a reductio ad absurdum. If the First only knows Himself, then the first intellect — which knows itself, its cause, and its effects — is nobler than God. This makes God lower than His creation, close to a dead person who lacks awareness of the world. Such a conclusion, Ghazali argues, is the inevitable result of relying solely on human reason and rejecting prophetic guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio argument
Read from 'And whoever is content...' to the end. Follow the logic: if God only knows Himself, then the first intellect is superior because it knows more.
This is a classic 'reductio ad absurdum' — assuming the opponent's position leads to a contradiction.
2
Consider the analogy with a dead person
Notice how Ghazali compares such a God to a dead person who only perceives himself. This vividly shows the deficiency of the original view.
The analogy is meant to shock the reader into recognizing the absurdity.
3
Reflect on the concluding warning
Read the final sentences where Ghazali warns against those who rely solely on intellect and reject revelation. He cites a Quranic verse and condemns such arrogance.
This connects the theological refutation to a broader critique of philosophical rationalism.

Source Text

The Refutation: First Answer – Historical and Theological Critique
Ghazali presents the objection and his first answer, combining historical authority and a comparison with the first effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that limiting God's knowledge to Himself is unacceptable, citing the authority of Ibn Sina and showing that it would make God less perfect than His creation.

Core Idea
If God only knows Himself, He is less perfect than His own effect, which knows itself, its cause, and its effects.

Ghazali begins by presenting the objection that the First cannot know anything other than Himself. In his first answer, he points out that even the leading philosopher Ibn Sina and other verifiers rejected this view for its offensiveness. He then argues that the effect (the first intellect) knows itself, its cause, and its effects, while the First would know only Himself—making the effect more noble. This leads to a rhetorical critique that such a view degrades God to a state lower than a dead person.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Pay attention to the flow from the objection to Ghazali's response.
The objection is followed by 'فالجواب من وجهين' indicating two answers.
2
Identify the two sub-points of the first answer
Notice that the first answer includes: (a) Ibn Sina and others rejected it, and (b) a comparative argument showing the effect knows more than the First.
Ghazali uses the authority of Ibn Sina to strengthen his case.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical critique
Ghazali compares the view to making God like a dead person. Consider why this is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الأول لا يعقل إلا ذاته ، وعقله ذاته هو عين ذاته ، فالعقل والعاقل والمعقول واحد ولا يعقل غيره
فالجواب من وجهين :
أحدهما : أن هذا المذهب لشناعته هجره ابن سينا وسائر المحققين ، وزعموا أن الأول يعقل نفسه مبدأ لفيضان ما يفيض منه ، ويعقل الموجودات كلها بأنواعها عقلا كليا لا جزئيا ، إذ استقبحوا قول القائل : المبدأ الأول لا يصدر منه إلا عقل واحد ، ثم لا يعقل ما يصدر منه ، ومعلوله عقل ، ويفيض منه : عقل ، ونفس فلك ، وجرم فلك ، ويعقل نفسه ومعلولاته الثلاثة ، وعلته ومبدؤه ، فيكون المعلول أشرف من حيث إن العلة ما فاض منها إلا واحد ، وقد فاض من هذا ثلاثة أمور ، والأول ما عقل إلا نفسه ، وهذا عقل نفسه ، ونفس المبدأ الأول ، ونفس المعلولات ؛ ومن قنع أن يكون قوله في الله تعالى راجعا إلى هذه الرتبة ، فقد جعله أحقر من كل موجود : يعقل نفسه ويعقل غيره ، فإن ما يعقل غيره ويعقل نفسه أشرف منزلة منه ، إذا كان هو لا يعقل إلا نفسه . فقد انتهى بهم التعمق في التعظيم إلى أن أبطلوا كل ما يفهم من العظمة ، وقربوا حاله تعالى من حال الميت الذي لا خبر له بما جرى في العالم ، إلا أنه فارق الميت في شعوره بنفسه فقط ، وهكذا يفعل الله سبحانه بالزائغين عن سبيله والناكبين عن طريق الهدى ، المنكرين لقوله تعالى : ( ما أشهدت السموات والأرض ولا خلق أنفسهم ) الظانين بالله ظن السوء ، المعتقدين أن أمور الربوبية تستولي على كنهها القوى البشرية ، المغرورين بعقولهم ، زاعمين أن فيها مندوحة عن تقليد الرسل صلوات الله عليهم وسلامه وأتباعهم رضوان الله عليهم فلا جرم اضطروا إلى الاعتراف بأن لباب معقولاتهم رجع إلى ما لو حكي في منام لتعجب منه .
الجواب الثاني : هو أن من ذهب إلى أن الأول لا يعقل إلا نفسه ، إنما حاذر من لزوم الكثرة ، إذ لو قال به للزم أن يقال : عقله غيره غير عقله نفسه ! وهذا لازم في المعلول الأول ، فينبغي ألا يعقل إلا نفسه ، لأنه لو عقل الأول أو غيره ، لكان التعقل غير ذاته ، ولافتقر إلى علة غير علة ذاته ، ولا علة إلا علة ذاته وهو المبدأ الأول ! فينبغي ألا يعلم إلا ذاته ، وتبطل الكثرة التي نشأت من هذا الوجه .
If it is said: The First does not intellect anything except His Essence, and His intellection of His Essence is identical to His Essence, so the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible are one, and He does not intellect other than Himself.
Then the answer is from two aspects:
The first: that this doctrine, due to its offensiveness, was abandoned by Ibn Sina and the rest of the verifiers, and they claimed that the First intellects Himself as a principle for the emanation of what emanates from Him, and He intellects all existents, according to their kinds, with a universal intellect, not a particular one, for they deemed abhorrent the statement of the one who says: The First Principle does not bring forth except a single intellect, then does not intellect what comes from Him, while His effect is an intellect, and there emanates from it: an intellect, a soul of the sphere, and a body of the sphere, and it intellects itself, its three effects, its cause and its principle. Thus the effect is more noble than the cause, since from the cause there emanated only one thing, while from this effect there emanated three things, and the First did not intellect except Himself, whereas this effect intellects itself, the essence of the First Principle, and the essences of the effects. And whoever is content that his statement concerning God the Exalted reverts to this rank has made Him lower than every existent that intellects itself and intellects others: for that which intellects others and intellects itself is of a higher station than that which does not intellect except itself. Indeed, their profundity in extolling has reached the point that they have nullified everything understood of majesty, and have brought the state of the Exalted near to the state of a dead man who has no news of what occurs in the world, except that He differs from the dead in His awareness of Himself only. Thus does God Almighty do to those who deviate from His path and stray from the way of guidance, those who deny His saying: 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth nor the creation of themselves' (18:51), those who think evil of God, who believe that the affairs of Lordship are fully grasped by human powers, who are deluded by their intellects, claiming that they have independence from following the Messengers—God's blessings and peace be upon them—and their followers—God's pleasure be upon them. Hence they were compelled to admit that the pith of their intelligibles returned to something that, if narrated in a dream, would cause astonishment.
The second answer: He who holds that the First does not intellect except Himself is only cautious about the occurrence of multiplicity, for if he said otherwise, the result would be that His intellection of other than Himself is other than His intellection of Himself! This also applies to the first effect, so it should not intellect except itself, because if it intellects the First or other than it, then intellection would be other than its essence, and it would need a cause other than the cause of its essence, but there is no cause except the cause of its essence, which is the First Principle! So it should not know except itself, and the multiplicity that arose from this aspect is invalidated.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector claims that the First cannot know anything other than His own essence because His knowledge is identical to His essence, making intellect, intelligent, and intelligible one. Ghazali replies with two answers. 

The first answer is historical and philosophical: Ibn Sina and other serious philosophers rejected this view because it diminishes the First's perfection. They held that the First knows Himself as the principle of emanation and also knows all existents universally (not partially). To claim otherwise would make the First inferior to His own effect, which knows itself, its cause, and its effects. Ghazali then launches a stinging rhetorical critique: those who limit God's knowledge to Himself alone have reduced Him to a state worse than a dead person—since even a dead person has no news, at least the dead does not know himself? Actually, Ghazali says the only difference is awareness of self, which is minimal. He then warns that such deviation leads to denial of revelation and reliance on defective intellects. 

The second answer is a logical argument: if the First knows only Himself to avoid multiplicity, then by the same reasoning the first effect (the first intellect) must also know only itself, because if it knew the First or anything else, that knowledge would be additional to its essence, requiring an external cause. But its only cause is the First, which is one and cannot produce multiple effects in this way. So the effect cannot know its cause, which is absurd. Thus the original premise (the First knows only Himself) leads to the collapse of the entire emanationist system. Hence the multiplicity supposedly avoided by the First's self-knowledge reappears in the effect, showing the argument to be incoherent.

Key Terms

العقل
العاقل
المعقول
المعلول الأول
المبدأ الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God's knowledge is identical to His essence, He cannot know anything other than Himself.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this conclusion is not necessary and leads to absurdities. Even Ibn Sina held that God knows all things universally.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what did Ibn Sina and the verifiers claim about the First's knowledge?
A) The First knows only Himself.
B) The First knows Himself as a principle and all existents universally.
C) The First knows nothing.
D) The First knows only His effects.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first answer shows that limiting God's knowledge to Himself is not only rejected by the best philosophers but also leads to the absurd conclusion that a created effect is more perfect than its Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' view reduces God to a state like a dead man, aware only of Himself. Have you ever treated God as distant or unaware of your daily struggles? How does this lesson confront that attitude? Will you now speak to God as One who knows every detail of your life?
?
Ghazali quotes the verse 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth' (18:51). In what areas of your faith do you rely on your own reasoning rather than submitting to revelation? Is there a belief you hold that you have never tested against the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
If you truly believed that God knows every thought and action, what would change about your private moments? This lesson exposes the absurdity of limiting God's knowledge. Are you willing to live as if God sees everything, even when no one else does?

The Refutation: Second Answer – The Logical Incoherence
Ghazali presents a logical reductio against the argument from multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that the claim 'the First knows only Himself' forces a similar limitation on the first effect, leading to an absurd denial of knowledge of the cause, thus collapsing the entire emanative system.

Core Idea
If the First knows only Himself to avoid multiplicity, then His effect must also know only itself, which is absurd.

In his second answer, Ghazali takes a logical approach. He argues that if the reason the First knows only Himself is to avoid multiplicity in His essence, then the same reasoning must apply to the first effect. If the effect knows the First, that knowledge would be additional to its essence, requiring an additional cause. But the only cause of the effect is the First, which is one and does not cause multiple things in that sense. Therefore, the effect cannot know its own cause, which contradicts the emanationist system.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the Arabic and translation of the second answer. Notice it is a concise logical argument.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Follow the logical steps
Trace the reasoning: (1) The objector avoids multiplicity. (2) But then the effect must also avoid multiplicity. (3) If the effect knows the First, that knowledge is additional. (4) It would need another cause. (5) None exists except the First. (6) So the effect cannot know the First. (7) This is absurd.
Ghazali uses the objector's own principle against him.
3
Understand the conclusion
The multiplicity that the objector sought to avoid actually reappears in the effect, showing the argument is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الأول لا يعقل إلا ذاته ، وعقله ذاته هو عين ذاته ، فالعقل والعاقل والمعقول واحد ولا يعقل غيره
فالجواب من وجهين :
أحدهما : أن هذا المذهب لشناعته هجره ابن سينا وسائر المحققين ، وزعموا أن الأول يعقل نفسه مبدأ لفيضان ما يفيض منه ، ويعقل الموجودات كلها بأنواعها عقلا كليا لا جزئيا ، إذ استقبحوا قول القائل : المبدأ الأول لا يصدر منه إلا عقل واحد ، ثم لا يعقل ما يصدر منه ، ومعلوله عقل ، ويفيض منه : عقل ، ونفس فلك ، وجرم فلك ، ويعقل نفسه ومعلولاته الثلاثة ، وعلته ومبدؤه ، فيكون المعلول أشرف من حيث إن العلة ما فاض منها إلا واحد ، وقد فاض من هذا ثلاثة أمور ، والأول ما عقل إلا نفسه ، وهذا عقل نفسه ، ونفس المبدأ الأول ، ونفس المعلولات ؛ ومن قنع أن يكون قوله في الله تعالى راجعا إلى هذه الرتبة ، فقد جعله أحقر من كل موجود : يعقل نفسه ويعقل غيره ، فإن ما يعقل غيره ويعقل نفسه أشرف منزلة منه ، إذا كان هو لا يعقل إلا نفسه . فقد انتهى بهم التعمق في التعظيم إلى أن أبطلوا كل ما يفهم من العظمة ، وقربوا حاله تعالى من حال الميت الذي لا خبر له بما جرى في العالم ، إلا أنه فارق الميت في شعوره بنفسه فقط ، وهكذا يفعل الله سبحانه بالزائغين عن سبيله والناكبين عن طريق الهدى ، المنكرين لقوله تعالى : ( ما أشهدت السموات والأرض ولا خلق أنفسهم ) الظانين بالله ظن السوء ، المعتقدين أن أمور الربوبية تستولي على كنهها القوى البشرية ، المغرورين بعقولهم ، زاعمين أن فيها مندوحة عن تقليد الرسل صلوات الله عليهم وسلامه وأتباعهم رضوان الله عليهم فلا جرم اضطروا إلى الاعتراف بأن لباب معقولاتهم رجع إلى ما لو حكي في منام لتعجب منه .

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that restricting God's knowledge avoids multiplicity in a consistent way.

According to the second answer, what would be required if the first effect knew the First?
A) The effect would become identical to the First.
B) The effect would need a cause for that knowledge other than the cause of its essence.
C) The effect would have no cause.
D) The First would be multiplied.

Ghazali's second answer demonstrates that the attempt to avoid multiplicity by limiting the First's knowledge leads to an absurd consequence in the first effect, revealing the logical flaw in the original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice a flaw in someone else's reasoning, are you equally rigorous in examining your own? This lesson shows how a seemingly clever move to avoid one problem creates a bigger one elsewhere. Where in your life do you accept convenient truths that, if followed consistently, would lead to absurdity? Will you commit to tracing out the full implications of your beliefs, no matter how uncomfortable?
?
The objector wanted to protect the First's simplicity, but Ghazali showed that this protection came at the cost of making the first effect unable to know its cause. Are you protecting something in your life—an image, a comfort, a position—by ignoring the consequences it imposes on others or on the bigger picture? What would it cost you to let go of that protection and embrace the full truth?
?
This argument forces you to see that principles applied selectively collapse under their own weight. Is your faith rooted in a coherent system of truth, or have you allowed pockets of inconsistency to avoid difficult implications? Today, be honest: which belief are you holding onto that, if fully consistent, would require you to change? Ask God for the courage to follow logic and revelation wherever they lead, even if it means dismantling your familiar frameworks.

The First Intellect Knows Only Itself: Ghazali's Second Answer
Ghazali presents the second answer arguing the first intellect cannot know the First, with an objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the first effect cannot know anything beyond itself without implying multiplicity, and how he defends this against the objection that knowledge of the cause is necessary.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the cause is not necessary for the effect's existence.

This passage continues Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' emanation scheme. Having already raised one objection, he now presents a second, more intricate argument focusing on the first intellect (the first effect). He argues that if the first intellect were to know the First, it would require a separate act of intellection, introducing multiplicity and violating the principle that from the One only one proceeds. He then anticipates and refutes a likely objection, showing that knowledge of the cause is not an inherent property of the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the full argument settle before analyzing its parts.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
Notice that the passage begins with a premise (the First only knows itself to avoid multiplicity), then applies it to the first effect, then raises and refutes an objection, and finally argues that knowledge of the cause is not necessary.
This is a dialectical structure: thesis, objection, response, further reasoning.
3
Understand the objection and its refutation
The objector says: since the first effect exists and knows itself, it must know its cause. Ghazali's refutation asks whether this necessity arises from a cause or not, leading to a dilemma.
Focus on the dilemma: if from cause, only one effect can proceed; if without cause, multiplicity becomes uncaused.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that knowledge of the cause is not a necessary concomitant of the effect's essence. This blocks the philosophers' claim that the first intellect knows the First.
Consider why this matters for the overall debate on divine attributes and multiplicity.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you have grasped Ghazali's main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب الثانى : هو أن من ذهب إلى أن الأول لا يعقل إلا نفسه ، إنما حاذر من لزم الكثرة ، إذ لو قال به للزم أن يقال : عقله غيره ، غير عقله نفسه ! وهذا لازم فى المعلول الأول ، فينبغى ألا يعقل إلا نفسه ، لأنه لو عقل الأول أو غيره ، لكان التعقل غير ذاته ، ولافتقر إلى علة غير علة ذاته ، ولا علة إلا علة ذاته وهو المبدأ الأول ! فينبغى ألا يعلم إلا ذاته ، وتبطل الكثرة التي نشأت من هذا الوجه . فإن قيل : لما وجد ، وعقل ذاته ! لزمه أن يعقل المبدأ الأول . قلنا : لزمه ذلك بعلة أو بغير علة ؟ ! فإن كان بعلة ، فلا علة إلا المبدأ الأول ، وهو واحد فلا يتصور أن يصدر منه إلا واحد ، وقد صدر ، وهو ذات المعلول ، فالثاني كيف يصدر منه ؟ ! ! وإن لزم بغير علة ، فليلزم من وجود الأول موجودات كثيرة بلا علة ، ولا يلزم منها الكثرة ! فإن لم يعقل هذا من حيث إن واجب الوجود لا يكون إلا واحداً ، والزائد على الواحد ممكن والممكن يفتقر إلى علة . فهذا اللازم فى حق المعلول ، وإن كان واجب الوجود بذاته ، فقد بطل قولهم : واجب الوجود واحد ، وإن كان ممكناً فلابد له من علّة ، ولا علة له ، فلا يعقل وجوده . وليس هو من ضرورة المعلول الأول لكونه ممكن الوجود ، فإن إمكان الوجود ضرورى في كل معلول ، أما كون المعلول عالماً بالعلة ، ليس ضرورياً في وجود ذاته ، فكما أن كون العلة عالمة بالمعلول ، ليس ضروريا في وجود ذاته ، بل لزوم العلم بالمعلول ، أظهر من لزوم العلم بالعلة فبان أن الكثرة الحاصلة من علمه بالمبدأ محال ، فإنه لا مبدأ له وليس هو من ضرورة وجود ذات المعلول ، وهذا أيضاً لا مخرج منه .
The second answer: He who holds that the First does not intellect anything but itself only fears the necessity of multiplicity. For if he said that [the First intellects other things], then it would be said: His intellect of others is other than His intellect of Himself! And this is necessary regarding the first effect (al-malul al-awwal). Thus it should not intellect anything but itself, because if it intellected the First or anything else, then intellection would be other than its essence, and it would need a cause other than the cause of its essence, and there is no cause but the cause of its essence, which is the First Principle! Therefore it should not know anything but its essence, and the multiplicity that arises from this consideration is invalid. Then it is said: Since it exists and intellection its essence, it must intellect the First Principle. We say: Does this necessity arise from a cause or without a cause? If from a cause, then there is no cause but the First Principle, which is one, and only one can proceed from it, and it has already proceeded, which is the essence of the effect, so how can a second proceed from it?! And if it is necessary without a cause, then let many existences proceed from the First without cause, and multiplicity would not be necessary! But if this is not understood, then since the Necessary Existent is only one, and what is additional to the one is possible, and the possible needs a cause. So this necessary consequence regarding the effect: if it is necessary by itself, then their statement 'the Necessary Existent is one' is false; and if it is possible, then it must have a cause, but it has no cause, so its existence is not comprehensible. And it is not of the necessities of the first effect because it is possible existence – for possibility of existence is necessary in every effect – but the fact that the effect is knowledgeable of the cause is not necessary for the existence of its essence. Just as the fact that the cause is knowledgeable of the effect is not necessary for the existence of its essence, indeed the necessity of knowledge of the effect is more apparent than the necessity of knowledge of the cause. Thus it is clear that the multiplicity resulting from its knowledge of the Principle is impossible, for it has no principle, and it is not of the necessities of the existence of the effect's essence. And this also has no escape.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument: Ghazali presents his second answer to a previous objection (likely from the philosophers) and then handles a counter-objection. The argument proceeds as follows: First, he explains the motive of those who claim the First intellection only itself—they want to avoid multiplicity. If the First intellection other things, that intellection would be distinct from His essence, implying multiplicity. Then Ghazali applies this reasoning to the first effect (the first intellect in Neoplatonic emanation): it too should not intellect anything but itself, for if it intellected the First or anything else, its intellection would be other than its essence and would need a cause beyond its own cause (the First). This would entail multiplicity in the causal chain. Next, an objector says: But if the first effect exists and intellection its essence, it necessarily intellection the First (its cause). Ghazali's response: This necessity either arises from a cause or not. If from a cause, the only cause is the First, which is one and produces only one effect (the first effect itself), so a second effect (the intellection of the First) cannot proceed. If without a cause, then multiple things could proceed from the First without cause, which is absurd. Ghazali then considers the status of the first effect: if it were necessary in itself, then the philosophers' principle that the Necessary Existent is only one would be false; if it is possible, it needs a cause, but it has none, so its existence is unintelligible. Finally, he argues that knowledge of the cause is not a necessary concomitant of the effect's existence; it is more plausible for the cause to know the effect than vice versa. Therefore the supposed multiplicity from the first effect's knowing the First is impossible.

Key Terms
المعلول الأول
الكثرة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali is denying that the first effect has any knowledge at all, but he is only denying knowledge of anything beyond itself to avoid multiplicity.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the first effect can know itself, but not its cause (the First), because that would require a second act of intellection implying multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's second answer, why does the first effect (first intellect) not know the First?
A) Because knowledge is impossible for an effect.
B) Because knowing the First would require a cause beyond the First, introducing multiplicity.
C) Because the First does not want to be known.
D) Because the first effect is ignorant by nature.

Key Takeaway
The first effect's knowledge of the First would require a separate intellection, introducing multiplicity and violating the principle that from the One only one proceeds, so such knowledge is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Ghazali argues that knowledge of the cause is not necessary for the effect's existence. In your own life, are there things you assume you must understand (like why something happened) before you can accept them? Is that assumption rooted in humility or in a subtle demand that reality conform to your intellect?
?
2. The philosophers feared that if the First knew anything other than itself, multiplicity would enter the Divine. Are you afraid of intellectual complexity in your faith? Do you prefer a simple, neat theology even if it means ignoring difficult truths? What would it cost you to sit with the tension?
?
3. Ghazali insists that the first effect's knowledge of the First is not necessary for its existence. Are there people or relationships in your life where you demand that they 'know' you or understand you in a certain way for their existence (in your eyes) to be valid? Can you release that demand, trusting that your worth does not depend on being known by others?

The Third Objection: Self-Knowledge of the First Intellect
Ghazali objects to the first intellect's self-knowledge being identical or distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's dilemma regarding the self-knowledge of the first intellect and why it leads to an absurd multiplicity.

Core Idea
Ghazali shows that the first intellect's self-knowledge leads to a quadruple or quintuple, not a trinity.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' emanation scheme by targeting the first intellect's self-knowledge. He poses a dilemma: if it's identical to its essence, that violates the distinction between knowledge and known; if distinct, then multiplicity follows. This objection exposes the internal contradictions in the philosophers' own logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the opening question: Is the first intellect's self-knowledge identical to its essence or distinct? Both horns lead to problems.
Notice the stark choice Ghazali presents.
2
Examine the 'identical' horn
If self-knowledge is identical to essence, that is impossible because knowledge is distinct from the known object. Reflect on why this is problematic.
This relies on a basic principle: knowledge and known are different.
3
Examine the 'distinct' horn
If self-knowledge is distinct, then the same logic applies to the First Principle, implying multiplicity in God. Then trace the resulting quadruple: essence, self-knowledge, knowledge of the principle, and possibility in itself.
Ghazali adds that one could even include necessity through another, making a quintuple.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that this shows the philosophers' depth in raving (haws) — their system leads to absurd consequences.
This is a common method of refutation: showing that an opponent's position leads to absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث : هو أن عقل المعلول الأول ذات نفسه عين ذاته أو غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه فهو محال ، لأن العلم غير المعلوم ، وإن كان غيره فليكن كذلك فى المبدأ الأول، ويلزم منه كثرة، ويلزم منه تربيع لا تثليث بزعمهم ، وهو : ذاته ، وعقله نفسه ، وعقله مبدأه ، وأنه ممكن الوجود بذاته ! ويمكن أن يزاد أنه واجب الوجود بغيره ، فيظهر تخميس . وبهذا يعرف تعمق هؤلاء في الهوس .
The third objection: Is the intellection of the first effect of its own essence identical to its essence or other? If it is identical, that is impossible because knowledge is other than the known. If it is other, then let it be likewise regarding the First Principle, and multiplicity follows, and a quadruple follows, not a trinity as they claim: namely, its essence, its intellection of itself, its intellection of its principle, and that it is possible in itself. And one could add that it is necessary by another, so a quintuple appears. By this is known the depth of these people in raving.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a formal objection (i'tirad) against the philosophers' doctrine of the first intellect. Ghazali presents a dilemma: Is the first intellect's self-knowledge identical to its essence or distinct? If identical, that is impossible because knowledge (the act or attribute) is distinct from the known object (the essence). If distinct, then the same reasoning should apply to the First (God): His self-knowledge would be other than His essence, implying multiplicity in the Godhead. Furthermore, if the first intellect has self-knowledge and knowledge of its principle, and is possible in itself, then that gives four realities (essence, self-knowledge, knowledge of the First, possibility) — a 'quadruple' — rather than the trinity some philosophers may have proposed. One could even add that it is necessary through another, making five. Ghazali concludes that this shows how deep these philosophers are in meaningless speculation (haws).

Key Terms
عقل المعلول الأول
تربيع
تخميس
الهوس
self-knowledge
quadruple
quintuple

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is affirming a quadruple in the first intellect as a valid description.
Correction: Ghazali is not affirming it; he is showing the absurd consequence of the philosophers' position.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali claim follows from the first intellect's self-knowledge being distinct from its essence?
A) A trinity.
B) A quadruple: essence, self-knowledge, knowledge of the First, and possibility.
C) A single unified intellect.
D) Nothing, because it is identical.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection shows that the philosophers' scheme leads to unacceptable multiplicity, undermining their claim of a simple first principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously imagine a multiplicity in His essence, like distinct 'parts' or 'aspects' that could be separated? How does this lesson force you to reconsider that tendency?
?
Ghazali exposes how a seemingly neat philosophical system can lead to absurd consequences. In your own understanding of faith, are there philosophical or intellectual constructs you cling to that might actually contradict the core simplicity of tawhid? What would it cost you to abandon them?
?
The philosophers' 'depth in raving' is a warning against overcomplicating divine matters. Where in your own study or thinking do you risk being impressed by clever arguments that actually lead away from truth? Will you commit to testing every idea against the touchstone of revelation and divine unity?

The Opponent's Dilemma and Ghazali's Initial Refutation
The opponent's challenge and Ghazali's initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four options the opponent presents to Ghazali and how Ghazali refutes them by declaring two key claims baseless and unproven.

Core Idea
The opponent's dilemma assumes unproven impossibilities.

Ghazali sets up a dialectical exchange where the philosopher's advocate presents a dilemma: either accept absurd consequences or admit the philosophers' emanation theory. Ghazali's response is not to propose a full alternative but to show that the alleged impossibilities are not rationally necessary, thus disrupting the opponent's argumentative structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's four options
Identify the four binary choices the opponent gives, each meant to corner Ghazali into accepting the philosophers' view or facing a contradiction.
The options cover reason, monotheism, sense experience, and philosophical intermediaries.
2
Understand Ghazali's immediate response
Ghazali clarifies the aim of his book—not to build a full system but to disturb the philosophers' claims—and then directly addresses two of the options as false claims without proof.
3
Analyze the two dismissed claims
Notice that Ghazali singles out the claims that two effects from one cause impossible and that attributing eternal attributes to God violates monotheism, calling them baseless.
This sets the stage for his positive argument in the next part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فإذا أبطلتم مذهبهم فماذا تقولون أنتم ؟! أتزعمون أنه يصدر من الشيء الواحد من كل وجه ، شيئان مختلفان ، فتكابرون العقول ؟ ! أو تقولون : المبدأ الأول فيه كثرة ، فتتركون التوحيد ؟ ! أو تقولون : لا كثرة في العالم ، فتنكرون الحس ؟ ! أو تقولون : لزمت بالوسائط ، فتضطرون إلى الاعتراف بما قالوه ؟! قلنا : نحن لم نخض فى هذا الكتاب خوض ممهد ، وإنما غرضنا أن نشوش دعاويهم ، وقد حصل ، على أنا نقول : ومن زعم أن المصير إلى صدور اثنين من واحد ، مكابرة للمعقول ، أو اتصاف المبدأ بصفات قديمة أزلية ، مناقض للتوحيد ؟ ! فهاتان دعویان باطلتان، لا برهان لهم عليهما ، فإنه ليس يعرف استحالة صدور اثنين من واحد ، كما يعرف استحالة كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين ، وعلى الجملة لا يعرف بالضرورة ولا بالنظر ، وما المانع من أن يقال : المبدأ الأول عالم مريد قادر ، يفعل ما يشاء ، ويحكم ما يريد ، يخلق المختلفات ، والمتجانسات ، كما يريد وعلى ما يريد ؟ ! فاستحالة هذا لا تعرف بضرور العقل ولا نظره ، وقد وردت به الأنبياء المؤيدون بالمعجزات ، فيجب قبوله . وأما البحث عن كيفية صدور الفعل من الله تعالى بالإرادة ، ففضول وطمع في غير مطمع ، والذين طمعوا في طلب المناسبة ومعرفتها ، رجع حاصل نظرهم إلى أن المعلول الأول ، من حيث إنه ممكن الوجود ، صدر منه فلك ، ومن حيث إنه يعقل نفسه ، صدر منه نفس الفلك ، وهذه حماقة لا إظهار مناسبة فلنتقبل مبادئ هذه الأمور من الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - وليصدقوا فيها ؛ إذ العقل ليس يحيلها ، وليترك البحث عن الكيفية والكمية والماهية ، فليس ذلك مما تتسع له القوى البشرية ، ولذلك قال صاحب الشرع - صلوات الله عليه - : ( تفكروا فى خلق الله ، ولا تفكروا في ذات الله ).
So if a questioner says: 'If you have refuted their doctrine, then what do you yourselves say? Do you claim that from a single thing in every respect, two different things issue, thereby opposing reason? Or do you say that the First Principle has multiplicity, thereby abandoning monotheism? Or do you say there is no multiplicity in the world, thereby denying sense? Or do you say that it follows through intermediaries, thereby being forced to admit what they said?' We say: We have not engaged in this book in a thorough investigation; rather our aim is to disturb their claims, and that has been achieved. Moreover, we say: Who claims that concluding that two things issue from one is an opposition to reason, or that the First Principle is described with eternal preexistent attributes is contrary to monotheism? These are two false claims, for which they have no proof. For the impossibility of two things issuing from one is not known as the impossibility of one person being in two places is known. In short, it is not known necessarily nor by reflection. And what prevents one from saying: The First Principle is knowing, willing, powerful; He does what He wills and decrees what He desires; He creates different things and homogeneous things as He wills and in the manner He wills? The impossibility of this is not known by necessity of reason nor by its reflection, and the prophets supported by miracles have brought it, so acceptance is obligatory. As for investigating the modality of the action issuing from God by will, it is excess and coveting what is not to be coveted. Those who coveted seeking appropriateness and knowing it, the outcome of their reflection came down to this: that the first effect, inasmuch as it is possible of existence, a sphere issued from it, and inasmuch as it intellects itself, the soul of the sphere issued from it. This is foolishness, not demonstration of appropriateness. So let us accept the principles of these matters from the prophets—peace be upon them—and let them be believed therein, for reason does not deem them impossible. And let the investigation into the 'how', the quantity, and the quiddity be abandoned, for that is not something that human powers can encompass. Therefore the author of the Law—peace be upon him—said: 'Reflect on the creation of God, and do not reflect on the essence of God.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage contains a question-and-answer structure. Ghazali presents the opponent's dilemma: if the philosophers are wrong, what alternative do you offer? The opponent lists four options: (1) admitting that two effects can issue directly from a single simple cause, which they claim is rationally absurd; (2) positing multiplicity in the First Principle, which compromises tawhid; (3) denying multiplicity in the world, which contradicts sense experience; or (4) accepting the philosophers' emanation through intermediaries. Ghazali responds by first clarifying the purpose of the book: not to establish a full system but to disturb the philosophers' claims. He then asserts that the alleged impossibility of two effects issuing from one is not self-evident; it is not like the impossibility of being in two places at once. Reason does not judge it impossible. He affirms that God is knowing, willing, powerful, and creates whatever He wills, whether diverse or homogeneous. This is not rationally impossible. Moreover, the prophets have taught this, supported by miracles, so it must be accepted. He then criticizes the philosophers' futile search for 'appropriateness' (مناسبة) between cause and effect, which led them to absurd claims about the first intellect producing a sphere and its soul. Finally, he exhorts acceptance of prophetic principles and avoidance of investigating the modality (كيفية) of divine action, citing the hadith: 'Reflect on the creation of God, and do not reflect on the essence of God.'

Key Terms
مبدأ أول
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's four options are logically exhaustive and unavoidable.
Correction: Ghazali shows that two of the options rest on unproven assumptions, so the dilemma is not binding.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how does he describe the two claims of the opponent?
A) They are known by necessity.
B) They are false claims without proof.
C) They are accepted by the prophets.
D) They are rationally necessary.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the opponent's dilemma by showing that key claims are unproven, not rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult question about your faith, do you tend to accept the first two options presented to you, or do you question whether the options themselves are valid? In what area of your life right now might you be accepting a false dilemma?
?
Ghazali says that the opponent's claims are 'false claims, for which they have no proof.' Are there beliefs you hold about God or religion that you have accepted without proof? How would you respond if someone asked you for evidence?
?
The opponent's dilemma includes the option that 'the First Principle has multiplicity'—a direct challenge to tawheed. Have you ever felt that affirming God's attributes (like knowledge, power) somehow compromises His oneness? How does this passage help you see that the charge is unproven?

Affirmation of Divine Attributes and the Role of Prophethood
Ghazali's positive doctrine of divine will and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the impossibility of multiple effects from a single cause is not rationally known, and that affirming divine attributes and will is consistent with reason and confirmed by prophets.

Core Idea
Reason does not deem God's attributes and free will impossible.

After dismissing the opponent's initial claims, Ghazali builds a positive case: the alleged impossibility of two effects issuing from one cause is not self-evident like the impossibility of being in two places. He then affirms that God is knowing, willing, and powerful, creating as He wills, and that this is not rationally impossible—especially since prophets supported by miracles have taught it. This lesson focuses on that constructive argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy of impossibility
Ghazali compares the claimed impossibility to the well-known impossibility of one person being in two places, showing that the former is not similarly known.
This is a key epistemological move.
2
Read Ghazali's positive statement
Identify the attributes Ghazali ascribes to God: knowing, willing, powerful; and His actions: doing what He wills, creating diverse and homogeneous things as He wills.
Note the phrase 'ما المانع' - 'what prevents' - emphasizing its rational permissibility.
3
Connect to prophethood
Notice that Ghazali cites the prophets' miracles as a basis for accepting this doctrine, making acceptance obligatory.
This shows a harmonious relationship between reason and revelation.

The Futility of Investigating Modality and Prophetic Guidance
Ghazali's rejection of philosophical search for appropriateness and call to accept prophetic principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophers' search for causal appropriateness (مناسبة) as foolish and why he advises accepting prophetic principles without investigating the modality (كيفية) of divine action.

Core Idea
Reason cannot grasp the 'how' of divine action; prophets suffice.

Having established the rational permissibility of divine attributes, Ghazali now turns to the philosophers' attempt to explain the exact mechanism of emanation. He critically examines their claim to have found 'appropriateness' between cause and effect, using the example of the first intellect producing a sphere and its soul. He dismisses this as foolishness and then exhorts believers to accept the principles brought by prophets, abandoning any investigation into the modality, quantity, or quiddity of divine actions, citing a famous hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' search for appropriateness
Identify the specific example Ghazali gives: the first effect producing a sphere and a soul based on its possibility and self-intellection.
This is a reference to Neoplatonic emanation theory.
2
Understand Ghazali's judgment
Note that Ghazali calls this 'foolishness' and denies that it demonstrates any genuine appropriateness.
He is rejecting philosophical cosmology, not rational inquiry per se.
3
Absorb the final exhortation
Ghazali instructs to accept prophetic principles, believe them because reason does not reject them, and avoid investigating the modality or essence of God.
This is tied to the hadith 'Reflect on the creation, not the essence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فإذا أبطلتم مذهبهم فماذا تقولون أنتم ؟! أتزعمون أنه يصدر من الشيء الواحد من كل وجه ، شيئان مختلفان ، فتكابرون العقول ؟ ! أو تقولون : المبدأ الأول فيه كثرة ، فتتركون التوحيد ؟ ! أو تقولون : لا كثرة في العالم ، فتنكرون الحس ؟ ! أو تقولون : لزمت بالوسائط ، فتضطرون إلى الاعتراف بما قالوه ؟! قلنا : نحن لم نخض فى هذا الكتاب خوض ممهد ، وإنما غرضنا أن نشوش دعاويهم ، وقد حصل ، على أنا نقول : ومن زعم أن المصير إلى صدور اثنين من واحد ، مكابرة للمعقول ، أو اتصاف المبدأ بصفات قديمة أزلية ، مناقض للتوحيد ؟ ! فهاتان دعویان باطلتان، لا برهان لهم عليهما ، فإنه ليس يعرف استحالة صدور اثنين من واحد ، كما يعرف استحالة كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين ، وعلى الجملة لا يعرف بالضرورة ولا بالنظر ، وما المانع من أن يقال : المبدأ الأول عالم مريد قادر ، يفعل ما يشاء ، ويحكم ما يريد ، يخلق المختلفات ، والمتجانسات ، كما يريد وعلى ما يريد ؟ ! فاستحالة هذا لا تعرف بضرور العقل ولا نظره ، وقد وردت به الأنبياء المؤيدون بالمعجزات ، فيجب قبوله . وأما البحث عن كيفية صدور الفعل من الله تعالى بالإرادة ، ففضول وطمع في غير مطمع ، والذين طمعوا في طلب المناسبة ومعرفتها ، رجع حاصل نظرهم إلى أن المعلول الأول ، من حيث إنه ممكن الوجود ، صدر منه فلك ، ومن حيث إنه يعقل نفسه ، صدر منه نفس الفلك ، وهذه حماقة لا إظهار مناسبة فلنتقبل مبادئ هذه الأمور من الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - وليصدقوا فيها ؛ إذ العقل ليس يحيلها ، وليترك البحث عن الكيفية والكمية والماهية ، فليس ذلك مما تتسع له القوى البشرية ، ولذلك قال صاحب الشرع - صلوات الله عليه - : ( تفكروا فى خلق الله ، ولا تفكروا في ذات الله ).
So if a questioner says: 'If you have refuted their doctrine, then what do you yourselves say? Do you claim that from a single thing in every respect, two different things issue, thereby opposing reason? Or do you say that the First Principle has multiplicity, thereby abandoning monotheism? Or do you say there is no multiplicity in the world, thereby denying sense? Or do you say that it follows through intermediaries, thereby being forced to admit what they said?' We say: We have not engaged in this book in a thorough investigation; rather our aim is to disturb their claims, and that has been achieved. Moreover, we say: Who claims that concluding that two things issue from one is an opposition to reason, or that the First Principle is described with eternal preexistent attributes is contrary to monotheism? These are two false claims, for which they have no proof. For the impossibility of two things issuing from one is not known as the impossibility of one person being in two places is known. In short, it is not known necessarily nor by reflection. And what prevents one from saying: The First Principle is knowing, willing, powerful; He does what He wills and decrees what He desires; He creates different things and homogeneous things as He wills and in the manner He wills? The impossibility of this is not known by necessity of reason nor by its reflection, and the prophets supported by miracles have brought it, so acceptance is obligatory. As for investigating the modality of the action issuing from God by will, it is excess and coveting what is not to be coveted. Those who coveted seeking appropriateness and knowing it, the outcome of their reflection came down to this: that the first effect, inasmuch as it is possible of existence, a sphere issued from it, and inasmuch as it intellects itself, the soul of the sphere issued from it. This is foolishness, not demonstration of appropriateness. So let us accept the principles of these matters from the prophets—peace be upon them—and let them be believed therein, for reason does not deem them impossible. And let the investigation into the 'how', the quantity, and the quiddity be abandoned, for that is not something that human powers can encompass. Therefore the author of the Law—peace be upon him—said: 'Reflect on the creation of God, and do not reflect on the essence of God.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage contains a question-and-answer structure. Ghazali presents the opponent's dilemma: if the philosophers are wrong, what alternative do you offer? The opponent lists four options: (1) admitting that two effects can issue directly from a single simple cause, which they claim is rationally absurd; (2) positing multiplicity in the First Principle, which compromises tawhid; (3) denying multiplicity in the world, which contradicts sense experience; or (4) accepting the philosophers' emanation through intermediaries. Ghazali responds by first clarifying the purpose of the book: not to establish a full system but to disturb the philosophers' claims. He then asserts that the alleged impossibility of two effects issuing from one is not self-evident; it is not like the impossibility of being in two places at once. Reason does not judge it impossible. He affirms that God is knowing, willing, powerful, and creates whatever He wills, whether diverse or homogeneous. This is not rationally impossible. Moreover, the prophets have taught this, supported by miracles, so it must be accepted. He then criticizes the philosophers' futile search for 'appropriateness' (مناسبة) between cause and effect, which led them to absurd claims about the first intellect producing a sphere and its soul. Finally, he exhorts acceptance of prophetic principles and avoidance of investigating the modality (كيفية) of divine action, citing the hadith: 'Reflect on the creation of God, and do not reflect on the essence of God.'

Key Terms
كيفية
مناسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for human reason to fully understand the modality of divine action.
Correction: Ghazali says this is beyond human capacity and the prophets have taught us what we need.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about the philosophers' attempt to find appropriateness (مناسبة)?
A) It is a valid method.
B) It results in foolishness.
C) It is confirmed by revelation.
D) It is the basis of tawhid.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali rejects philosophical speculation into the modality of divine action and urges acceptance of prophetic guidance within the limits of reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological matter that your mind cannot fully grasp, do you humbly accept the prophetic guidance as sufficient, or do you persist in seeking a 'how' that may lead you to frustration? Will you now commit to stopping at the limits revelation sets?
?
Think of a time you insisted on understanding the logistics of a divine decree or attribute. Did that bring you closer to certainty or to confusion? Would you now choose to reflect on creation instead of the essence of God?
?
Have you ever felt that your faith required you to abandon reason? Or have you perhaps over-relied on reason at the expense of revelation? How can you balance both as Ghazali teaches: accept what reason does not deem impossible, but refrain from probing beyond your capacity?

Two More Multiplicities Negated from the First Principle
Ghazali enumerates two additional types of multiplicity negated from God by the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim philosophers deny real attributes and composition of genus and species from Allah to preserve His absolute oneness.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of attributes or composition would shatter divine simplicity.

Ghazali continues his exposition of the philosophers' arguments for tawhid by presenting two additional types of multiplicity that they negate from Allah. The first concerns distinct attributes like knowledge and power, which would imply multiple co-eternals. The second concerns any conceptual composition, such as genus and species, which would make the First Principle composite. This lesson explores these arguments and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Notice the clear enumeration of two types.
2
Understand the third type: attributes
The passage argues that if God had distinct attributes like knowledge, each would be necessary of existence, leading to multiple necessary existents and negating unity. Reflect on why this would compromise tawhid.
3
Understand the fourth type: genus-species composition
The passage gives examples like blackness being composed of color (genus) and blackness (species-difference), and man being composed of animal and rational. This shows that any conceptual composition introduces multiplicity. Why does this apply to the First Principle?
4
Grasp the overarching conclusion
The philosophers negate both types of multiplicity from the First Principle to maintain absolute simplicity. Consider how this aligns with the broader Islamic emphasis on tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الكثرة بالصفات ، بتقدير العلم والقدرة والإرادة ، فإن هذه الصفات إن كانت واجبة الوجود ، كان وجوب الوجود مشتركاً بين الذات ، وبين هذه الصفات ، ولزمت كثرة فى واجب الوجود ، وانتفت الوحدة . الرابع : كثرة عقلية تحصل بتركب الجنس والفصل ، فإن السواد سواد ولون ، والسوادية غير اللونية فى حق العقل ، بل اللونية جنس ، والسوادية فصل ، فهو مركب من جنس وفصل ، والحيوانية غير الإنسانية في العقل ، فإن الإنسان حيوان وناطق ، والحيوان جنس ، والناطق فصل ، وهو مركب من الجنس والفصل ، وهذا نوع كثرة . فزعموا : أن هذا أيضاً منفى عن المبدأ الأول.
Third: multiplicity by attributes, by positing knowledge, power, and will. For if these attributes were necessary of existence, then necessary existence would be shared between the essence and these attributes, and multiplicity in the Necessary Existent would be necessary, and unity would be negated. Fourth: intellectual multiplicity that occurs by composition of genus and species. For blackness is blackness and color, and blackness-aspect is other than color-aspect in the mind; rather color is a genus, and blackness is a species-difference, so it is composed of genus and species. And animality is other than humanity in the mind, for man is animal and rational; animal is genus, rational is species-difference, and he is composed of genus and species. This is a type of multiplicity. They claimed that this too is negated from the First Principle.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth ways multiplicity enters beings, as held by the philosophers. The third is multiplicity of attributes: if God had distinct attributes like knowledge, power, and will, each would be necessary of existence, leading to multiple necessary existents and destroying unity. The philosophers thus negate real distinct attributes from God. The fourth is intellectual multiplicity through composition of genus and species. For example, blackness is both a color (genus) and a blackness (species-difference); similarly, a human is animal (genus) and rational (species-difference). Such composition implies multiplicity in the essence. The philosophers assert that this is also negated from the First Principle, who must be simple without any such composition.

Key Terms
صفات (attributes)
وجوب الوجود (necessary existence)
جنس (genus)
فصل (species-difference)
كثرة عقلية (intellectual multiplicity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny all attributes from God entirely.
Correction: They only deny multiplicity, not necessarily all attributes; as Ghazali explains elsewhere, they affirm attributes through negation or relation, avoiding real multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why does the composition of genus and species imply multiplicity in the First Principle?
A) Because genus and species are both substances.
B) Because the combination of a more universal and a more specific concept introduces multiplicity into the essence.
C) Because blackness is a color, so any composition implies bodily extension.
D) Because the First Principle is not a species of any genus, but composition is only for contingent beings.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers negate both distinct attributes and conceptual composition from God to preserve absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you find yourself mentally dividing His essence into 'parts' like power, wisdom, and mercy? How does this passage challenge you to instead conceive of Him as absolutely simple, and what practical change in your worship might that require?
?
If God's attributes are not distinct from His essence, then your understanding of His names like 'Al-'Aleem (The All-Knowing) must be purified. Do you ever treat God's knowledge as something separate from His very being? What would it mean for your reliance on Him if you truly believed His knowledge is identical to His essence?
?
The philosophers negate even conceptual composition from God. Is there any way you unconsciously imagine God as having 'parts'—like a mixture of qualities—that you need to repent of? Commit today to reciting a verse or name of God while deliberately affirming His absolute oneness.

The Philosophers' Paradox: Many Names, One Reality
Ghazali presents the philosophers' list of names and their claim of one meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that all divine names refer to a single, undivided meaning, and the list of names they affirm.

Core Idea
Twenty names, one meaning — the philosophers affirm multiplicity of expression but deny multiplicity of essence.

Al-Ghazali has just enumerated five types of multiplicity that cannot apply to God. Now he presents the philosophers' surprising doctrine: they affirm a long list of divine names yet insist all refer to one simple reality. This seems contradictory, and Ghazali will eventually critique it, but first he faithfully presents their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the list of names
Read the Arabic passage carefully, paying attention to the long list of names Ghazali attributes to the philosophers. Then read the translation.
The list includes over twenty names such as 'beginning', 'first', 'existent', etc.
2
Identify the central claim
The philosophers claim that all these names are expressions of a single meaning with no multiplicity. Recognize this as a key point for later critique.
3
Consider why this is paradoxical
Reflect on why Ghazali calls this 'among the wonders' — if names have different meanings, how can they all refer to one thing without multiplicity?
This sets up the need for the philosophers' explanation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومع هذا فإنهم يقولون في البارى تعالى : إنه مبدأ ، وأول ، وموجود ، وجوهر ، وواحد ، وقديم، وباق ، وعالم ، وعقل ، وعاقل ، ومعقول ، وفاعل ، وخالق ، ومريد ، وقادر ، وحى ، وعاشق ، ومعشوق ، ولذيذ ، ومتلذذ ، وجواد ، وخير محض ، وزعموا : أن كل ذلك عبارة عن معنى واحد ، لا كثرة فيه ، وهذا من العجائب . فينبغي أن نحقق مذهبهم للتفهيم أولا ، ثم نشتغل بالاعتراض ، فإن الاعتراض على المذهب ، قبل تمام التفهيم ، رمى في عماية والعمدة في فهم مذهبهم ، أنهم يقولون : ذات المبدأ الأول واحدة ، وإنما تكثر الأسماء بإضافة شيء إليه ، أو إضافته إلى شيء ، أو سلب شيء عنه ، والسلب لا يوجب كثرة في الذات المسلوب عنه ، ولا الإضافة توجب كثرة
And with this, they say concerning the Creator, exalted: that He is a beginning, first, existent, substance, one, eternal, everlasting, knower, intellect, intelligent, intelligible, agent, creator, willer, powerful, living, lover, beloved, delicious, delighting, generous, pure good. And they claimed that all of that is an expression of a single meaning, with no multiplicity in it. And this is among the wonders. So it is necessary that we verify their doctrine for explanation first, then occupy ourselves with objection. For objecting to a doctrine before complete explanation is throwing into blindness. And the main point in understanding their doctrine is that they say: the essence of the First Principle is one, and the names only multiply by the addition of something to Him, or His addition to something, or the negation of something from Him. And negation does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence from which something is negated, nor does addition necessitate multiplicity.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage serves as a conclusion to the enumeration of the five types of multiplicity and introduces the philosophers' paradoxical claim. After negating all multiplicity from God, they nevertheless affirm many names and attributes for Him—over twenty are listed. They claim that all these names refer to a single meaning without any multiplicity. Ghazali finds this astonishing ('ajab) because it seems contradictory to deny multiple attributes while affirming multiple names. He then outlines his methodology: he will first clarify the philosophers' doctrine properly (to avoid misdirected criticism), then raise objections. The core of their doctrine, he explains, is that the essence of the First Principle is one, and the multiplicity of names arises from three sources: (1) adding something to Him (e.g., Creator adds a relation to creation), (2) adding Him to something (e.g., Knowledge relates to objects known), or (3) negating something from Him (e.g., Eternal negates beginning). Negation and addition do not introduce real multiplicity in the essence itself. This sets the stage for al-Ghazali's critique.

Key Terms
مبدأ أول (First Principle)
صفات (attributes)
كثرة (multiplicity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what do the philosophers claim about the divine names?
A) They are meaningless.
B) They all refer to one meaning without multiplicity.
C) Each name refers to a distinct attribute.
D) They are only metaphors.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers affirm many divine names but insist they all refer to one simple reality, a claim that appears paradoxical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the divine names, do you ever pause to consider that each name points to a single, indivisible Reality? Or do you mentally divide God into separate attributes? How does this lesson's paradox mirror your own struggle to understand God's oneness?
?
The philosophers claim that all names are expressions of one meaning. Do you live as though every attribute of God you invoke – His mercy, His justice, His power – is a direct encounter with the same, undivided Being? Or do you parcel out His attributes to suit your needs? What would change if you truly believed that His mercy and His justice are not separate but the same reality?
?
Ghazali finds the philosophers' claim 'astonishing'. Does your own heart find anything astonishing about the unity of God? Have you ever been shaken by the mystery that the All-Merciful is also the All-Just? Reflect on a time when you struggled to reconcile two divine attributes. How did that struggle draw you closer to, or away from, Him?

The Philosophers' Solution: How Names Multiply Without Multiplicity
Ghazali explains the philosophers' methodology and their threefold mechanism for name multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation that divine names multiply only through negation, addition, or relation, not through any real multiplicity in God's essence.

Core Idea
No real multiplicity — divine names arise from how we relate or negate, not from divisions in God.

Ghazali now clarifies the core of the philosophers' doctrine. After affirming many names, they explain how this is possible without compromising God's oneness. The key is that all multiplicity comes from external relations, additions, or negations, not from any composition in the essence itself. Understanding this is crucial before critiquing it.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's methodological note
Ghazali states that he will first explain the doctrine properly before objecting. Notice the importance he places on correct understanding.
2
Identify the three sources of multiplicity
The philosophers say names multiply by adding something to Him, adding Him to something, or negating something from Him. List them.
3
Understand why these do not affect the essence
Negation and addition are external relations; they do not introduce real composition or multiplicity in the essence itself.
This is the core of their defense of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومع هذا فإنهم يقولون في البارى تعالى : إنه مبدأ ، وأول ، وموجود ، وجوهر ، وواحد ، وقديم، وباق ، وعالم ، وعقل ، وعاقل ، ومعقول ، وفاعل ، وخالق ، ومريد ، وقادر ، وحى ، وعاشق ، ومعشوق ، ولذيذ ، ومتلذذ ، وجواد ، وخير محض ، وزعموا : أن كل ذلك عبارة عن معنى واحد ، لا كثرة فيه ، وهذا من العجائب . فينبغي أن نحقق مذهبهم للتفهيم أولا ، ثم نشتغل بالاعتراض ، فإن الاعتراض على المذهب ، قبل تمام التفهيم ، رمى في عماية والعمدة في فهم مذهبهم ، أنهم يقولون : ذات المبدأ الأول واحدة ، وإنما تكثر الأسماء بإضافة شيء إليه ، أو إضافته إلى شيء ، أو سلب شيء عنه ، والسلب لا يوجب كثرة في الذات المسلوب عنه ، ولا الإضافة توجب كثرة
And with this, they say concerning the Creator, exalted: that He is a beginning, first, existent, substance, one, eternal, everlasting, knower, intellect, intelligent, intelligible, agent, creator, willer, powerful, living, lover, beloved, delicious, delighting, generous, pure good. So it is necessary that we verify their doctrine for explanation first, then occupy ourselves with objection. For objecting to a doctrine before complete explanation is throwing into blindness. Commentary
This passage serves as a conclusion to the enumeration of the five types of multiplicity and introduces the philosophers' paradoxical claim. After negating all multiplicity from God, they nevertheless affirm many names and attributes for Him—over twenty are listed. They claim that all these names refer to a single meaning without any multiplicity. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage states that negation does not cause multiplicity. In your own belief, have you ever thought that negating limitations from Allah still implies something negative? How does understanding negation as not affecting the essence change how you affirm His attributes?
?
What will it cost you to truly believe that Allah's essence is absolutely one, and that your calling Him by many names does not divide Him? Is there a part of your heart that still resists this simplicity?

The Philosophers' Many Names for God and Their Claim of Unity
Ghazali lists 22 names philosophers use for God and states their claim that all refer to one meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers affirm many positive names for God yet claim these names refer to a single meaning, revealing an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The philosophers use 22 names for God but say all are one—a puzzling claim.

In the previous discussion, the philosophers argued that the First has no quiddity separate from existence, which would seem to limit affirmative naming. Yet here Ghazali points out that they themselves use a long list of positive names for God. What is their explanation? They assert that all these names refer to one and the same meaning with no multiplicity. Ghazali finds this astonishing and uses it as a springboard for his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the long list of names and the final claim.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find the sentence that states the philosophers' claim: 'all of that is expression of one meaning, no multiplicity in it.' Underline it.
This claim is the central point Ghazali will challenge.
3
Reflect on the apparent contradiction
Consider why this claim is surprising. Many names like Lover and Beloved imply relation or action. How can they all refer to one meaning without multiplicity?
Ghazali's next arguments will explore this tension.
4
Note Ghazali's reaction
Observe the final phrase: 'And this is among the wonders.' This is Ghazali's puzzled reaction, setting up his critique.
This phrase signals the start of a refutation.

ومع هذا فإنهم يقولون في البارى تعالى : إنه مبدأ ، وأول ، وموجود ، وجوهر ، وواحد ، وقديم، وباق ، وعالم ، وعقل ، وعاقل ، ومعقول ، وفاعل ، وخالق ، ومريد ، وقادر ، وحى ، وعاشق ، ومعشوق ، ولذيذ ، ومتلذذ ، وجواد ، وخير محض ، وزعموا : أن كل ذلك عبارة عن معنى واحد ، لا كثرة فيه ، وهذا من العجائب
And despite this, they say concerning the Creator, exalted: He is Beginning, First, Existent, Substance, One, Eternal, Permanent, Knowing, Intellect, Intelligent, Intelligible, Agent, Creator, Willing, Powerful, Living, Lover, Beloved, Delighted, Delighting, Generous, Pure Good, and they claim that all of that is expression of one meaning, no multiplicity in it. And this is among the wonders.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the philosophers' position. After the previous argument that the First has no quiddity separate from existence, one might expect them to avoid positive names. Yet Ghazali lists 22 names they use for God, many of which are clearly positive attributes. The philosophers claim that all these names refer to a single meaning with no multiplicity in God. Ghazali remarks that this is astonishing ('ajab), setting up his critique.

The philosophers affirm many positive names for God but claim they refer to one meaning without multiplicity, a claim Ghazali finds astonishing and will critique.

Write down one way you can deepen your understanding of His oneness while using His names in dua.
?

Have you ever casually recited Allah's names without considering how each name reflects a different aspect of His essence? Commit to learning the meaning of one new name of Allah this week and reflect on how it enhances your awe of Him.
?
