The True Meaning of God's Generosity
Ghazali defines 'Generous' for God as giving all without need or return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of God's attribute 'Generous' and the two conditions that distinguish divine generosity from human generosity.

Core Idea
True generosity gives all without any self-interest.

When we call God 'Generous', we are making a profound claim about His nature. But what does 'generosity' really mean when applied to God? Ghazali cuts through our everyday assumptions and gives a precise definition that eliminates any hint of need or self-interest. In this passage, he explains two essential conditions for true generosity and shows that only God meets them perfectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning. Let Ghazali's own phrasing set the stage.
2
Identify the two conditions of generosity
The passage lists two conditions for true generosity. Find them and note how each condition eliminates a form of self-interest.
The first condition relates to the recipient, the second to the giver.
3
Compare human and divine generosity
Notice why Ghazali says that every human giver is actually a 'compensator' (musta'id) rather than truly generous. Contrast this with God's generosity.
This highlights the radical difference between creature and Creator.
4
Reflect on the theological payoff
Consider why Ghazali ends by stating that the name 'Generous' indicates God's existence plus action minus purpose, and how that avoids multiplicity in the divine essence.
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل له : جواد، أريد به أنه يفيض عنه الكل . لا لغرض يرجع إليه ، والجود يتم بشيئين : أحدهما ، أن يكون للمنعم عليه فائدة فيما وهب له ، له ، فإن من يهب شيئاً من هو مستغن عنه ، لا يوصف بالجود والثانى أن لا يحتاج الجواد إلى الجود ، فيكون إقدامه على الجود الحاجة نفسه ، وكل من يجود يمدح ويثني عليه ، أو يتخلص من مذمة ، فهو مستعيض ، وليس بجواد، وإنما الجود الحقيقى لله سبحانه وتعالى، فإنه ليس يبغى به خلاصاً عن ذم، ولا كمالاً مستفاداً بمدح ، فيكون الجواد اسماً منبئا عن وجوده مع إضافة إلى الفعل، وسلب للغرض ، فلا يؤدى إلى الكثرة في ذاته .
And when it is said of Him: 'Generous', it is meant that from Him all things emanate, not for a purpose returning to Him. And generosity is completed by two things: one is that the beneficiary should have a benefit in what is given to him, for whoever gives something to one who is needless of it is not described as generous. The second is that the generous one should not need generosity, so that his undertaking of generosity is due to need itself. Everyone who gives is praised or thanked, or frees himself from blame; he is a compensator, not generous. But true generosity belongs to God, the Exalted and Majestic, for He does not seek by it freedom from blame nor a perfection obtained by praise. So 'Generous' is a name indicating His existence with an addition to action and negation of purpose, so it does not lead to multiplicity in His essence.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine name 'Generous' (al-Jawad). Ghazali states that it means that from Him all things emanate, not for a purpose returning to Him. He then gives two conditions for true generosity: first, that the recipient should actually benefit from the gift (giving to someone who is not in need is not generosity); second, that the giver should not be in need of giving (so that giving is not due to his own need). Everyone who gives for praise, thanks, or to avoid blame is actually seeking compensation, not truly generous. True generosity belongs only to God, for He does not seek to free Himself from blame or gain perfection through praise. The name therefore indicates God's existence with an addition to action and a negation of purpose, without implying multiplicity in His essence.

Key Terms
جواد
الجود
الغرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human generosity can be fully analogous to God's generosity.
Correction: Ghazali shows that all human generosity is tainted by self-interest (praise, thanks, avoiding blame). Only God's generosity is absolutely free from any need or purpose, making it the only true generosity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the second condition for true generosity?
A) The giver must be praised.
B) The giver must not need generosity.
C) The gift must be large.
D) The giver must be wealthy.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute 'Generous' means He gives all things to creation without any need or purpose returning to Him, making Him the only truly generous being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of charity or kindness, do you ever catch yourself hoping for recognition or reciprocation? How does Ghazali's definition of true generosity expose the hidden self-interest in your heart? What would it take for you to give purely for God, expecting nothing in return?
?
Consider the times you have served God in prayer, fasting, or charity. Have any of these acts been tainted by a desire to earn status, escape guilt, or secure a reward? If God's generosity is without any purpose, how does your calculation of 'what you get out of it' become a form of compensation? Will you commit to examining your intentions before every act of worship?
?
If only God is truly generous, what does that mean for your relationship with Him? Are you willing to receive His generosity without trying to pay it back, or do you secretly feel you must earn His favor? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in His pure grace and abandon your need to 'deserve' His gifts?

Two Interpretations of 'Pure Good'
Ghazali explains 'Pure Good' as existence free from defect or as cause of order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways Ghazali explains the divine attribute 'Pure Good' (Khayr Mahd): as negation of defect and as cause of order.

Core Idea
Pure Good means existence free from defect or the source of all order.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles the meaning of the divine name 'Pure Good'. He offers two complementary interpretations. The first focuses on God's perfection and the non-existence of evil. The second links goodness to being the cause of cosmic order. Understanding these interpretations helps clarify how Islamic theology handles divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the two interpretations signaled by 'famma' and 'wa qad'.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Ghazali states the first interpretation (existence free from defect) and the second (cause of order). Note how he explains evil as non-existence.
The first interpretation focuses on negation, the second on positive causality.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on why Ghazali provides two possibilities. How do they avoid attributing a separate positive attribute to God?
Both interpretations treat 'Pure Good' as indicating God's essence with an additional relation or negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : خير محض ، فإما أن يراد به وجوده بريئاً عن النقص وإمكان العدم، فإن الشر لاذات له بل يرجع إلى عدم جوهر ، أو عدم صلاح حال الجوهر ، وإلا فالوجود من حيث إنه وجود – خير ، فيرجع هذا الاسم إلى السلب، لإمكان النقص والشر ، وقد يقال : خير ، لما هو السبب لنظام الأشياء ، والأول مبدأ لنظام كل شيء ، فهو خير ، ويكون الاسم دالا على الوجود ، مع نوع إضافة
And when it is said: 'Pure Good', then either it is meant by it His existence free from defect and possibility of non-existence, for evil has no essence but rather returns to the non-existence of a substance or the non-existence of a suitable state of the substance. Otherwise, existence insofar as it is existence is good, so this name returns to negation of the possibility of defect and evil. And sometimes it is said: 'Good' for that which is the cause of the order of things, and the First is the principle of the order of everything, so He is Good. And the name indicates existence with a kind of addition.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine name 'Pure Good' (Khayr Mahd). Ghazali gives two possible interpretations. The first is that God's existence is free from defect and the possibility of non-existence. He explains that evil has no real essence; it is simply the non-existence of a substance or the non-existence of a proper state of a substance. In itself, existence as such is good. So the name 'Pure Good' primarily indicates negation of defect and evil. The second interpretation is that 'Good' refers to that which is the cause of the order of things; since God is the First Principle of all order, He is Good. In either case, the name indicates God's existence with a kind of addition (i.e., negation of defect or causal relation), not a separate positive attribute.

Key Terms
خير محض
الشر
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that evil is a positive, created entity alongside good.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that evil has no essence (la dhat lahu) but is merely non-existence of a substance or its suitable state. Existence as such is good.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is evil?
A) A positive entity created by God.
B) The non-existence of a substance or its unsuitable state.
C) A separate attribute of God.
D) The opposite of good in equal measure.

Key Takeaway
Pure Good refers either to God's existence free from all defect (negation of evil) or to His being the cause of all order in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil has no real essence but is only the absence of good (non-existence of a substance or its proper state), how do you explain the evil you see or experience in your own life? Does this change how you respond to it, or does it feel like empty theory?
?
Ghazali says existence in itself is good. When you feel your own existence is full of defect or disorder, can you still affirm that your existence—just as existence—is good? What would it cost you to believe that?
?
God is Pure Good as the cause of all order. Look at one area of your life today that feels chaotic or broken. If God is truly the source of order, what step of surrender must you take to align with that order, even if it makes no sense to you?

Meaning of 'Necessary Existence'
Ghazali defines 'Necessary Existence' as existence with no cause and impossibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'Necessary Existence' (Wajib al-Wujud) is defined by the negation of any cause for existence and the impossibility of any cause for non-existence.

Core Idea
Necessity of existence is defined by what it lacks: a cause and the possibility of ceasing.

Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers by clarifying the term 'Necessary Existence'. Before debating whether the world is necessary or contingent, we must settle what 'necessary' means. This passage gives the precise definition: existence without cause and impossibility of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the phrase 'with negation of a cause for its existence' and 'impossibility of a cause for its non-existence'.
2
Identify the two negations
Understand that necessity is defined by two negations: no cause for existence, and no possibility of non-existence.
The definition does not describe a positive quality.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Ghazali defines necessity negatively. This sets the foundation for arguing that only God can be necessary.
Contrast with possible existents that have a cause.

And when it is said: 'Necessary Existence', its meaning is this existence with negation of a cause for its existence and impossibility of a cause for its non-existence, first and last.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the fundamental term 'Necessary Existence' (Wajib al-Wujud). Ghazali states that it means this particular existence (i.e., God's existence) with the negation of any cause for its existence (it is uncaused) and the impossibility of any cause for its non-existence (it cannot cease to be), both initially and perpetually. The definition is purely negative: it denies a cause for existence and denies any possibility of non-existence.

Key Takeaway

Meaning of 'Lover, Beloved, Pleasure' in God
Ghazali explains the divine attributes of love and pleasure as God's perception of His own perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali reinterprets terms like 'lover,' 'beloved,' and 'pleasure' to apply to God, emphasizing the perfection and security of divine self-awareness.

Core Idea
God's pleasure is His perception of His own perfect, secure, and eternal attributes.

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa addresses a sensitive theological point: how can we speak of God 'loving' or 'taking pleasure'? Ghazali carefully defines these terms to show that they refer to God's perfect self-awareness, not human emotions. He uses a human analogy to clarify, then elevates the concept to the divine level.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let Ghazali's own words set the stage.
Notice how he defines 'lover' and 'beloved' from the start.
2
Understand the definition of pleasure
Ghazali defines pleasure as 'perception of suitable perfection.' Grasp this definition, as it is key to the whole argument.
This is not a feeling but an intellectual recognition.
3
Analyze the human analogy
Consider the hypothetical human who knows all his perfections. How would he feel? Why is his pleasure limited?
Focus on the role of fear of loss and deficiency.
4
Contrast with God
Now see how Ghazali moves from the human analogy to God: all perfections are present, secure, and eternal. Therefore, God's pleasure is incomparable.
This is the climax of the passage.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali ends by stating that divine pleasure is too exalted to relate to ours. Sit with this conclusion and its implications.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : ( عاشق ومشعوق ولذيذ وملتذ ) ، فمعناه هو أن كل جمال وبهاء وكمال فهو محبوب ومعشوق لذى الكمال ، ولا معنى للذة إلا إدراك الكمال الملائم ، ومن عرف كمال نفسه في إحاطته بالمعلومات – لو أحاط بها - وفى جمال صورته ، وفى كمال قدرته ، وقوة أعضائه ، وبالجملة إدراكه لحضور كل كمال ممكن له ، لو أمكن أن يتصور ذلك في هو إنسان واحد ؛ لكان محبا لكماله وملتذا به ، وإنما تنتقص لذته بتقدير العدم والنقصان ، فإن السرور لا يتم بما يزول أو يخشى زواله ، والأول له البهاء الأكمل ، والجمال الأتم ؛ إذ كل كمال هو ممكن له فهو حاصل له ، وهو مدرك لذلك الكمال ، مع الأمن من إمكان النقصان والزوال ، والكمال الحاصل له فوق كل كمال ، فحبه وعشقه لذلك الكمال فوق كل حب ، والتذاذه به فوق كل التذاذ ، بل لا نسبة للذاتنا إليها البتة ، بل هى أجل من أن
And when it is said: 'Lover and Beloved and Pleasure and Delighted', its meaning is that every beauty, splendor, and perfection is beloved and loved by the possessor of perfection. And there is no meaning for pleasure except the perception of the suitable perfection. And whoever knows his own perfection in encompassing his informations—if he encompassed them—and in the beauty of his form, and in the perfection of his power and the strength of his limbs, and in sum, his perception of the presence of every perfection possible for him—if it were possible to imagine that in one human—he would be a lover of his perfection and taking pleasure in it. But his pleasure is diminished by the supposition of non-existence and deficiency, for joy is not completed by what disappears or is feared to disappear. And the First has the most complete splendor and the most perfect beauty; since every perfection possible for Him is actually present for Him, and He perceives that perfection, with security from the possibility of deficiency and disappearance, and the perfection present for Him is above every perfection, so His love and passion for that perfection is above all love, and His pleasure in it is above all pleasure. Rather, there is no relation of our pleasures to it at all; rather, it is too great to be
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the divine names 'Lover, Beloved, Pleasure, Delighted' (Ashiq, Mashuq, Ladhdha, Multadhdh). Ghazali begins by stating that every beauty, splendor, and perfection is loved by its possessor. Pleasure is defined as the perception of a suitable perfection. He uses a human analogy: if a person were to fully know his own perfections in knowledge, form, power, strength, and perceive all his perfections as present, he would love his perfection and take pleasure in it. However, human pleasure is diminished by the possibility of loss or deficiency. In contrast, God has the most complete splendor and perfect beauty; every possible perfection is actually present in Him, and He perceives it with security from any deficiency or disappearance. Therefore, His love and passion for His own perfection surpass all love, and His pleasure surpasses all pleasure. In fact, our pleasures bear no relation to His at all; His pleasure is too exalted to be compared.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think divine love and pleasure are similar to human emotions, just stronger.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes that divine love and pleasure are incomparably greater and free from any deficiency or fear of loss; they are not emotions but intellectual perceptions.

Checkpoint

A) Because God has more perfections than any human.
B) Because God's perfections are all present and secure from loss, while human pleasure is diminished by fear of loss.
C) Because God is the only one who can feel pleasure.
D) Because God's pleasure is the same as His essence.

Key Takeaway
God's love and pleasure are not emotional but refer to His perfect, secure, and eternal perception of His own infinite attributes.

The Meaning of Al-Jawad (The Generous) as Applied to God
Ghazali explains the philosophical definition of God as al-Jawad (the Generous).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's philosophical definition of God's attribute of generosity (al-Jawad) and how it avoids implying multiplicity in God's essence.

Core Idea
God's generosity is pure giving without any need or reward.

This passage from Ghazali's critique of the philosophers examines the meaning of one of God's names, al-Jawad (the Generous). Ghazali contrasts human generosity, which is often motivated by praise or avoiding blame, with God's absolute selflessness. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Islamic philosophical concept of divine attributes without multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast structure: first human, then divine.
2
Identify the key contrast
Find where Ghazali distinguishes true generosity from motivated giving. What is the defining criterion?
The term 'musta'id' (compensated) is crucial.
3
Understand the theological implication
Reflect on why Ghazali emphasizes that this definition does not lead to multiplicity in God's essence. How does 'negation of purpose' preserve divine unity?
This connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى أن لا يحتاج الجواد إلى الجود ، فيكون إقدامه على الجود الحاجة نفسه ، وكل من يجود يمدح ويثني عليه ، أو يتخلص من مذمة ، فهو مستعيض ، وليس بجواد، وإنما الجود الحقيقى لله سبحانه وتعالى، فإنه ليس يبغى به خلاصاً عن ذم، ولا كمالاً مستفاداً بمدح ، فيكون الجواد اسماً منبئا عن وجوده مع إضافة إلى الفعل، وسلب للغرض ، فلا يؤدى إلى الكثرة في ذاته .
And the second (point) is that the Generous does not need generosity, so his proceeding to generosity is the need itself. And everyone who gives and is praised and commended, or frees himself from blame, is compensated (musta'id) and is not generous. But true generosity belongs to God, may He be exalted, for He does not seek thereby escape from blame, nor perfection acquired through praise. So 'al-Jawad' is a name indicating His existence with an addition to action and negation of purpose; it does not lead to multiplicity in His essence.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines one of God's names according to the philosophers: al-Jawad (the Generous). The argument proceeds by contrasting true generosity with human generosity. Humans often give to gain praise or avoid blame; such giving is a transaction (isti'ada) and not pure generosity. God's generosity is entirely free from any need, reward, or external motive. Therefore, the name 'al-Jawad' indicates God's existence along with the performance of action (generosity) and the negation of any purpose or gain. This definition ensures that attributing generosity to God does not imply multiplicity in His essence, because it is a relational attribute that does not add a real component to His being.

Key Terms
الجواد (al-jawad)
الجود (al-jood)
مستعيض (musta'id)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's generosity is similar to human generosity, motivated by some need or desire for reward.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's generosity is absolutely selfless, without any need, praise, or blame avoidance, unlike human generosity.

Checkpoint

A) God gives more than humans
B) God's generosity is not motivated by any need or gain
C) Humans cannot be generous at all
D) God's generosity is an essential attribute

Key Takeaway
True generosity is giving without any need or reward, and this alone applies to God, preserving His unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
God's generosity is not motivated by need or reward. Reflect on a specific relationship in your life—do you give to that person out of a sense of obligation, or out of pure grace? How can you move closer to giving without expecting anything back, as a reflection of al-Jawad?

The Two Meanings of Pure Good
Ghazali defines the attribute 'Pure Good' and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'Pure Good' as applied to God: one negative (absence of evil and deficiency) and one positive (cause of cosmic order).

Core Idea
God's goodness is both the absence of evil and the source of all order.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali unpacks the divine attribute 'Pure Good' (Khair Mahd). He shows that this attribute can be understood in two complementary ways: as a negation of any deficiency, and as a positive attribution of being the cause of cosmic order. Understanding these nuances helps avoid the misconception that evil has independent existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let Ghazali's own words introduce the concept.
Notice the two conditional phrases: 'فإما أن يراد به...' and 'وقد يقال...'
2
Identify the two meanings
The passage presents two ways to understand 'Pure Good'. Find the first (negative) meaning and the second (positive) meaning. Note how each is expressed.
The first meaning involves negation (سلب), the second involves addition (إضافة).
3
Reflect on evil as privation
The passage states that evil has no essence but is merely non-existence. Consider why this matters for understanding God's attribute of Pure Good.
This guards against the misconception that evil is a positive reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : خير محض ، فإما أن يراد به وجوده بريئاً عن النقص وإمكان العدم، فإن الشر لاذات له بل يرجع إلى عدم جوهر ، أو عدم صلاح حال الجوهر ، وإلا فالوجود من حيث إنه وجود – خير ، فيرجع هذا الاسم إلى السلب، لإمكان النقص والشر ، وقد يقال : خير ، لما هو السبب لنظام الأشياء ، والأول مبدأ لنظام كل شيء ، فهو خير ، ويكون الاسم دالا على الوجود ، مع نوع إضافة .
And if it is said: Pure Good, then it is either intended by it His existence free from deficiency and the possibility of non-existence. For evil has no essence but rather refers to the non-existence of substance, or the non-existence of the well-being of the substance's state. Otherwise, existence insofar as it is existence is good. So this name refers to negation, due to the possibility of deficiency and evil. And it may be said: Good, for that which is the cause of the order of things. And the First is the principle of the order of everything, so He is Good. And the name indicates existence, with a kind of addition.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of God being called 'Khair Mahd' (Pure Good). Two possible interpretations are given. First, it means God's existence is free from any deficiency and from the possibility of non-existence. This interpretation is negative because it denies evil and deficiency. The passage then clarifies that evil has no positive reality; it is merely the absence of substance or the absence of a good state in substance. Second, 'Good' can be understood positively as the cause of the order in all things. Since God is the first principle of all order, He is Good. Thus the name 'Khair Mahd' combines a negation (of evil/deficiency) and a positive attribution (causing order). It indicates God's existence with an addition of relational meaning.

خير محض (khair mahd)
شر (sharr)
وجود (wujud)
عدم ('adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil has an independent positive existence.
Correction: The passage states that evil has no essence; it is merely the non-existence (عدم) of a substance or the non-existence of its good state.

Checkpoint

A) A positive substance
B) The non-existence of substance or its good state
C) A separate created entity
D) The absence of divine goodness

Key Takeaway
God is called Pure Good because He is free from all evil and deficiency and is the source of all cosmic order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If existence itself is good, what does that say about your own existence? Do you live as if your existence is a gift of goodness from God, or do you view it as a burden? What one change can you make today to reflect gratitude for the sheer fact that you exist?

The Meaning of Wajib al-Wujud and Divine Love and Pleasure
Ghazali defines 'Wajib al-Wujud' and explains divine love and pleasure through analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of 'Necessary Existent' and how God's love and pleasure are based on His perfect self-knowledge.

Core Idea
God's love and pleasure are the perfect self-awareness of His infinite perfection.

Imam al-Ghazali begins by defining a key term used by the philosophers: 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existent). He then explains how this concept relates to God being described as Lover, Beloved, and taking pleasure. In this passage, he sets the foundation for understanding divine attributes as rooted in God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Wajib al-Wujud
Notice that the definition is purely negative: it denies any cause for existence or non-existence. This sets God apart from all contingent beings.
2
Examine the analogy of human self-love
Ghazali gives an analogy of a human who perceives his own perfection and loves it. This helps understand how God's self-knowledge entails love.
Human pleasure is imperfect because of possible loss.
3
Understand the infinity of God's love
Because God's perfection is eternal and unchanging, His love and pleasure are infinite, beyond comparison to any created love.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : واجب الوجود ، فمعناه هذا الوجود ، مع سلب علة لوجوده ، وإحالة علة لعدمه أولاً وآخراً ، وإذا قيل : عاشق ومشعوق ولذيذ وملتذ ، فمعناه هو أن كل جمال وبهاء وكمال فهو محبوب ومعشوق لذى الكمال ، ولا معنى للذة إلا إدراك الكمال الملائم ، ومن عرف كمال نفسه في إحاطته بالمعلومات – لو أحاط بها - وفى جمال صورته ، وفى كمال قدرته ، وقوة أعضائه ، وبالجملة إدراكه لحضور كل كمال ممكن له ، لو أمكن أن يتصور ذلك في هو إنسان واحد ؛ لكان محبا لكماله وملتذا به ، وإنما تنتقص لذته بتقدير العدم والنقصان ، فإن السرور لا يتم بما يزول أو يخشى زواله ، والأول له البهاء الأكمل ، والجمال الأتم ؛ إذ كل كمال هو ممكن له فهو حاصل له ، وهو مدرك لذلك الكمال ، مع الأمن من إمكان النقصان والزوال ، والكمال الحاصل له فوق كل كمال ، فحبه وعشقه لذلك الكمال فوق كل حب ، والتذاذه به فوق كل التذاذ ، بل لا نسبة للذاتنا إليها البتة ، بل هى أجل من أن يعبر عنها باللذة والسرور والطيبة ، إلا أن تلك المعانى ليست لها عبارات عندنا ، فلابد من الإبعاد فى الاستعارة ، كما نستعير له لفظ المريد ، والمختار والفاعل ، منا ، مع القطع ببعد إرادته عن إرادتنا ، وبعد علمه وقدرته عن علمنا وقدرتنا ، ولا بعد فى أن نستبشع لفظة اللذة ، فيستعمل غيرها . والمقصود أن حاله أشرف من أحوال الملائكة ، وأحرى بأن يكون مغبوطاً وحال الملائكة أشرف من أحوالنا ولو لم تكن لذة إلا في شهوة البطن والفرج ، لكان حال الحمار والخنزير أشرف من حال الملائكة ، وليست لها لذة – أى للمبادئ من الملائكة المجردة عن المادة - إلا فى السرور بالشعور بماخصوا به الكمال والجمال الذى لا يخشى زواله ، ولكن الذي للأول فوق الذى للملائكة ، فإن وجود الملائكة - التى هى العقول المجردة – وجود ممكن في ذاته ، واجب الوجود بغيره ، وإمكان العدم نوع شر ونقص ، فليس شيء بريئاً عن كل شر مطلقاً سوى الأول ، فهو الخير المحض ، وله البهاء والجمال الأكمل ، ثم هو معشوق ، عشقه غيره أو لم يعشقه ، كما أنه عاقل ومعقول عقله غيره أو لم يعقله ، وكل هذه المعانى راجعة إلى ذاته ، وإلى إدراكه لذاته ، وعقله له ، وعقله لذاته ، هو عين ذاته ، فإنه عقل مجرد ، فرجع الكل إلى معنى واحد .
And if it is said: Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud), its meaning is this existence, with the negation of a cause for its existence, and the impossibility of a cause for its non-existence from the beginning and end. And if it is said: Lover and Beloved, and Pleasurable and Pleasure-seeker, its meaning is that every beauty, splendor, and perfection is beloved and loved by the possessor of perfection. And there is no meaning for pleasure except the perception of fitting perfection. And whoever knows his own perfection in his encompassing of knowables – if he were to encompass them – and in the beauty of his form, and in the perfection of his power, and the strength of his limbs, and in sum his perception of the presence of every possible perfection for him, if it could be imagined that this be in one human being, he would be a lover of his perfection and taking pleasure in it. His pleasure is only diminished by the estimation of non-existence and deficiency, for joy is not complete with what ceases or whose cessation is feared. The First has the most complete splendor and the most perfect beauty, since every perfection possible for Him is actual for Him, and He is perceiver of that perfection, with safety from the possibility of deficiency and cessation. The perfection present for Him is above every perfection, so His love and passion for that perfection is above every love, and His taking pleasure in it is above every pleasure. Rather, there is no comparison of our pleasure to it at all. Rather, it is too great to be expressed by pleasure, joy, and delight. But those meanings have no expressions for us, so one must resort to metaphor, just as we use for Him the terms 'willer', 'chooser', 'agent' from us, while affirming the distance of His will from our will, and the distance of His knowledge and power from our knowledge and power. And there is no harm in finding the term 'pleasure' objectionable, so another term is used. The point is that His state is more noble than the states of the angels, and more deserving of being envied. The state of the angels is more noble than our states. If there were no pleasure except in the desire of the belly and genitals, then the state of the donkey and pig would be more noble than the state of the angels. They (the angels, i.e., the principles, the angels stripped of matter) have no pleasure except in joy through the awareness of the perfection and beauty they have been given, which is not feared to cease. But that which belongs to the First is above that which belongs to the angels, for the existence of the angels – which are the abstract intellects – is possible existence in itself, necessary existence through another, and the possibility of non-existence is a kind of evil and deficiency. So nothing is free from every evil absolutely except the First. He is Pure Good. He has the most complete splendor and beauty. Then He is Beloved, whether others love Him or not, just as He is Intelligent and Intelligible whether others intellection Him or not. And all these meanings revert to His essence, and to His perception of His essence, and His intellection of Him [i.e., of His essence] and His intellection of His essence is His very essence, for He is abstract intellect. So everything reverts to one meaning.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the name 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existent) and then explains the philosophic understanding of God as Lover, Beloved, and subject of pleasure. The definition of Wajib al-Wujud is short: it affirms existence while negating any cause for existence and negating any cause for non-existence. Then the longer part addresses the attributes of love and pleasure. The argument uses an analogy: a human who perfectly knows his own perfection would love it and take pleasure in it. However, human pleasure is diminished by the possibility of loss. God, by contrast, possesses all perfection actually and eternally, with no possibility of deficiency, so His love and pleasure are infinite and incomparable. The passage emphasizes that these terms are metaphorical, as human language cannot capture the divine reality. The superiority of God over angels is then argued: angels are possible beings and thus susceptible to non-existence, so they are not pure good. Only God is pure good. Finally, all these attributes—being necessary, good, lover, beloved, intellect, intelligible—reduce to one meaning: God's self-knowledge, because He is pure intellect.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
عاشق
معشوق
لذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what diminishes human pleasure in perfection?
A) Lack of knowledge
B) The possibility of loss or deficiency
C) Lack of desire
D) External obstacles

Key Takeaway
God's love and pleasure are the perfect self-awareness of His eternal, unchanging perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's love, do you truly believe it is based on His own eternal perfection—not on anything you do—or do you still secretly measure His love by your actions? What would need to change in your heart for you to rest entirely in that reality?
?
This lesson says God's pleasure is complete because He cannot lose His perfection. But your own joys are always mixed with the fear of loss. Where in your life are you clutching a gift from God as if losing it would destroy you? How does knowing God's secure self-sufficiency help you let go?
?
If you really knew that God's love is infinite and unchanging, what would you dare to do or become for His sake that you have been too afraid to attempt? Will you take one step toward that today, trusting in His love not your own strength?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Metaphorical Nature of Divine Attributes and the Transcendence of God's Pleasure
Ghazali emphasizes the metaphorical nature of divine attributes and the superiority of God over angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp that divine pleasure transcends human conception, and that all terms used for God are metaphorical, emphasizing His incomparability.

Core Idea
Human language cannot capture divine reality; all attributes are used metaphorically.

After establishing the infinite nature of God's pleasure, Ghazali insists that such pleasure is beyond human language. He compares God's state to that of angels, showing that even the highest created beings are contingent and thus not pure good. This lesson highlights the limits of our language and the absolute transcendence of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial of comparison
Notice Ghazali's strong statement: 'rather, our pleasure has absolutely no comparison to it.' This establishes God's utter transcendence.
2
Understand the need for metaphor
Ghazali explains that since we lack words for divine realities, we must use metaphors, just as we do for will and power, while affirming that God's attributes are far beyond ours.
3
Examine the angelic comparison
Ghazali compares God's state to the angels, arguing that angels, though pure intellects, are contingent and thus not free from the possibility of non-existence, which is a type of evil. Only God is pure good.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine pleasure can be understood as similar to human pleasure.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly denies any comparison, stating that God's pleasure is far beyond our conception, and we must use metaphors while affirming distance.

Checkpoint
Why is only God 'Pure Good' according to Ghazali?
A) Because God is the most powerful
B) Because God has no possibility of non-existence
C) Because God created everything
D) Because God is the only intellect

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence requires metaphorical language, and only He is absolutely free from any evil or deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine that the pleasures of Paradise are merely a better version of earthly pleasures? How does Ghazali's insistence that divine pleasure is beyond any human concept challenge your current hope for the afterlife? What would it mean to yearn for something you cannot even articulate?
?
If only God is Pure Good, and your own existence is contingent, how does that affect your sense of self-worth? Are you building your identity on something that can cease? Today, consciously anchor your worth in the One who is absolutely free from deficiency.
?
Ghazali says we must use metaphors for God. Is there a metaphor you have adopted for God that you need to peel back, recognizing it falls short? Spend time in silence before the One who is beyond all descriptions, and let go of a comfortable image of Him.

Divine Attributes
advanced
4 min
The Unity of Divine Attributes: All Attributes Return to One Meaning
Ghazali concludes that all divine attributes revert to God's self-knowledge and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how all divine attributes—love, belovedness, intellect, intelligibility—are ultimately one and the same in God's essence.

Core Idea
In God, all attributes reduce to one simple reality: His self-knowledge.

In the concluding part of this passage, Ghazali synthesizes the discussion. He emphasizes that whether God is loved or not, He remains the Beloved because His perfection is intrinsic. Then he makes a profound statement: all these attributes—being lover, beloved, intellect, intelligible—revert to God's essence and His self-knowledge, which is identical to His essence. This demonstrates the simplicity and unity of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement about God as Beloved
Notice that God is beloved regardless of whether others love Him, because His beauty is objective, not dependent on perception.
2
Examine the reduction to one meaning
Ghazali asserts that all these attributes are ultimately the same: God's intellection of His essence is His essence itself, since He is pure intellect.
This is a key philosophical concept of divine simplicity.
3
Reflect on the implications
Understand that this means God's attributes are not separate 'parts' but identical with His essence, avoiding multiplicity in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If there were no pleasure except in the desire of the belly and genitals, then the state of the donkey and pig would be more noble than the state of the angels. They (the angels, i.e., the principles, the angels stripped of matter) have no pleasure except in joy through the awareness of the perfection and beauty they have been given, which is not feared to cease. But that which belongs to the First is above that which belongs to the angels, for the existence of the angels – which are the abstract intellects – is possible existence in itself, necessary existence through another, and the possibility of non-existence is a kind of evil and deficiency. So nothing is free from every evil absolutely except the First. He is Pure Good. He has the most complete splendor and beauty. Then He is Beloved, whether others love Him or not, just as He is Intelligent and Intelligible whether others intellection Him or not. And all these meanings revert to His essence, and to His perception of His essence, and His intellection of Him [i.e., of His essence] and His intellection of His essence is His very essence, for He is abstract intellect. So everything reverts to one meaning.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the name 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existent) and then explains the philosophic understanding of God as Lover, Beloved, and subject of pleasure. The definition of Wajib al-Wujud is short: it affirms existence while negating any cause for existence and negating any cause for non-existence. Then the longer part addresses the attributes of love and pleasure. The argument uses an analogy: a human who perfectly knows his own perfection would love it and take pleasure in it. However, human pleasure is diminished by the possibility of loss. God, by contrast, possesses all perfection actually and eternally, with no possibility of deficiency, so His love and pleasure are infinite and incomparable. The passage emphasizes that these terms are metaphorical, as human language cannot capture the divine reality. The superiority of God over angels is then argued: angels are possible beings and thus susceptible to non-existence, so they are not pure good. Only God is pure good. Finally, all these attributes—being necessary, good, lover, beloved, intellect, intelligible—reduce to one meaning: God's self-knowledge, because He is pure intellect.

Key Terms
معشوق
عاقل
معقول
عقل مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are separate from His essence.
Correction: Ghazali argues that attributes like intellect, lover, and beloved are identical with God's essence, as they all return to self-knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the single meaning to which all divine attributes revert, according to Ghazali?
A) God's power
B) God's will
C) God's self-knowledge
D) God's mercy

Key Takeaway
All divine attributes are ultimately one: God's simple self-knowledge, identical with His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite that God is the Beloved whether you love Him or not. Does your own love for Him reflect that truth, or do you treat His lovability as if it depends on your emotional state? What will you do today to fix your heart on His unchanging worth?
?
Ghazali says God's self-knowledge is identical to His essence. You are fully known by God. Are you willing to let go of the illusion that you can hide any part of yourself from Him? What one area of your life are you currently not transparent before Him, and what step will you take to bring it into the light?
?
If all divine attributes collapse into one simple reality, then your journey to know God cannot be about accumulating concepts—it must be about unification. Are you willing to simplify your spiritual life, shedding distractions that fragment your attention from His oneness? What specific attachment will you release today?

Divine Pleasure and the First Being's Superiority
Ghazali explains the philosophers' view on divine pleasure and the First Being's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical view of divine pleasure as metaphorical, and why the First Being's joy is superior to any other being's.

Core Idea
God's pleasure is not sensual; His necessary existence makes His joy absolute.

In this passage, Ghazali explains how philosophers describe God's attributes of pleasure and joy using metaphors from human experience. He then argues that the First Being's state of perfection and pure goodness far surpasses that of the angels, whose existence is contingent. This sets the stage for understanding the unique nature of the First.

Guided Path
1
Understand the use of metaphor
Ghazali points out that terms like 'pleasure' are borrowed from human language and must be stripped of human connotations. Recognize that we use similar metaphors for attributes like will and power.
Metaphor is necessary because our language cannot directly express divine realities.
2
Compare the hierarchies
Notice the ranking: God's state is nobler than the angels', and the angels' state is nobler than ours. Sensual pleasure is base; angels delight in intellectual beauty, which is superior.
If pleasure were only sensual, animals would be higher than angels.
3
Identify the deficiency in angels
Angels' existence is possible in itself and necessary through another, implying potential non-existence, which is a kind of evil. Only the First Being is necessary in itself and free from all evil.
This deficiency explains why God's pleasure is superior.
4
Grasp the unity of attributes in God
All divine attributes – being loved, intelligent, intelligible – reduce to His essence. He is a pure intellect, so these are one reality.
This demonstrates the simplicity and perfection of the First Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعبر عنها باللذة والسرور والطيبة، إلا أن تلك المعانى ليست لها عبارات عندنا، فلابد من الإبعاد فى الاستعارة، كما نستعير له لفظ المريد، والمختار والفاعل، منا، مع القطع ببعد إرادته عن إرادتنا، وبعد علمه وقدرته عن علمنا وقدرتنا، ولا بعد فى أن نستبشع لفظة اللذة، فيستعمل غيرها. والمقصود أن حاله أشرف من أحوال الملائكة، وأحرى بأن يكون مغبوطاً وحال الملائكة أشرف من أحوالنا ولو لم تكن لذة إلا في شهوة البطن والفرج، لكان حال الحمار والخنزير أشرف من حال الملائكة، وليست لها لذة – أى للمبادئ من الملائكة المجردة عن المادة - إلا فى السرور بالشعور بماخصوا به الكمال والجمال الذى لا يخشى زواله، ولكن الذي للأول فوق الذى للملائكة، فإن وجود الملائكة - التى هى العقول المجردة – وجود ممكن في ذاته، واجب الوجود بغيره، وإمكان العدم نوع شر ونقص، فليس شيء بريئاً عن كل شر مطلقاً سوى الأول، فهو الخير المحض، وله البهاء والجمال الأكمل، ثم هو معشوق، عشقه غيره أو لم يعشقه، كما أنه عاقل ومعقول عقله غيره أو لم يعقله، وكل هذه المعانى راجعة إلى ذاته، وإلى إدراكه لذاته، وعقله له، وعقله لذاته، هو عين ذاته، فإنه عقل مجرد، فرجع الكل إلى معنى واحد. فهذا طريق تفهيم مذهبهم.
It is expressed by pleasure, delight, and goodness, but those meanings have no expressions in our language, so one must go far in metaphor, just as we borrow for Him the terms 'willing', 'choosing', and 'acting' from ourselves, while decisively cutting off that His will is far from our will, and His knowledge and power are far from our knowledge and power. And there is no harm if one finds the term 'pleasure' ugly, so another is used. The point is that His state is nobler than the states of the angels, and more deserving of being envied; and the state of the angels is nobler than our states. If there were no pleasure except in the appetite of the belly and genitals, then the state of the donkey and pig would be nobler than the state of the angels. And they (i.e., the first principles among the angels detached from matter) have no pleasure except in delight through awareness of what they have been given of perfection and beauty that is not feared to vanish. But that which belongs to the First is above that which belongs to the angels. For the existence of the angels—which are the detached intellects—is possible in itself, necessary through another. The possibility of non-existence is a kind of evil and deficiency. So nothing is completely free from all evil except the First. He is pure good, and He has the most perfect splendor and beauty. Then He is loved, whether another loves Him or not, just as He is intellect and intelligible, whether another intellects Him or not. All these meanings refer to His essence, and to His perception of His essence, and His intellection of Him, and His intellection of His essence is identical to His essence, for He is a detached intellect. So everything returns to a single meaning. This is a way of explaining their doctrine.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the philosophers' view on the nature of pleasure in the First Being and the separated intellects (angels). Ghazali begins by noting that when philosophers speak of God's pleasure, delight, and goodness, these terms are metaphorical because we lack direct expressions for such realities. As we use terms like 'willing' and 'powerful' for God while negating similarity to human attributes, so we use 'pleasure' metaphorically. Ghazali then argues that God's state is superior to that of the angels, and the angels' state superior to ours. If pleasure were only sensual, animals would be higher than angels. Instead, the angels' pleasure comes from awareness of their own perfection and eternal beauty, but God's pleasure surpasses this because His existence is necessary in itself, while angels' existence is contingent. The possibility of non-existence in angels implies a kind of deficiency, so only the First is free from all evil—He is pure good. He is intrinsically beloved, intelligent, and intelligible, and all these attributes reduce to His essence, since He is a pure intellect. Ghazali concludes that this is a way to convey the philosophers' doctrine.

Misconception: That God's pleasure is similar to human sensory pleasure.
Correction: Ghazali explains that divine pleasure is metaphorical; it is not based on physical appetites but on the awareness of absolute perfection and necessary existence.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the First Being's pleasure superior to that of the angels?
A) Because He has greater sensory delights.
B) Because His existence is necessary in itself and free from any possibility of non-existence.
C) Because He is loved by others.
D) Because He is a detached intellect.

Key Takeaway
God's pleasure is not comparable to human or angelic pleasure; it flows from His necessary existence and absolute perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You spend your life chasing fleeting pleasures that vanish. The First Being's joy is absolute and eternal. In light of this lesson, what specific pleasure are you clinging to that, when compared to God's perfection, is like the pleasure of a donkey? Will you trade it for something higher?
?
The angels rejoice in their awareness of their own perfection that never fades. But your perfection is not your own—you are contingent. How does knowing that only God is pure good and free from all evil change how you view your own achievements and failures? What do you truly have to boast about?
?
If all God's attributes reduce to His essence, then His pleasure is not a separate emotion but His very being. You cannot please Him with external acts if you do not align your existence with His will. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient or as if your existence depends on Him every moment? What would change if you truly felt your contingency?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Philosophers' Argument Against Real Attributes
Philosophers argue additional attributes necessitate multiplicity, so impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that affirming real, additional attributes in God leads to multiplicity, and why they therefore deny such attributes.

Core Idea
Real attributes would make God multiple, so they must be denied.

The philosophers and Mu'tazila agree that God cannot have attributes distinct from His essence. They argue that even if attributes are concomitant, they remain distinct things, so multiplicity follows. This lesson presents that argument as a challenge to the traditional Islamic view of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the core claim: attributes cannot be additional to God's essence.
2
Follow the reasoning from temporal to concomitant
The philosophers illustrate their point with temporal attributes: when an attribute occurs to us, we recognise it as additional. Even if it were non-temporal but concomitant, it would still be distinct. Understand this analogy.
3
Understand the conclusion about multiplicity
They conclude that any two things that are not identical are two distinct things. Therefore, real attributes would necessitate multiplicity in the Necessary Being, which is impossible.
This is the heart of their argument.
4
Reflect on the disagreement
Note that this view contradicts the majority of Muslims who affirm real attributes. The philosophers admit that the names are permissible but deny they refer to additional realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٦] اتفقت الفلاسفة على استحالة إثبات العلم والقدرة والإرادة ، للمبدأ الأول كما اتفقت عليه المعتزلة، وزعموا أن هذه الأسامى وردت شرعاً ، ويجوز إطلاقاً لغة ، ولكن ترجع إلى ذات واحدة ، كما سبق . ولا يجوز إثبات صفات زائدة على ذاته ، كما يجوز في حقنا أن يكون علمنا وقدرتنا أوصافاً له زائدة على ذاتنا ، وزعموا أن ذلك يوجب كثرة ، لأن هذه الصفات لو طرأت علينا لكنا نعلم أنها زائدة على الذات ، إذ تجددت ؛ ولو قدرت مقارنة لوجودنا من غير تأخير لما خرج عن كونه زائداً على الذات بالمقارنة ، فكل شيئين إذا طرأ أحدهما على الآخر ، وعلم أن هذا ليس ذاك ، وذاك ليس هذا ، فلو اقترنا أيضاً، لعقل كونهما شيئين ، فإذن لا تخرج هذه الصفات بأن تكون صفات مقارنة لذات الأول ، عن أن تكون أشياء سوى الذات، فيوجب ذلك كثرة في واجب الوجود . وهو محال ، فلهذا أجمعوا على نفى الصفات فيقال لهم : وبم عرفتم استحالة الكثرة من هذا الوجه ، وأنتم مخالفون من كافة المسلمين سوى المعتزلة ، فما البرهان عليه ؟ ! فإن قول القائل : الكثرة إلى أنه محالة في واجب الوجود ، مع كون الذات الموصوفة واحدة ، تستحيل كثرة الصفات ، وفيه النزاع ، وليست استحالته معلومة بالضرورة ، فلا بد من البرهان • يرجع ولهم مسلكان : الأول : قولهم : إن البرهان عليه أن كل واحد من الصفة والموصوف ، إذا لم يكن هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، فإما أن يستغنى كل واحد منهما عن الآخر في وجوده ، أو يفتقر كل واحد إلى الآخر ، أو يستغنى واحد عن الآخر
Issue [6]: The philosophers agreed on the impossibility of affirming knowledge, power, and will for the First Principle, just as the Mu'tazila agreed. They claimed that these names occur in the Law and are permissible to use linguistically, but they refer to one essence, as previously. And it is not permissible to affirm attributes additional to His essence, as is permissible in our case that our knowledge and power are attributes additional to our essence. They claimed that this necessitates multiplicity, because if these attributes were to occur to us, we would know that they are additional to the essence, since they are temporally originated; and if they were supposed to be concomitant to our existence without temporal delay, they would not cease to be additional to the essence by concomitance. For any two things, if one occurs to the other and it is known that this is not that and that is not this, even if they were concomitant, they would be rationally conceived as two things. So these attributes, even if they are concomitant to the essence of the First, do not cease to be things other than the essence, thus necessitating multiplicity in the Necessary Being, which is impossible. For this reason they agreed on negating attributes. So it is said to them: By what did you know the impossibility of multiplicity from this aspect, while you are opposed by all Muslims except the Mu'tazila? So what is the proof for it? For the statement of the one who says multiplicity is impossible in the Necessary Being, while the described essence is one, and multiplicity of attributes is impossible—this is the very point of dispute, and its impossibility is not known by necessity, so a proof is required. They have two approaches: The first is their saying: The proof for it is that each one of the attribute and the described, if this is not that nor that this, then either each is independent of the other in its existence, or each depends on the other, or one is independent of the other...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument (shared by the Mu'tazila) against attributing knowledge, power, and will to God as real, additional attributes distinct from His essence. They accept these names as permissible linguistic expressions but maintain they refer to the same essence. Their core argument: if attributes were additional, they would be distinct things, causing multiplicity in God. They illustrate: when a temporal attribute occurs to us, we recognize it as additional; even if it were non-temporal but concomitant, it remains distinct. Any two things that are not identical are two things, so necessarily attributes are other than the essence, leading to multiplicity, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. Therefore, they deny real attributes. Ghazali then rebuts: this claim is not self-evident and is opposed by most Muslims, so a proof is needed. The philosophers offer two arguments; the first is presented: given attribute and essence are distinct, their existential relationship must be either mutual independence (which would make each a separate necessary being), mutual dependence (which implies imperfection), or one dependent on the other (which would make the dependent possible). The passage breaks off at the beginning of this argument.

Key Terms
صفات زائدة
كثرة
واجب الوجود
المبدأ الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny God's attributes entirely.
Correction: They affirm the names but consider them identical to the essence, not additional realities.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' main reason for denying real attributes of God?
A) Attributes are impossible to conceive.
B) Attributes would necessitate multiplicity in the Necessary Being.
C) Attributes are only applicable to created beings.
D) The Law forbids attributing anything to God.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers reject real attributes because they would compromise the absolute oneness of the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes like His knowledge or power, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'things' added to God? How does this lesson expose that subtle division in your own thinking?
?
The philosophers argue that any two non-identical things are two. In your daily worship, do you ever treat God's names as if they were multiple distinct beings? What one change can you make today to ensure your heart affirms His absolute oneness?
?
If you were asked by a friend why God's mercy is not a separate entity from God, could you explain it using the logic of this lesson? What step will you take to internalize this so deeply that your very conception of God is purified?

Ghazali's Rebuttal and the First Proof
Ghazali rebuts and philosophers give first proof based on existential relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique that the impossibility of multiplicity is not self-evident, and the philosophers' first proof based on the existential relationship between attribute and essence.

Core Idea
Ghazali demands a proof; the philosophers respond with three possible relations.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that multiplicity is impossible. He points out that this is not a self-evident truth and is disputed by most Muslims. The philosophers then present their first proof, which examines the possible existential relationships between an attribute and its subject: mutual independence, mutual dependence, or one independent of the other.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's objection
Read how Ghazali questions the philosophers: by what proof did you know multiplicity is impossible? This sets up the need for a demonstration.
Ghazali notes that the statement is not known by necessity.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The philosophers propose three logical relations between attribute and essence: each is independent, each depends on the other, or one depends on the other. Understand each possibility.
These are the three ways two distinct things can relate in existence.
3
Anticipate the refutation
Though the passage ends here, think about how each possibility might lead to a contradiction for the Necessary Being. The lesson sets the stage for that reasoning.
The full argument would show that only one possibility is acceptable, but it leads to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the philosophers' first proof, how many possible relationships exist between attribute and essence?

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any core beliefs about Allah without having examined the proof for them? What would change if you demanded a burhān for every claim you accept about Him?
?
When you hear a theological argument that contradicts your understanding, do you dismiss it because it goes against what you've been taught, or do you seek the proof as Ghazali demands here?
?
If you cannot trace your belief in any single attribute of Allah back to a clear proof from revelation or reason, are you willing to suspend judgment until you find one?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Philosophers' Argument Against Attributes
Ghazali presents the philosophers' position and his initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that affirming attributes leads to multiplicity in the Necessary Existent, and Ghazali's initial objection that this claim is not self-evident.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in God is not obviously impossible—it requires proof.

Ghazali opens this question by presenting the philosophers' consensus—shared with the Mu'tazila—that attributes like knowledge, power, and will cannot be proven for God because they would entail multiplicity. He then challenges them: this is not a self-evident truth but a disputed claim requiring a demonstration. This lesson sets the stage for the ensuing debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'اتفقت الفلاسفة' to 'على نفي الصفات'. Notice how they argue that attributes would be additional to the essence, causing multiplicity.
The philosophers equate multiplicity with impossibility in the Necessary Existent.
2
Identify Ghazali's initial objection
After presenting their view, Ghazali asks: 'By what means did you know the impossibility of multiplicity?' He points out that this is the very point of dispute and not known by necessity.
This objection is the foundation of his critique.
3
Understand the need for proof
Ghazali insists that proof (برهان) is required because the impossibility is not self-evident. This sets up the philosophers' subsequent attempt to provide a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٦] اتفقت الفلاسفة على استحالة إثبات العلم والقدرة والإرادة ، للمبدأ الأول كما اتفقت عليه المعتزلة، وزعموا أن هذه الأسامى وردت شرعاً ، ويجوز إطلاقاً لغة ، ولكن ترجع إلى ذات واحدة ، كما سبق . ولا يجوز إثبات صفات زائدة على ذاته ، كما يجوز في حقنا أن يكون علمنا وقدرتنا أوصافاً له زائدة على ذاتنا ، وزعموا أن ذلك يوجب كثرة، لأن هذه الصفات لو طرأت علينا لكنا نعلم أنها زائدة على الذات ، إذ تجددت ; ولو قدرت مقارنة لوجودنا من غير تأخير لما خرج عن كونه زائداً على الذات بالمقارنة ، فكل شيئين إذا طرأ أحدهما على الآخر ، وعلم أن هذا ليس ذاك ، وذاك ليس هذا ، فلو اقترنا أيضاً، لعقل كونهما شيئين ، فإذن لا تخرج هذه الصفات بأن تكون صفات مقارنة لذات الأول ، عن أن تكون أشياء سوى الذات، فيوجب ذلك كثرة في واجب الوجود . وهو محال ، فلهذا أجمعوا على نفى الصفات

فنقول لهم : وبم عرفتم استحالة الكثرة من هذا الوجه ، وأنتم مخالفون من كافة المسلمين سوى المعتزلة ، فما البرهان عليه ؟ ! فإن قول القائل : الكثرة أنها محالة في واجب الوجود ، مع كون الذات الموصوفة واحدة ، تستحيل كثرة الصفات ، وفيه النزاع ، وليست استحالته معلومة بالضرورة ، فلا بد من البرهان

ولهم مسلكان :

الأول : قولهم : إن البرهان عليه أن كل واحد من الصفة والموصوف ، إذا لم يكن هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، فإما أن يستغنى كل واحد منهما عن الآخر في وجوده ، أو يفتقر كل واحد إلى الآخر ، أو يستغنى واحد عن الآخر

فإن فرض كل واحد مستغنياً ، فهما واجبا وجود ، وهذه التثنية المطلقة ؛ وهو محال

وإن احتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر ، فلا يكون واحد منهما واجب الوجود ، إذ معنى واجب الوجود ، ما قوامه بذاته ، وهو مستغن من كل وجه عن غيره ، فما احتاج إلى غيره ، فذلك الغير علته ، إذ لو رفع ذلك الغير ، لامتنع وجوده ، فلا يكون وجوده من ذاته ، بل من غيره

وإن قيل : أحدهما يحتاج دون الآخر ، فالذي يحتاج معلول ، والواجب الوجود هو الآخر ، ومهما كان معلولا افتقر إلى سبب ، فيؤدى إلى أن ترتبط ذات واجب الوجود بسبب

والاعتراض : على هذا أن يقال : المختار من هذه الأقسام هو القسم الأخير . ولكن إبطالكم القسم الأول وهو التثنية المطلقة ، قد بينا أنه لا برهان لكم عليه ، في المسألة التي قبل هذه ، وأنها لا تتم إلا بالبناء على نفى الكثرة في هذه المسألة وما بعدها فها هو فرع هذه المسألة ، كيف تنبنى هذه المسألة عليه ؟ !

ولكن المختار أن يقال : الذات في قوامها غير محتاجة إلى الصفات ، والصفات محتاجة إلى الموصوف كما في حقنا

فيبقى قولهم : إن المحتاج إلى غيره لا يكون واجب الوجود .

فنقول لهم : إن أردتم بواجب الوجود أنه ليس له علة فاعلة ، فلم قلتم ذلك ؟ ! ولم استحال أن يقال : كما أن ذات واجب الوجود قديم ، ولا فاعل له ، فكذلك صفته قديمة معه ، ولا فاعل لها ؛ وإن أردتم بواجب الوجود
Question [6]: The philosophers agreed on the impossibility of proving knowledge, power, and will for the First Principle, just as the Mu'tazila agreed. They claimed that these names are transmitted by revelation and are permissible to use in language, but they refer to one essence, as previously mentioned. It is not permissible to affirm attributes additional to His essence, as is permissible in our case that our knowledge and power are attributes additional to our essence. They claimed that this entails multiplicity, because if these attributes occurred to us, we would know that they are additional to the essence, since they are new; and if they are supposed to be concomitant with our existence without delay, they would not cease to be additional to the essence due to concomitance. For any two things, if one occurs after the other, and it is known that this is not that and that is not this, even if they were also concomitant, they would be understood as two things. So these attributes, even if they are attributes concomitant with the essence of the First, do not cease to be things other than the essence; this necessitates multiplicity in the Necessary Existent. That is impossible, therefore they all agreed on the negation of attributes.

We say to them: By what means did you know the impossibility of multiplicity from this aspect, while you are opposed by all Muslims except the Mu'tazila? What is the proof for it? For the statement of the one who says: 'Multiplicity is impossible in the Necessary Existent, even though the described essence is one; the multiplicity of attributes is impossible' — that is the very point of dispute, and its impossibility is not known by necessity, so proof is needed.

They have two approaches:

The first: Their claim that the proof for it is that every one of the attribute and the subject, if this is not that and that is not this, then either each of them is independent of the other in its existence, or each is dependent on the other, or one is independent of the other.

If each is supposed independent, then both are Necessary Existents, and this is absolute duality; that is impossible.

If each is dependent on the other, then neither is a Necessary Existent, for the meaning of Necessary Existent is that whose subsistence is by itself, and it is independent from every aspect of other than itself. So what is dependent on another, that other is its cause, for if that other were removed, its existence would be impossible; so its existence is not from itself but from another.

If it is said: One of them is dependent without the other, then the dependent is an effect, and the Necessary Existent is the other. Whenever something is an effect, it requires a cause; this leads to the essence of the Necessary Existent being connected to a cause.

The objection to this is to say: The chosen one of these divisions is the last division. But your refutation of the first division, which is absolute duality, we have shown that you have no proof for it in the preceding question, and that it is only completed by building upon the negation of multiplicity in this question and what follows. So this is a branch of this question; how can this question build upon it? Rather, the chosen view is to say: The essence in its subsistence is not in need of attributes, and attributes are in need of the subject, as in our case.

Then their statement remains: 'That which is in need of another is not a Necessary Existent.'

We say to them: If you mean by Necessary Existent that which has no efficient cause, why did you say that? Why is it impossible to say: Just as the essence of the Necessary Existent is eternal and has no agent, so also His attribute is eternal with Him and has no agent? And if you mean by Necessary Existent...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ghazali of the philosophers' (falasifa) argument against affirming attributes (sifat) for God. The philosophers, agreeing with the Mu'tazila, assert that knowledge, power, and will cannot be proven for the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) because affirming attributes additional to the essence would entail multiplicity (kathra) in the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud), which is impossible. They argue that if attributes were additional, they would either be temporal (tari'a) or concomitant (muqarina), but in either case they would be distinct from the essence, implying two distinct things and thus multiplicity. Ghazali begins by objecting that this claim is not self-evident and requires proof. He then presents the philosophers' first proof (al-maslak al-awwal), which is a trilemma: attribute and subject are either (1) both independent, (2) both dependent, or (3) one dependent on the other. The philosophers argue (1) leads to two necessary existents (absolute duality), (2) makes neither necessary, and (3) makes the dependent an effect requiring a cause, which would connect the Necessary Existent's essence to a cause—all impossible. Ghazali's objection (al-i'tirad) is that the philosophers' refutation of the first division (absolute duality) lacks proof and presupposes the very multiplicity they aim to disprove, making their argument circular. He then proposes the correct view: the essence is independent of attributes, but attributes depend on the essence, analogous to our own case. Finally, he challenges the philosophers' definition of 'Necessary Existent'—if they mean 'that which has no efficient cause,' why cannot attributes be eternal and uncaused like the essence? The passage ends mid-argument on page 173, with a conditional: 'if you mean by Necessary Existent...' likely continues on the next page.

واجب الوجود
كثرة
برهان

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the philosophers' claim that multiplicity is impossible not sufficient?
A) Because the philosophers themselves disagree about it.
B) Because it is not known by necessity and requires proof.
C) Because the Mu'tazila already proved it.
D) Because attributes are never really additional.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali insists that the impossibility of multiplicity in God is not a self-evident truth but a disputed claim that requires a rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief about God that you hold simply because it 'feels obvious'—something you have never subjected to rational scrutiny? Will you dare to examine it now with the same seriousness Ghazali demands?
?
The philosophers thought their argument was self-evident, but Ghazali challenged them for proof. In your own faith, have you ever assumed something was true just because it was common among your community? Are you willing to question that assumption and seek a real proof?
?
When you are faced with a difficult theological question, does your heart naturally resist asking for proof, fearing what you might find? What would it mean for your relationship with God if you committed to seeking truth even when it disturbs your comfort?

The Philosophers' Trilemma
The philosophers' first proof: a trilemma on attribute and subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the trilemma the philosophers use to prove that attributes cannot be additional to God's essence.

Core Idea
For any two distinct things, they must be independent, mutually dependent, or one dependent on the other—all problematic for God.

The philosophers now present their first proof: a logical trilemma. They argue that if attribute and subject are two distinct things, they must fall into one of three categories, each of which contradicts the concept of the Necessary Existent. This lesson unpacks their argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
Read the passage and note the three possibilities: both independent, both dependent, or one dependent on the other.
These are exhaustive logical divisions for any two distinct things.
2
Understand the refutation of each
For each division, the philosophers argue why it is impossible for the Necessary Existent: (1) leads to two necessary existents (duality), (2) makes neither necessary, (3) makes the dependent an effect requiring a cause.
They conclude that all possibilities are impossible, so attribute and subject cannot be distinct.
3
See the conclusion
The conclusion is that attributes cannot be additional to God's essence; they must be identical with it.
This is the philosophers' intended result.

Source Text

Question [6]: The philosophers agreed on the impossibility of proving knowledge, power, and will for the First Principle, just as the Mu'tazila agreed. They claimed that these names are transmitted by revelation and are permissible to use in language, but they refer to one essence, as previously mentioned. It is not permissible to affirm attributes additional to His essence, as is permissible in our case that our knowledge and power are attributes additional to our essence. They claimed that this entails multiplicity, because if these attributes occurred to us, we would know that they are additional to the essence, since they are new; and if they are supposed to be concomitant with our existence without delay, they would not cease to be additional to the essence due to concomitance. For any two things, if one occurs after the other, and it is known that this is not that and that is not this, even if they were also concomitant, they would be understood as two things. So these attributes, even if they are attributes concomitant with the essence of the First, do not cease to be things other than the essence; this necessitates multiplicity in the Necessary Existent. That is impossible, therefore they all agreed on the negation of attributes.

We say to them: By what means did you know the impossibility of multiplicity from this aspect, while you are opposed by all Muslims except the Mu'tazila? What is the proof for it? For the statement of the one who says: 'Multiplicity is impossible in the Necessary Existent, even though the described essence is one; the multiplicity of attributes is impossible' — that is the very point of dispute, and its impossibility is not known by necessity, so proof is needed.

The first: Their claim that the proof for it is that every one of the attribute and the subject, if this is not that and that is not this, then either each of them is independent of the other in its existence, or each is dependent on the other, or one is independent of the other.

If each is supposed independent, then both are Necessary Existents, and this is absolute duality; that is impossible.

If each is dependent on the other, then neither is a Necessary Existent, for the meaning of Necessary Existent is that whose subsistence is by itself, and it is independent from every aspect of other than itself. So what is dependent on another, that other is its cause, for if that other were removed, its existence would be impossible; so its existence is not from itself but from another.

If it is said: One of them is dependent without the other, then the dependent is an effect, and the Necessary Existent is the other. Whenever something is an effect, it requires a cause; this leads to the essence of the Necessary Existent being connected to a cause.

The objection to this is to say: The chosen one of these divisions is the last division. But your refutation of the first division, which is absolute duality, we have shown that you have no proof for it in the preceding question, and that it is only completed by building upon the negation of multiplicity in this question and what follows. So this is a branch of this question; how can this question build upon it? Rather, the chosen view is to say: The essence in its subsistence is not in need of attributes, and attributes are in need of the subject, as in our case.

Then their statement remains: 'That which is in need of another is not a Necessary Existent.'

We say to them: If you mean by Necessary Existent that which has no efficient cause, why did you say that? Why is it impossible to say: Just as the essence of the Necessary Existent is eternal and has no agent, so also His attribute is eternal with Him and has no agent?
