Knowledge of Multiple Things Implies Multiplicity
Ghazali argues that knowledge of multiple things requires multiple acts of knowledge, using father-son example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge of multiple objects necessarily involves multiplicity, and why this challenges the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is one and simple.

Core Idea
Knowing many things cannot be a single act—multiplicity in objects implies multiplicity in knowledge.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts the philosophical view that God's knowledge of all things is one simple essence. He argues that knowledge of multiple distinct objects requires multiple distinct acts of knowledge. Using the example of knowing a relation like father and son, he shows that even relational knowledge involves three separate knowledges. This directly challenges the claim that God's knowledge is unitary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Read the first sentence: existence (wujud) is added to essence and is other than it, so it necessitates multiplicity. This is the foundation of the argument.
Notice that Ghazali starts with a broader metaphysical principle.
2
Examine the father-son example
Ghazali illustrates with knowledge of a relation: father and son. He identifies three distinct knowledges: the essence of the son, the essence of the father, and the relation itself.
Even though the relation depends on the relata, it is a separate knowledge.
3
Apply the argument to the First (God)
If God knows Himself as the principle (mabda') of all things, He must know His own essence, the individuals of all genera and species, and the relation of principality. This results in multiplicity.
Thus, God's knowledge cannot be a single, simple act if it encompasses many objects.
4
Recognize the implications
This argument undermines the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is one and simple. Ghazali forces them to accept multiplicity in the divine knowledge or reject their premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الوجود مضاف إلى الحقيقة وهو غيره ، فيقتضى كثرة . فعلى هذا الوجه لا يمكن تقدير علم يتعلق بمعلومات كثيرة ، إلا يلزم فيه نوع كثرة أجل وأبلغ من اللازم فى تقدير وجود مضاف إلى ماهية . وأما العلم بالابن ، وكذا سائر المضافات ، ففيه كثرة ، إذ لابد من العلم بذات الابن ، وذات الأب ، وهما علمان ؛ وعلم ثالث وهو الإضافة . نعم هذا الثالث مضمن بالعلمين السابقين ، إذ هما من شروطه وضرورته ، وإلا فما لم يعلم المضاف أولا ، لا تعلم الإضافة ، فهى علوم متعددة ، بعضها مشروط بالبعض فكذلك إذا علم الأول ذاته مضافة إلى سائر الأجناس والأنواع ، لكونه مبدأ لها ، افتقر إلى أن يعلم ذاته ، وآحاد الأجناس ، وأن يعلم إضافة نفسه بالمبدئية إليها ، وإلا لم يعقل كون الإضافة معلومة له
Existence is added to the essence and is other than it, so it necessitates multiplicity. So in this manner it is not possible to conceive knowledge that relates to many known things without there being entailed in it a type of multiplicity greater and more extensive than that required in conceiving existence added to a quiddity. As for knowledge of the son, and similarly all relations, there is multiplicity in it, for there must be knowledge of the essence of the son and the essence of the father — these are two items of knowledge — and a third knowledge which is the relation. Yes, this third is implied in the two previous knowledges, for they are its conditions and necessaries; otherwise, if the related thing is not known first, the relation cannot be known. So they are multiple sciences, some conditional upon others. Likewise, if the First knows His own essence as related to all genera and species, since He is their principle, He would need to know His essence, the individuals of the genera, and to know the relation of His own principality to them; otherwise the relation being known to Him is not intelligible.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the philosophers' view that God's knowledge of many things can be one simple knowledge. Ghazali begins by establishing a general principle: existence (wujud) is an attribute added to essence (haqiqa) and is other than it, so it entails multiplicity. He then applies this to knowledge: if knowledge relates to many objects, it must entail multiplicity. He illustrates with the example of knowing a relation like father and son: one must know the essence of the son, the essence of the father (two knowledges), and the relation itself (a third knowledge). Although the third knowledge is implied by the first two, they are distinct. Similarly, if God (the First) knows Himself as the principle (mabda') of all things, He must know His own essence, the essences of all things, and the relation of principality. This would necessitate multiplicity in God's knowledge, which contradicts the philosophers' claim that His knowledge is one and simple. Ghazali follows the author's order: first the general principle, then the relational example, then the application to the First.

Key Terms
وجود
حقيقة
ماهية
إضافة
علم
معلوم
مبدئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing a relation can be reduced to knowing the relata alone, so that relational knowledge does not add multiplicity.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the relation is a third distinct knowledge, even though it depends on the prior knowledges of the relata. Thus, relational knowledge inherently introduces multiplicity.

Checkpoint

B) Two: knowledge of the son and the father
C) Three: knowledge of the son, the father, and the relation
D) Four: knowledge of the son, the father, the relation, and one's own knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that relational knowledge, such as knowing God as Creator, requires multiple distinct acts of knowledge, undermining the claim that God's knowledge is simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your relationship with Allah as your Creator, do you treat that as a simple fact or do you recognize the profound multiplicity of what He knows about you? How does this understanding of multiple knowledges affect your awareness of being known?
?
If the mere act of knowing a relation like 'son of Zayd' requires three distinct cognitions, how much more must Allah's knowledge of your entire life story be infinitely rich? Does this make you feel more accountable knowing that each detail is distinct in His knowledge?
?
In your daily life, you are a 'relational being'—a child, a parent, a spouse. How does Ghazali's argument that relational knowledge introduces multiplicity challenge you to see each relationship as a unique object of Allah's knowledge? Will you live today with greater consciousness that every relation you have is specifically known by Him?

Knowing That One Knows Requires Another Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the claim that knowing something implies knowing that one knows by the same knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge of an object and knowledge of that knowledge are distinct acts, and why this does not lead to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowing that you know is a separate act of knowledge, and it stops where attention stops.

The philosophers argued that if you know something, you automatically know that you know it by the same single knowledge. This would preserve simplicity. Ghazali refutes this claim, arguing that knowing one's own knowledge is a separate act of knowledge. He then explains how this chain of knowledges terminates without infinite regress, using the example of knowing blackness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of Ghazali's refutational style.
Notice the structure: opposing view first, then denial, then explanation.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find where Ghazali states what the philosophers say: that knowing something implies knowing that one knows by the same knowledge.
This is the view he will refute.
3
Understand Ghazali's denial and alternative
Ghazali says 'it is not so' and explains that knowing one's knowledge requires another knowledge. Knowledge has multiple objects.
This is the core of his refutation.
4
Follow the example of blackness
Ghazali gives an example: one who knows blackness may be absorbed in the object and heedless of his own knowledge. If he pays attention to his knowledge, another knowledge is needed, but it stops when attention stops.
This illustrates how the chain terminates.
5
Reflect on the prevention of infinite regress
Ghazali explicitly denies infinite regress: the chain stops at a knowledge the knower is unaware of. This is the key point against the philosophers' objection.
Consider why this is important for theology and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : إن من علم شيئاً ، علم كونه عالماً ، بذلك العلم نفسه ، فيكون واحداً . فليس كذلك ، بل يعلم كونه عالماً بعلم آخر والعلم متعدداً المعلوم وينتهى إلى علم يغفل عنه ، ولا يعلمه ، ولا نقول : يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية ، بل ينقطع على علم متعلق بمعلومه ، وهو غافل عن وجود العلم المعلوم ، كالذى يعلم السواد ، وهو في حال علمه مستغرق النفس بمعلومه الذي هو سواد ، وغافل عن علمه بالسواد ، وليس ملتفتاً إليه ، فإن التفت إليه ، افتقر إلى علم آخر ، إلى أن ينقطع التفاته
As for their statement: 'Whoever knows something knows that he is knowing by that same knowledge, so it is one' — it is not so. Rather, he knows that he is knowing by another knowledge. Knowledge has multiple objects and terminates in a knowledge of which he is unaware and does not know it. And we do not say it goes on to infinity, but it stops at a knowledge related to its object, while he is heedless of the existence of the known knowledge; like one who knows blackness, and while knowing he is absorbed in his object which is blackness, and heedless of his knowledge of blackness, not paying attention to it. If he pays attention to it, he needs another knowledge, until his attention stops.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that knowledge of an object and knowledge of that knowledge are identical, thereby preserving simplicity. Ghazali denies this: knowing that one knows (knowing one's own knowledge) is a separate act of knowledge. He explains that knowledge has multiple objects: the object known and the knowledge itself. This chain of knowledges does not regress infinitely; it terminates in a knowledge of which the knower is unaware. He gives the example of knowing blackness: when one is fully absorbed in the perception of blackness, one is not simultaneously reflecting on one's own act of knowing it. The moment one attends to that knowledge, a new act of knowledge is required, and this attention can stop. Thus the philosophers' claim that one simple knowledge suffices for both the object and the awareness of knowing is false. Ghazali follows the order: first states the opposing view, then refutes it, then explains the alternative, then provides an illustration.

Key Terms
علم
غفلة
سواد
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that self-awareness of knowledge is inherent in the act of knowing, so knowledge is one.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes them as separate knowledges; knowing the object and knowing that one knows are different acts.

Checkpoint

A) He immediately knows blackness and his knowledge of it in one act
B) He needs another knowledge, and this can continue indefinitely
C) He needs another knowledge, but the process can stop when attention stops
D) He becomes unaware of blackness

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that knowing one's own knowledge is distinct from knowing the object, and this chain terminates in heedlessness, not infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'I know that Allah is One,' do you also know that you know it? Or are you simply absorbed in the words, heedless of your own act of knowing? What would it take for you to turn your attention to your own knowledge of Him, and would that not require yet another knowledge? Does this leave you in a state of humility, realizing your ignorance of your own certainty?
?
Think of a time you were utterly absorbed in studying a topic—perhaps a verse of the Qur'an or a proof of God's existence. Were you simultaneously aware of your own knowledge? Did that awareness ever cause you to doubt or question your grasp? If you never attended to that second knowledge, does that mean you were heedless? And if you are heedless of your own faith, what does that say about the depth of your conviction?
?
Ghazali says the chain of knowledges stops when you are heedless. Is your relationship with Allah characterized by heedless absorption in the world, or by an active awareness of your own knowledge of Him? What one concrete change could you make today to stop being absorbed in the objects of your life and instead attend to the knowledge you have of your Creator—even if that attention requires yet another step? Are you willing to take that step?

Ghazali's Refutation of Divine Self-Knowledge Only
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that the First knows only itself as disgraceful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophical view that God knows only Himself to be disgraceful and how he refutes it using comparisons to created beings.

Core Idea
If even animals know other things, a God who knows only Himself would be deficient.

In this passage, Ghazali takes on a specific philosophical position held by some 'verified' philosophers: that the First (God) knows only Himself, not His creation. He immediately calls this doctrine disgraceful and proceeds to demonstrate why. By comparing God to angels, humans, and even beasts, he shows that the view leads to an absurd conclusion: that God is inferior to His own effects. This lesson unpacks his argument and its rhetorical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the objection and Ghazali's sharp response.
The Arabic ends abruptly; focus on the complete refutation.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that Ghazali distinguishes between Ibn Sina's view (God knows other things) and the 'verified' philosophers' view (God knows only Himself). He attacks the latter.
3
Analyze Ghazali's comparative argument
Examine how Ghazali uses comparisons to angels, humans, and even beasts to show that the view implies deficiency. Then consider his rhetorical question about beauty.
This is the core of his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الإشكال إنما يلزم « ابن سينا » حيث زعم أن الأول يعلم غيره ، فأما المحققون من الفلاسفة ، فقد اتفقوا على أنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، فيندفع هذا الإشكال فنقول : ناهيكم خزياً بهذا المذهب ، ولولا أنه في غاية الركاكة ، لما استنكف المتأخرون عن نصرته ؛ ونحن ننبه على وجه الخزى فيه ، فإن فيه تفضيل معلولاته عليه ، إذ الملك والإنسان ، وكل واحد من العقلاء ، يعرف نفسه ومبدأه ، ويعرف غيره ؛ والأول لا يعرف إلا نفسه ، فهو ناقص بالإضافة إلى آحاد الناس ، فضلاً عن الملائكة ، بل البهيمة مع شعورها بنفسها ، تعرف أموراً أخر سواها ، ولا شك فى أن العلم شرف ، وأن عدمه نقصان ، فأين قولهم إنه عاشق ومعشوق ، لأن له البهاء الأكمل ، والجمال الأتم ، وأى جمال لوجود بسيط ، لا ما هية له ولا حقيقة ، ولا خبر له بما يجرى فى العالم ، ولا بما يلزم
If it is said: 'This problem only applies to Ibn Sina, who claimed that the First knows other than Himself. As for the verified among the philosophers, they agreed that He knows only Himself, so this problem is repelled.' We say: Sufficient for you is disgrace in this doctrine. Had it not been extremely flimsy, the later ones would not have been ashamed to support it. We shall point out the aspect of disgrace in it. For it entails preferring His effects over Him, since the angel, the human, and every rational being knows himself and his principle and knows other than himself; while the First knows only Himself. So He is deficient in comparison to individual people, let alone angels. Rather, even the beast, with its self-awareness, knows things other than itself. And there is no doubt that knowledge is an honor and its absence is a deficiency. So where is their claim that He is lover and beloved because He has perfect splendor and complete beauty? And what beauty is there for a simple existence that has no quiddity and no reality, and has no news of what happens in the world, nor of what follows?
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a fierce refutation of the view held by some philosophers (the 'verified' ones, in contrast to Ibn Sina) that God knows only Himself, not other things. Ghazali calls this doctrine disgraceful (khizyun). He first states the objection: the problem of multiplicity does not apply if God knows only Himself. He then replies sarcastically, saying this position is so weak that later philosophers were ashamed to embrace it. He identifies the disgrace: it makes God inferior to His effects. Angels, humans, and even animals know themselves and other things, whereas according to this view God knows only Himself, implying deficiency. Knowledge is an honor; its absence is a lack. Ghazali then attacks their description of God as 'lover and beloved' with perfect beauty, asking: what beauty is there in a simple existence with no quiddity, no reality, and no awareness of the world? The argument follows the structure: objection, Ghazali's response stating disgrace, then supporting reasoning with comparisons to humans and beasts, and finally a rhetorical question challenging their notion of divine beauty.

Key Terms
ابن سينا
محققون
خزى
معلولات
عاشق
معشوق
بهاء
جمال
وجود بسيط
لا ماهية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is arguing that God must know all details to be greater than humans; he is refuting a specific philosophical claim about divine simplicity.
Correction: Ghazali is not demanding that God know every trivial detail; he is refuting the claim that God knows only Himself, which would imply He knows less than animals.

Checkpoint

B) It makes God superior to angels and humans
C) It implies God is deficient because even animals know other things
D) It is consistent with God being the lover and beloved

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the view that God knows only Himself by arguing it implies God is less knowledgeable than animals, contradicting His perfection.

?
Knowledge is honor; its absence is deficiency. When you consider your own relationship with God, do you seek to be known by Him, or do you hide from His knowledge? ?
Ghazali calls the idea of a God who knows only Himself 'disgraceful' because it makes Him less than a beast. But have you ever, in your own mind, reduced God to a distant, unknowing force? Where does that thought come from, and how does this lesson confront it? What is one concrete way you will remind yourself today that God is fully aware of you and your world?

Ghazali's Dilemma on Divine Self-Knowledge
Ghazali argues against identifying self-knowledge with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers cannot escape multiplicity by claiming God's self-knowledge is identical to His essence, and why the analogy with human self-awareness undermines that claim.

Core Idea
Claiming God's self-knowledge is identical to His essence leads to an absurd analogy with human self-awareness.

Ghazali here tackles a key philosophical claim: that God's knowledge of Himself is exactly the same as His essence, thereby avoiding multiplicity. He presents a dilemma and uses a sharp analogy with human self-awareness to expose the logical flaw. The passage demonstrates how careful reasoning about attributes and essence prevents unwarranted theological conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the condensed style; each sentence carries a logical step.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find where Ghazali poses the question: Is God's knowledge of His essence identical to His essence or other? See how he uses this to trap the philosophers.
The dilemma forces either multiplicity (if other) or absurdity (if identical).
3
Understand the human analogy
Examine why Ghazali says claiming identity is like saying a human's self-knowledge is identical to his essence. Note the absurdity: a human can be unaware of himself, proving awareness is distinct.
This is the core of the reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
See the philosophers' objection that human self-knowledge is absent then occurs, proving otherness. Then study Ghazali's reply: otherness is not defined by temporal occurrence or conjunction.
Ghazali refines the concept of 'otherness'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final point: the eternity of God's self-knowledge does not prove identity. The imagination can still conceive essence and awareness separately.
This closes the argument against identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهؤلاء لم تتخلصوا من الكثرة ، مع اقتحام هذه المخازى أيضاً ، فإنا نقول : علمه بذاته ، عين ذاته أو غير ذاته ؟ ! فإن قلتم : إنه غير ذاته ، فقد جاءت الكثرة ، وإن قلتم : إنه عين ذاته ، فما الفصل بينكم وبين قائل : إن علم الإنسان بذاته عين ذاته ، وهذه حماقة ، إذ يعقل وجود ذاته في حالة ، هو فيها غافل عن ذاته ، ثم تزول غفلته ، ويتنبه لذاته ، فيكون شعوره بذاته غير ذاته لا محالة . فإن قلتم : إن الإنسان قد يخلو عن العلم بذاته ، فيطرأ عليه ، فيكون غيره لا محالة . فنقول : الغيرية لا تعرف بالطريان والمقارنة ، فإن عين الشيء لا يجوز أن يطرأ على الشيء ، وغير الشيء إذا قارن الشيء ، لم يصر هو هو ، ولم يخرج عن كونه غيراً ، فبأن كان الأول لم يزل عالماً بذاته لا يدل على أن علمه بذاته عين ذاته ، ويتسع الوهم لتقدير الذات ، ثم طريان الشعور ، ولو كان الذات هو بعينه لما تصور التوهم .
Then it is said to them: You have not escaped multiplicity, while also plunging into these disgraces. For we say: His knowledge of His essence—is it identical to His essence or other than His essence? If you say it is other than His essence, then multiplicity has occurred. If you say it is identical to His essence, then what distinguishes you from one who says that a human's knowledge of his self is identical to his self? This is foolishness, because his self can be conceived to exist in a state where he is unaware of his self, then his unawareness ceases and he becomes attentive to his self, so his awareness of his self is necessarily other than his self. If you say: A human can be devoid of knowledge of his self, then it occurs to him, so it is clearly something other than him. We say: Otherness is not known by temporal occurrence and conjunction. For the very essence of a thing cannot occur to the thing, and something other than the thing, when it conjoins with the thing, does not become identical to it and does not cease to be other. So the fact that the First has always been knowing His essence does not prove that His knowledge of His essence is identical to His essence. The imagination can conceive of the essence then the occurrence of awareness; if the essence were identical to awareness, imagination would not conceive this.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument against the philosophers' attempt to avoid multiplicity. Ghazali presents a dilemma: either God's knowledge of Himself is identical to His essence or distinct. If distinct, multiplicity arises (which they wanted to avoid). If identical, then it becomes analogous to claiming that human self-knowledge is identical to the human essence—which is absurd, because a human can be in a state of unawareness and then become aware, proving that the awareness is an accident distinct from the essence. The philosophers object that human self-knowledge can be absent and then occur, so it is clearly other than the essence. Ghazali responds that otherness is not determined by temporal occurrence or conjunction; the essence cannot 'occur' to itself, and if something other conjoins with it, it does not become identical. Therefore, the fact that God's self-knowledge is eternal does not prove identity. The imagination can still distinguish the essence from awareness, showing they are not identical.

Key Terms
الكثرة (multiplicity)
علمه بذاته (His knowledge of His essence)
عين ذاته (identical to His essence)
غير ذاته (other than His essence)
الطريان (temporal occurrence)
المقارنة (conjunction)
الغيرية (otherness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal self-knowledge in God necessarily implies that His knowledge of Himself is identical to His essence.
Correction: Ghazali argues that eternalness does not establish identity; logical analysis shows that essence and awareness are conceptually distinct, as demonstrated by the human analogy and the refined notion of otherness.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it absurd to say that God's knowledge of Himself is identical to His essence?
A) It implies multiplicity in God.
B) It is analogous to saying human self-knowledge is identical to the human essence, yet humans can be unaware of themselves.
C) It denies God's simplicity.
D) It contradicts Aristotelian logic.

Key Takeaway
Eternal self-knowledge does not prove identity with the essence; logical analysis shows they are conceptually distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's self-awareness, do you unconsciously picture it as similar to your own self-awareness? How does Ghazali's argument force you to purify that concept?
?
If attributing knowledge to God as a real distinction from His essence is necessary to avoid absurdity, what does that demand of your heart when you say 'Allah knows'? Are you truly affirming an attribute without reducing it to a human analogy?
?
This lesson shows that even your own self-awareness is not identical to your essence. Does this humble your confidence in understanding God? Will you repent of any intellectual pride that claimed to grasp the Unseen through mere analogy?

Refuting the Identity of God's Essence and Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God's essence is intellect and knowledge without a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God's essence is identical to His knowledge, by distinguishing between attributes and their substrates.

Core Idea
Attributes require a subject; God's knowledge is an attribute, not His essence.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a philosophical claim that seems to preserve God's simplicity: that His essence is itself intellect and knowledge. The philosophers argue this avoids positing multiple attributes. Ghazali, however, sees a fundamental error: they treat knowledge as if it could subsist without a subject. Using analogies from physical properties, he shows the absurdity of the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the objection and Ghazali's response.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We say...'
2
Understand the key concept
Focus on Ghazali's assertion that knowledge is an attribute and an accident that requires a subject (موصوف).
This is a fundamental principle of kalam.
3
Examine the analogies
Ghazali compares the claim to saying blackness, whiteness, squareness subsist by themselves. How do these analogies support his point?
Think about everyday objects: can blackness exist without something black?
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this matters: if God's essence were knowledge, then knowledge would not be a distinct attribute. This impacts how we understand God's other attributes.
This debate shapes the Sunni doctrine of eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ذاته عقل وعلم ، فليس له ذات ثم علم قائم بها . قلنا : الحماقة ظاهرة فى هذا الكلام ، فإن العلم صفة وعرض يستدعى موصوفاً . وقول القائل : هو فى ذاته عقل وعلم ، كقوله : هو قدرة وإرادة ، وهو قائم بنفسه ولو قيل به ؛ فهو كقول القائل في سواد وبياض : إنه قائم بنفسه ، وفى كمية وتربيع وتثليث ، إنه قائمة بنفسه ، وكذا في كل الأعراض ، وبالطريق التى يستحيل أن تقوم صفات الأجسام بنفسها دون جسم هو غير الصفات ،
If it is said: His essence is intellect and knowledge, so there is no essence with knowledge subsisting in it. We say: Foolishness is apparent in this statement. For knowledge is an attribute and an accident that requires a subject. The statement 'He is in His essence intellect and knowledge' is like saying 'He is power and will' and that He subsists by Himself, even if that were said. It is like one saying about blackness and whiteness that they subsist by themselves, and about quantity, squareness, and triangularity that they subsist by themselves, and likewise in all accidents. And in the way in which it is impossible for the attributes of bodies to subsist by themselves without a body that is other than the attributes.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The philosophers object that God's essence is itself intellect and knowledge, so there is no separate essence with knowledge subsisting in it. Ghazali responds that this is foolish because knowledge is an attribute and an accident that requires a subject (a thing that possesses it). Claiming that God is 'in His essence intellect and knowledge' is like claiming that power and will subsist by themselves, or that blackness and whiteness subsist by themselves without a body. Similarly, quantities like squareness and triangularity cannot subsist without a subject. The point is that all accidents require a substrate. Therefore, God's knowledge cannot be identical to His essence; rather, knowledge is an attribute that inheres in the essence. Ghazali uses the analogy of bodily attributes to show that such a claim is absurd.

موصوف

تثليث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's simplicity implies no distinction between essence and attributes.
Correction: Ghazali shows that attributes are accidents requiring a subject, so knowledge must be distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint

A) The analogy of fire being hot.
B) The analogy of blackness and whiteness subsisting by themselves without a body.
C) The analogy of a triangle having three sides.
D) The analogy of the soul being self-knowing.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the identification of God's essence with knowledge by showing that knowledge, being an accident, requires a subject distinct from itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Absurdity of the Philosophers' View on Divine Knowledge
Ghazali begins with astonishment at the philosophers' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophers' claim that God knows only Himself to be scandalously absurd.

Core Idea
Denying God's knowledge of the world reduces Him below a dead person.

Ghazali opens his refutation with a tone of astonishment, exposing the sheer absurdity of the philosophers' position. He argues that to deny God knowledge of His creation is to strip Him of any meaningful awareness, leaving Him no better than the inanimate. This lesson highlights the first and most glaring consequence of their claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening shock
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting Ghazali's incredulous tone. He asks how ignorance can proceed from God.
Notice the rhetorical questions: they are meant to provoke outrage.
2
Identify the central comparison
Find where Ghazali compares God to a dead person. Understand why this is such a powerful argument.
The only difference they allow is self-knowledge – which is a minimal perfection.
3
Grasp the self-defeating nature
Reflect on how the philosophers' own intellectual claims lead to an absurd conclusion. They claim depth but end up demeaning God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جهله مع ذاته، ويصدر عنه؟! وأى نقصان فى عالم الله تعالى يزيد على هذا؟! وليتعجب العاقل من طائفة، يتعمقون في المعقولات بزعمهم، ثم ينتهى آخر نظرهم، إلى أن رب الأرباب، ومسبب الأسباب، لا علم له أصلا بما يجرى فى العالم، وأى فرق بينه وبين الميت - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيراً - إلا في علمه بنفسه؟! وأى كمال في علمه بنفسه، بغيره، وهذا مذهب تغنى صورته فى الافتضاح عن الإطناب والإيضاح. ثم يقال لهؤلاء لم تتخلصوا من الكثرة، مع اقتحام هذه المخازى أيضاً، فإنا نقول: علمه بذاته، عين ذاته أو غير ذاته؟! فإن قلتم: إنه غير ذاته، فقد جاءت الكثرة، وإن قلتم: إنه عين ذاته، فما الفصل بينكم وبين قائل: إن علم الإنسان بذاته عين ذاته، وهذه حماقة، إذ يعقل وجود ذاته في حالة، هو فيها غافل عن ذاته، ثم تزول غفلته، ويتنبه لذاته، فيكون شعوره بذاته غير ذاته لا محالة. فإن قلتم: إن الإنسان قد يخلو عن العلم بذاته، فيطرأ عليه، فيكون غيره لا محالة. فنقول: الغيرية لا تعرف بالطريان والمقارنة، فإن عين الشيء لا يجوز أن يطرأ على الشيء، وغير الشيء إذا قارن الشيء، لم يصر هو هو، ولم يخرج عن كونه غيراً، فبأن كان الأول لم يزل عالماً بذاته لا يدل على أن علمه بذاته عين ذاته، ويتسع الوهم لتقدير الذات، ثم طريان الشعور، ولو كان الذات هو بعينه هذا لما تصور التوهم. فإن قيل: ذاته عقل وعلم، فليس له ذات ثم علم قائم بها. قلنا: الحماقة ظاهرة فى هذا الكلام، فإن العلم صفة وعرض يستدعى موصوفاً. وقول القائل: هو فى ذاته عقل وعلم، كقوله: هو قدرة وإرادة، وهو قائم بنفسه ولو قيل به؛ فهو كقول القائل في سواد وبياض: إنه قائم بنفسه، وفى كمية وتربيع وتثليث، إنه قائمة بنفسه، وكذا في كل الأعراض، وبالطريق التى يستحيل أن تقوم صفات الأجسام بنفسها دون جسم هو غير الصفات، بعين ذلك الطريق يعلم أن صفات الأحياء، من العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة أيضاً، لا تقوم بنفسها، وإنما تقوم بذات، فالحياة تقوم بالذات، فتكون حياته بها، وكذلك سائر الصفات، فإذن لم يقنعوا بسلب الأول سائر الصفات، ولا بسلبه الحقيقة والماهية، حتى سلبوه أيضاً القيام بنفسه، وردوه إلى حقائق الأعراض والصفات التي لا قوام لها بنفسها. على أنا سنبين بعد هذا عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل على كونه سبحانه عالماً بنفسه وبغيره، في مسألة مفردة، إن شاء الله تعالى.
His ignorance with His essence, and it proceeds from Him?! And what deficiency in the world of God Almighty exceeds this?! And let the intelligent person marvel at a group who delve deeply into intellectual matters by their claim, then the end of their investigation concludes that the Lord of lords, and the Causer of causes, has no knowledge at all of what occurs in the world. And what difference is there between Him and a dead person - exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, greatly - except in His knowledge of Himself?! And what perfection is there in His knowledge of Himself, but not of others? This is a school of thought whose form suffices in disgrace, without elaboration and clarification. Then it is said to these people: You have not escaped multiplicity, while also plunging into these disgraces. For we say: His knowledge of His essence: is it identical with His essence or other than His essence? If you say that it is other than His essence, then multiplicity has come. And if you say that it is identical with His essence, then what is the distinction between you and the one who says that man's knowledge of his essence is identical with his essence? And this is foolishness, for it is conceivable that his essence exists in a state in which he is heedless of his essence, then his heedlessness ceases and he becomes aware of his essence, so his awareness of his essence is necessarily other than his essence. For if you say that man may be devoid of knowledge of his essence, then it occurs to him, so it is necessarily other than him. We say: Otherness is not known by temporal occurrence and conjunction. For the identity of a thing cannot occur to a thing, and if something other than a thing is conjoined with the thing, it does not become identical with it, nor does it cease to be other. That He, may He be exalted, has always been knowing of His essence does not indicate that His knowledge of His essence is identical with His essence. And the imagination can conceive the essence, then the occurrence of awareness. And if the essence were identical with this, the imagination would not be possible. If it is said: His essence is intellect and knowledge, so He does not have an essence and then knowledge subsisting in it. We say: The foolishness is apparent in this statement, for knowledge is an attribute and an accident that requires a subject. And the statement of one who says: He is in His essence intellect and knowledge, is like His saying: He is power and will, and He is self-subsisting even if it is said of Him; so it is like the statement of one who says of blackness and whiteness: It is self-subsisting, and of quantity, squaring, and cubing: It is self-subsisting; and likewise for all accidents. And by the same way that it is impossible for the attributes of bodies to subsist by themselves without a body that is other than the attributes, by that same way it is known that the attributes of living beings, such as knowledge, life, power, and will also do not subsist by themselves, but rather subsist by a subject. So life subsists by the subject, so His life is through it, and likewise the rest of the attributes. Thus they were not content to strip the First of the rest of the attributes, nor to strip Him of reality and quiddity, until they also stripped Him of self-subsistence and reduced Him to the realities of accidents and attributes that have no subsistence by themselves. Moreover, we will show after this their inability to establish the proof for His being, may He be exalted, knowing of Himself and of others, in a separate question, God willing.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God knows only His own essence and that His knowledge is identical with His essence, leading to the conclusion that He has no knowledge of particulars. Ghazali begins by expressing astonishment at such a view, which implies deficiency in God's knowledge compared to a dead person who knows nothing. He then raises a dilemma: if God's knowledge of His essence is other than His essence, then multiplicity enters (contradicting divine simplicity). If it is identical with His essence, then how is this different from a human who, when unaware of himself, later becomes aware—showing that awareness is other than essence? The philosophers might object that man's consciousness is subject to change, but God's is eternal. Ghazali replies that otherness is not determined by temporal occurrence or conjunction; even if God's knowledge is eternal, it does not prove identity. He then addresses the philosophers' claim that God's essence itself is intellect and knowledge, so no additional attribute is needed. Ghazali calls this foolish: knowledge is an attribute that requires a subject. Saying that essence is knowledge is like saying blackness is self-subsisting or that accidents subsist by themselves. By analogy, all attributes of living beings (life, knowledge, power, will) require a subject. The philosophers have thus stripped God not only of attributes but also of self-subsistence, reducing Him to accidents. Finally, Ghazali promises a separate proof against their ability to demonstrate that God knows Himself and others.

Key Terms
جهل
علم
غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's ignorance of the world is compatible with His perfection.
Correction: Ghazali shows that such ignorance makes God inferior to a dead person who at least knows nothing but is not expected to know; God is expected to know all.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali use to highlight the absurdity of the philosophers' view?
A) A comparison to a dead person
B) A proof from the Quran
C) A mathematical argument
D) A reference to Aristotle

Key Takeaway
Denying God's knowledge of the world is so absurd that it needs no lengthy refutation.

?
When you sin, do you remember that God is the Causer of causes and knows your every excuse? How can you use this awareness to turn to Him in sincere repentance today?
?
If someone told you that God does not know your struggles, would you feel liberated or abandoned? Let this passage remind you that your Lord knows all—will you now speak to Him with that confidence? What will you say?

The Dilemma of Identity and Multiplicity
Ghazali presents and resolves a dilemma about divine self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's dilemma that forces philosophers into either multiplicity or a absurd analogy with human awareness.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of self must be either identical or distinct – both lead to problems.

Having exposed the scandal of their view, Ghazali now presses a logical dilemma. Either God's knowledge of His essence is identical with His essence or it is not. Both options seem to create problems for the philosophers. This lesson walks through the dilemma and the philosophers' attempted escape through the concept of temporality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the passage where Ghazali presents the two alternatives: knowledge is either identical to essence or other. Note the consequence of each.
If identical, then human self-knowledge would also be identical – an absurdity. If other, then multiplicity enters.
2
Examine the human analogy
Notice how Ghazali uses the case of human consciousness: one can be unaware of oneself and later become aware. This shows awareness is other than essence.
This is a decisive refutation of the identity claim.
3
Analyze the objection and reply
The philosophers might object that temporality is irrelevant for God. Ghazali replies that otherness is not determined by time.
He argues that even if awareness is eternal, it could still be other than essence. The imagination can conceive essence without awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جهله مع ذاته، ويصدر عنه؟! وأى نقصان فى عالم الله تعالى يزيد على هذا؟! وليتعجب العاقل من طائفة، يتعمقون في المعقولات بزعمهم، ثم ينتهى آخر نظرهم، إلى أن رب الأرباب، ومسبب الأسباب، لا علم له أصلا بما يجرى فى العالم، وأى فرق بينه وبين الميت - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيراً - إلا في علمه بنفسه؟! وأى كمال في علمه بنفسه، بغيره، وهذا مذهب تغنى صورته فى الافتضاح عن الإطناب والإيضاح. ثم يقال لهؤلاء لم تتخلصوا من الكثرة، مع اقتحام هذه المخازى أيضاً، فإنا نقول: علمه بذاته، عين ذاته أو غير ذاته؟! فإن قلتم: إنه غير ذاته، فقد جاءت الكثرة، وإن قلتم: إنه عين ذاته، فما الفصل بينكم وبين قائل: إن علم الإنسان بذاته عين ذاته، وهذه حماقة، إذ يعقل وجود ذاته في حالة، هو فيها غافل عن ذاته، ثم تزول غفلته، ويتنبه لذاته، فيكون شعوره بذاته غير ذاته لا محالة. فإن قلتم: إن الإنسان قد يخلو عن العلم بذاته، فيطرأ عليه، فيكون غيره لا محالة. فنقول: الغيرية لا تعرف بالطريان والمقارنة، فإن عين الشيء لا يجوز أن يطرأ على الشيء، وغير الشيء إذا قارن الشيء، لم يصر هو هو، ولم يخرج عن كونه غيراً، فبأن كان الأول لم يزل عالماً بذاته لا يدل على أن علمه بذاته عين ذاته، ويتسع الوهم لتقدير الذات، ثم طريان الشعور، ولو كان الذات هو بعينه هذا لما تصور التوهم. فإن قيل: ذاته عقل وعلم، فليس له ذات ثم علم قائم بها. قلنا: الحماقة ظاهرة فى هذا الكلام، فإن العلم صفة وعرض يستدعى موصوفاً. وقول القائل: هو فى ذاته عقل وعلم، كقوله: هو قدرة وإرادة، وهو قائم بنفسه ولو قيل به؛ فهو كقول القائل في سواد وبياض: إنه قائم بنفسه، وفى كمية وتربيع وتثليث، إنه قائمة بنفسه، وكذا في كل الأعراض، وبالطريق التى يستحيل أن تقوم صفات الأجسام بنفسها دون جسم هو غير الصفات، بعين ذلك الطريق يعلم أن صفات الأحياء، من العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة أيضاً، لا تقوم بنفسها، وإنما تقوم بذات، فالحياة تقوم بالذات، فتكون حياته بها، وكذلك سائر الصفات، فإذن لم يقنعوا بسلب الأول سائر الصفات، ولا بسلبه الحقيقة والماهية، حتى سلبوه أيضاً القيام بنفسه، وردوه إلى حقائق الأعراض والصفات التي لا قوام لها بنفسها. على أنا سنبين بعد هذا عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل على كونه سبحانه عالماً بنفسه وبغيره، في مسألة مفردة، إن شاء الله تعالى.
His ignorance with His essence, and it proceeds from Him?! And what deficiency in the world of God Almighty exceeds this?! And let the intelligent person marvel at a group who delve deeply into intellectual matters by their claim, then the end of their investigation concludes that the Lord of lords, and the Causer of causes, has no knowledge at all of what occurs in the world. And what difference is there between Him and a dead person - exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, greatly - except in His knowledge of Himself?! And what perfection is there in His knowledge of Himself, but not of others? This is a school of thought whose form suffices in disgrace, without elaboration and clarification. Then it is said to these people: You have not escaped multiplicity, while also plunging into these disgraces. For we say: His knowledge of His essence: is it identical with His essence or other than His essence? If you say that it is other than His essence, then multiplicity has come. And if you say that it is identical with His essence, then what is the distinction between you and the one who says that man's knowledge of his essence is identical with his essence? And this is foolishness, for it is conceivable that his essence exists in a state in which he is heedless of his essence, then his heedlessness ceases and he becomes aware of his essence, so his awareness of his essence is necessarily other than his essence. For if you say that man may be devoid of knowledge of his essence, then it occurs to him, so it is necessarily other than him. We say: Otherness is not known by temporal occurrence and conjunction. For the identity of a thing cannot occur to a thing, and if something other than a thing is conjoined with the thing, it does not become identical with it, nor does it cease to be other. That He, may He be exalted, has always been knowing of His essence does not indicate that His knowledge of His essence is identical with His essence. And the imagination can conceive the essence, then the occurrence of awareness. And if the essence were identical with this, the imagination would not be possible. If it is said: His essence is intellect and knowledge, so He does not have an essence and then knowledge subsisting in it. We say: The foolishness is apparent in this statement, for knowledge is an attribute and an accident that requires a subject. And the statement of one who says: He is in His essence intellect and knowledge, is like His saying: He is power and will, and He is self-subsisting even if it is said of Him; so it is like the statement of one who says of blackness and whiteness: It is self-subsisting, and of quantity, squaring, and cubing: It is self-subsisting; and likewise for all accidents. And by the same way that it is impossible for the attributes of bodies to subsist by themselves without a body that is other than the attributes, by that same way it is known that the attributes of living beings, such as knowledge, life, power, and will also do not subsist by themselves, but rather subsist by a subject. So life subsists by the subject, so His life is through it, and likewise the rest of the attributes. Thus they were not content to strip the First of the rest of the attributes, nor to strip Him of reality and quiddity, until they also stripped Him of self-subsistence and reduced Him to the realities of accidents and attributes that have no subsistence by themselves. Moreover, we will show after this their inability to establish the proof for His being, may He be exalted, knowing of Himself and of others, in a separate question, God willing.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God knows only His own essence and that His knowledge is identical with His essence, leading to the conclusion that He has no knowledge of particulars. Ghazali begins by expressing astonishment at such a view, which implies deficiency in God's knowledge compared to a dead person who knows nothing. He then raises a dilemma: if God's knowledge of His essence is other than His essence, then multiplicity enters (contradicting divine simplicity). If it is identical with His essence, then how is this different from a human who, when unaware of himself, later becomes aware—showing that awareness is other than essence? The philosophers might object that man's consciousness is subject to change, but God's is eternal. Ghazali replies that otherness is not determined by temporal occurrence or conjunction; even if God's knowledge is eternal, it does not prove identity. He then addresses the philosophers' claim that God's essence itself is intellect and knowledge, so no additional attribute is needed. Ghazali calls this foolish: knowledge is an attribute that requires a subject. Saying that essence is knowledge is like saying blackness is self-subsisting or that accidents subsist by themselves. By analogy, all attributes of living beings (life, knowledge, power, will) require a subject. The philosophers have thus stripped God not only of attributes but also of self-subsistence, reducing Him to accidents. Finally, Ghazali promises a separate proof against their ability to demonstrate that God knows Himself and others.

Key Terms
ذات
كثرة
شعور
طريان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That eternal knowledge must be identical with essence.
Correction: Ghazali shows otherness is not about time; an eternal attribute can still be distinct.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali refute the claim that God's eternal knowledge is identical with His essence?
A) By quoting scripture
B) By arguing that otherness is not determined by temporality
C) By denying God's eternity
D) By using a mathematical proof

Key Takeaway
Eternality of an attribute does not prove its identity with the essence; otherness is a logical distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own moments of heedlessness and awakening, do you truly believe that your awareness is not your essence? How does that realization humble you before a God whose knowledge is eternal yet distinct from His being?
?
If God's knowledge is not identical to His essence, does that make you question His unity? Or does it deepen your trust that His attributes, though distinct, are inseparably part of who He is? Where do you need to realign your understanding?
?
Imagine you are in a state of complete heedlessness of God. Then you remember Him. That awareness is not your essence—it is a gift. Will you let that gift transform how you live today, knowing that your own awareness is temporal and fragile, while His knowledge is eternal and perfect?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Knowledge as an Attribute Requiring a Subject
Ghazali refutes the claim that God's essence is knowledge itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge is an accident requiring a subject, refuting the philosophers' claim that God's essence is itself knowledge.

Core Idea
Saying essence is knowledge is like saying blackness is self-subsisting.

The philosophers attempt to escape Ghazali's dilemma by claiming that God's essence is intellect and knowledge itself, so there is no multiplicity. Ghazali demolishes this move by analysing the nature of knowledge: it is an attribute that must inhere in a subject. To say that essence is knowledge is like saying accidents subsist by themselves, which is absurd. This lesson focuses on that crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' counterclaim
The philosophers say God's essence is intellect and knowledge, no separate attribute needed. Read Ghazali's response.
They are trying to avoid both multiplicity and the human analogy.
2
Understand Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali calls this foolishness. Knowledge is an attribute (accident) that needs a subject. To say essence is knowledge is like saying blackness is self-subsisting.
He draws an analogy to physical accidents: they cannot subsist without a substance.
3
See the implication
If knowledge can subsist without a subject, then all attributes can, reducing God to a bundle of accidents. This strips God of self-subsistence.
Ghazali accuses them of destroying divine simplicity and self-subsistence.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God knows only His own essence and that His knowledge is identical with His essence, leading to the conclusion that He has no knowledge of particulars. Ghazali begins by expressing astonishment at such a view, which implies deficiency in God's knowledge compared to a dead person who knows nothing. He then raises a dilemma: if God's knowledge of His essence is other than His essence, then multiplicity enters (contradicting divine simplicity). If it is identical with His essence, then how is this different from a human who, when unaware of himself, later becomes aware—showing that awareness is other than essence? The philosophers might object that man's consciousness is subject to change, but God's is eternal. Ghazali replies that otherness is not determined by temporal occurrence or conjunction; even if God's knowledge is eternal, it does not prove identity. He then addresses the philosophers' claim that God's essence itself is intellect and knowledge, so no additional attribute is needed. Ghazali calls this foolish: knowledge is an attribute that requires a subject. Saying that essence is knowledge is like saying blackness is self-subsisting or that accidents subsist by themselves. By analogy, all attributes of living beings (life, knowledge, power, will) require a subject. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God as a set of attributes rather than a personal being? How does this lesson challenge you to see God as a living, knowing subject who is distinct from His attributes? This week, make dua asking Allah to make you know Him as He deserves.
?

?

Denying Self-Subsistence and Future Discussion
Ghazali concludes the refutation and previews a future discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali concludes that the philosophers reduced God to the level of accidents by denying Him self-subsistence, and you will see the preview of the upcoming proof about God's knowledge.

Core Idea
The philosophers stripped God of self-subsistence, reducing Him to an accident.

Ghazali wraps up his refutation by showing that the philosophers' denial of attributes logically leads to denying self-subsistence, which reduces God to an accident. He then announces that he will later demonstrate their failure to prove God's knowledge of Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from the attributes to self-subsistence and the preview.
The passage is a conclusion and a preview.
2
Identify the main claim
What is the key accusation Ghazali makes against the philosophers? They stripped God of self-subsistence, reducing Him to an accident.
3
Recognize the preview
Ghazali announces a future discussion on God's knowledge. This shows the structure of his work.
This preview indicates a separate question later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعين ذلك الطريق يعلم أن صفات الأحياء ، من العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة أيضاً ، لا تقوم بنفسها ، وإنما تقوم بذات ، فالحياة تقوم بالذات ، فتكون حياته بها ، وكذلك سائر الصفات ، فإذن لم يقنعوا بسلب الأول سائر الصفات ، ولا بسلبه الحقيقة والماهية ، حتى سلبوه أيضاً القيام بنفسه ، وردوه إلى حقائق الأعراض والصفات التي لا قوام لها بنفسها .
•
على أنا سنبين بعد هذا عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل على كونه سبحانه عالماً بنفسه وبغيره ، في مسألة مفردة ، إن شاء الله تعالى :
By that same method it is known that the attributes of living beings—knowledge, life, power, and will—do not subsist by themselves; rather they subsist by a subject (dhat). Life subsists by the subject, so His life is by it, and likewise the rest of the attributes. Therefore they were not content with stripping the First of all attributes, nor with stripping Him of reality and quiddity, until they also stripped Him of self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafsihi) and reduced Him to the realities of accidents and attributes which have no subsistence by themselves. Rather we will show after this their inability to establish the proof that He, exalted, is knowing by Himself and by other than Himself, in a separate question, God willing.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage concludes a previous argument and previews a future discussion. The author begins by stating that just as attributes of living beings (knowledge, life, power, will) cannot subsist by themselves but require a subject (dhat), so too the philosophers have denied God all attributes and even His self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafsihi). They reduced Him to the level of accidents that have no subsistence. This is a summary of the refutation. Then Ghazali announces that he will later show their inability to prove that God is knowing by Himself, which will be a separate question. This preview indicates the structure of the work.

Key Terms

القيام بنفسه
الذات
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers only denied God's attributes but did not deny His self-subsistence.
Correction: According to Ghazali, they went further and denied even His self-subsistence, reducing Him to the level of accidents.

Checkpoint
What did the philosophers not content themselves with denying to the First, according to this passage?
A) Only the attributes of knowledge and life
B) Only reality and quiddity
C) All attributes, reality, quiddity, and even self-subsistence
D) Only the attribute of will

Key Takeaway
Ghazali concludes that the philosophers reduced God to an accident by denying self-subsistence, and he announces a future proof about God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever unconsciously reduce Him to something less than self-subsistent—perhaps feeling He depends on your worship or understanding? How will you train your heart to see Him as absolutely independent starting today?
?
The philosophers thought they were exalting God by stripping attributes, but ended up making Him an accident. Have you ever justified a wrong belief about Allah, thinking it was more 'sophisticated' or 'reasonable'? Will you renounce that pride and submit to what the Qur’an and Sunnah say, even if it challenges your intellect?
?
Ghazali announces a future proof about God's knowledge. Do you live as if Allah knows every detail of your life—and will you change one habit today to align with that reality?

The Philosophers' Claim: God Has No Definition
Ghazali presents the philosophers' claim and their argument from definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that God cannot have a definition because definition requires genus and specific difference, implying composition.

Core Idea
If God had a definition, He would be composed — but composition is impossible for Him.

The philosophers claim that God can neither share a genus nor be distinguished by a specific difference, because both would imply composition in His essence. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and shows why they conclude that God cannot be defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the opening lines of the passage where Ghazali presents the position: that the First cannot share a genus, differ by a difference, or be subject to division by genus and species.
The phrase 'فى حق العقل' means 'in respect of the intellect'.
2
Understand the argument from definition
Notice how they build on the premise: if no genus, then no specific difference, so no definition — because definition is composed of genus and difference.
A definition (had) is a compound of genus and specific difference.
3
Grasp the implication of composition
They conclude that what has no composition has no definition. This implies that God, being simple, cannot be defined — which they take as a proof of His uniqueness.
This is a key logical move in their overall system.
4
Reflect on Ghazali's purpose
Ask yourself: why would Ghazali start by stating their position so clearly? He is setting up a target for refutation in the coming lessons.
This lesson is purely expository of the opposing view.

مسألة [۷]
في إبطال قولهم : إن الأول لا يجوز أن يشارك غيره في جنس ويفارقه بفصل وأنه لا يتطرق إليه انقسام ، فى حق العقل ، بالجنس والفصل
وقد اتفقوا على هذا ، وبنوا عليه أنه إذا لم يشارك غيره بمعنى جنسى ، لم ينفصل عنه بمعنى فصلي ، فلم يكن له حد ، إذ الحد ينتظم من الجنس والفصل ، وما لا تركب فيه فلا حد له ، وهذا نوع من التركب
وزعموا : أن قول القائل : إنه يساوى المعلول الأول ، في كونه موجوداً وجوهراً ، وعلة لغيره ، ويباينه بشيء آخر لا محالة ، فليس هذا مشاركة في الجنس ، بل هو مشاركة فى لازم عام ، وفرق بين الجنس واللازم في الحقيقة ، وإن لم يفترقا فى العموم ، على ما عرف في المنطق ، فإن الجنس الذاتي ، هو العام المقول في جواب ما هو ، ويدخل في ماهية الشيء المحدود ، ويكون مقوماً لذاته ، فكون الإنسان حيا ، داخل في ماهية الإنسان - أعنى الحيوانية – فكان جنساً ، وكونه مولوداً ، ومخلوقاً ، لازم له ، لا يفارقه قط ، ولكنه ليس داخلا في الماهية ، وإن كان لازماً عاماً ، ويعرف ذلك فى المنطق معرفة لا يتمارى فيها .
وزعموا : أن الوجود لا يدخل قط فى ماهيات الأشياء، بل هو مضاف إلى الماهية، إما لازماً لا يفارق كالسماء، أو واردا بعد أن لم يكن كالأشياء الحادثة، فالمشاركة فى الوجود ليست مشاركة في الجنس .
وأما مشاركته فى كونه علة لغيره ، كسائر العلل ، فهي مشاركة في إضافة لازمة ، لا تدخل أيضاً فى الماهية ، فإن المبدئية والوجود لا يقوم واحد منهما الذات ، بل يلزمان الذات ، بعد أن تقوم الذات بأجزاء ماهيتها ، فليست المشاركة فيه إلا مشاركة فى لازم عام ، يتبع الذات لزومه ، لا في جنس ؛ ولذلك لا تحد الأشياء إلا بالمقومات ، فإن حدت بالاوازم ، كان ذلك رسماً
،
للتمييز لا لتصوير حقيقة الشي ء ، فلا يقال فى حد المثلث : إنه الذي تساوى
Question [7]: On refuting their statement that the First cannot share with another in a genus and differ by a difference, and that no division by genus and difference can apply to Him in respect of the intellect. They have agreed upon this, and built upon it that if He does not share with another in a generic meaning, He does not differ from it by a specific difference, so He has no definition, for definition is composed of genus and difference, and what has no composition has no definition—and this is a kind of composition. They claimed that the one who says: He equals the First Effect in being existent and substance and a cause for another, and differs from it by something else necessarily—this is not participation in genus, but rather participation in a common necessary accident (lazim). There is a difference between genus and necessary accident in reality, even if they do not differ in universality, as is known in logic. For the essential genus is the universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and enters into the quiddity of the defined thing and is constitutive of its essence. Thus a human being's being alive enters into the quiddity of a human—I mean animality—so it is a genus. And his being born and created is a necessary accident that never leaves him, but it does not enter into the quiddity, even if it is a universal necessary accident. That is known in logic with indisputable knowledge. And they claimed that existence never enters into the quiddity of things; rather it is additional to quiddity, either as a necessary accident that does not separate like the sky, or as coming after not having been like temporal things. So participation in existence is not participation in genus. As for His participation in being a cause for another like other causes, that is participation in a necessary relation that also does not enter into the quiddity. For initiatorship and existence neither of them subsist by the essence; rather they attach to the essence (laziman li al-dhat) after the essence is constituted by the parts of its quiddity. Therefore participation in it is only participation in a universal necessary accident that follows the essence, not in genus. For this reason things are only defined by the constitutives; if they are defined by necessary accidents, that is a 'drawing' (rasm) for distinction, not for depicting (taswir) the reality of the thing. So it is not said in the definition of a triangle: that it is that which is equal...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a refutation (question 7) of the philosophers' claim that the First (God) cannot share a genus with others, cannot be distinguished by a specific difference, and is not subject to division by genus and species. Ghazali states their agreed position: if He does not participate in a generic meaning, He does not differ by a specific difference, so He has no definition—definition being composed of genus and difference—and no composition in Him. Then he explains their arguments: 1) They assert that if someone says God equals the First Effect in existence, substance, and being a cause, this is not participation in genus but in a common necessary accident (lazim 'amm). Ghazali clarifies the logical distinction: the essential genus is the universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and enters into the quiddity and is constitutive (muqawwim). Example: a human's being alive (animality) is a genus because it is part of the quiddity, while being born or created is a necessary accident that never leaves but is not part of the quiddity. 2) They claim existence never enters into quiddities; it is additional, either inseparable like the sky's existence or temporally acquired. So participation in existence is not genus-participation. 3) Participation in being a cause is participation in a necessary relation, not in genus—initiatorship and existence are not self-subsistent but attach to the essence after its quiddity is constituted. Therefore things are defined only by constitutives; if defined by necessary accidents, it is a 'drawing' (rasm) for distinction, not a depiction of reality. The text ends mid-sentence giving an example about a triangle, which is cut off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since God is unique, He cannot be defined at all, even in terms of necessary attributes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the philosophers' argument is specifically about logical definition (genus + difference), not about describing God by attributes. Ghazali will later argue that God can be described by attributes without implying composition.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, why does God have no definition?
A) Because He is too great to be defined
B) Because definition requires genus and specific difference, which imply composition
C) Because He cannot be known by human intellect
D) Because He has no attributes

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God cannot be defined because definition requires genus and difference, both of which imply composition incompatible with divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that even denying a definition can be a kind of composition. Are you content to leave Allah's nature as an unfathomable mystery, or do you secretly try to fit Him into a mental box to feel in control? What would it take to truly submit to a God you cannot define?

Distinguishing Genus from Necessary Accident
Ghazali explains the philosophers' distinction between genus and necessary accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical distinction between genus and common necessary accident, and why participation in existence or causality does not imply a shared genus with God.

Core Idea
Existence and causality are necessary accidents, not constitutive of essence — so they cannot be genera.

The philosophers further argue that even if God shares properties like existence and causality with other beings, these are not generic participations because they are necessary accidents that attach to essence without constituting it. This lesson explains the logical basis of that distinction, as presented by Ghazali.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument on sharing
Read the passage starting from 'وزعموا' where they argue that comparing God to the First Effect in existence, substance, and causality is not genus-participation but participation in a common necessary accident (lazim 'amm).
Key term: اللازم العام (common necessary accident).
2
Understand the genus vs. lazim distinction
Note the logical distinction: genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and is constitutive of quiddity; a necessary accident is not constitutive even if it is universal and inseparable.
Example: animality is genus for humans; being born is a necessary accident.
3
Apply to existence and causality
The philosophers claim existence never enters quiddity; it is additional. Similarly, being a cause is an additional relation. So these are not genera.
Existence is either inseparable (like sky) or acquired (like temporal things).
4
Learn the consequence for definition
Things are defined only by their constitutives (muqawwimat); if defined by necessary accidents, it is only a 'drawing' (rasm) for distinction, not a real definition.
This sets the stage for Ghazali's refutation in subsequent lessons.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation: multiplicity does not require genus-species composition
Ghazali refutes that multiplicity requires genus-species composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that multiplicity in a necessary being does not imply composition into genus and species.

Core Idea
Multiplicity can exist without genus-species composition.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' claim that the First Principle must be absolutely simple. He distinguishes between two types of composition—genus-species and essence-attribute—and shows that even if there is multiplicity, it need not imply the kind of composition that undermines unity. He then raises a powerful objection: why can't the chain of caused things end in two distinct necessary causes?

Guided Path
1
Distinguish two types of composition
Notice how Ghazali separates genus-species composition (where species includes genus plus an addition) from essence-attribute composition (where attribute is distinct from essence).
This distinction is key to the argument.
2
Analyze the 'human' example
Ghazali uses 'human' as an example: 'human' is 'animal' plus 'rational'. The species includes the genus, so asking if humanity dispenses with animality is like asking if it dispenses with itself.
This shows that genus-species composition involves inclusion, not externality.
3
Examine the objection about two causes
Ghazali questions why the chain of caused things cannot end at two distinct necessary causes—one for the heavens, another for the elements. They could be distinct in meaning without needing genus-species composition.
This challenges the philosophers' assumption of absolute simplicity.
4
Understand the redness-heat analogy
Redness and heat in one substance are distinct in meaning but do not imply composition into genus and species. Multiplicity here does not impair unity of essence.
This illustrates that multiplicity can coexist with unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن انقسام الشيء إلى الجنس والفصل ، ليس كانقسام الموصوف إلى ذات وصفة ، فإن الصفة غير الذات ، والذات غير الصفة ؛ والنوع ليس غير الجنس من كل وجه ، فمهما ذكرنا النوع فقد ذكرنا الجنس وزيادة ؛ فإنا إذا ذكرنا الإنسان ، فلم نذكر إلا الحيوان مع زيادة نطق ؛ فقول القائل : إن الإنسانية هل تستغنى عن الحيوانية ، كقوله : إن الإنسانية هل تستغنى عن نفسها ، إذا انضم إليها شيء آخر ، فهذا أبعد عن الكثرة من الصفة والموصوف ومن أي وجه يستحيل أن تنقطع سلسلة المعلولات ، على علتين ، إحداهما علة السموات ، والأخرى علة العناصر ؛ أو إحداهما علة العقول ، والأخرى علة الأجسام كلها ؟ ! وتكون بينهما مباينة ومفارقة في المعنى ، كما بين الحمرة والحرارة فى محل واحد ، فإنهما يتباينان فى المعنى ، من غير أن نفرض فى الحمرة تركيباً جنسياً وفصليا ، بحيث يقبل الانفصال ، بل إن كانت فيه كثرة ، فهى نوع كثرة لا تقدح في وحدة الذات
For the division of a thing into genus and species is not like the division of the described into essence and attribute, for the attribute is other than the essence, and the essence is other than the attribute; and the species is not other than the genus in every respect, for whenever we mention the species we have already mentioned the genus and addition; for when we mention 'human', we only mention 'animal' with the addition of 'rational'. So the statement of the one who says: 'Does humanity dispense with animality?' is like his saying: 'Does humanity dispense with itself, when something else is joined to it?' This is further from multiplicity than [the case of] attribute and described. And from what point is it impossible that the chain of caused things ends at two causes, one of them the cause of the heavens, and the other the cause of the elements; or one the cause of the intellects, and the other the cause of all bodies? And there would be between them a distinction and separation in meaning, like the distinction between redness and heat in one locus, for they are distinct in meaning, without our positing in redness a genus-species composition such that it accepts separation; rather if there is multiplicity in it, it is a type of multiplicity that does not impair the unity of the essence.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is the second criticism (the refutation). Ghazali distinguishes between two types of composition: genus-species composition and essence-attribute composition. In genus-species, the species includes the genus and an addition; the species is not entirely other than the genus. For example, 'human' includes 'animal' plus 'rational'. So if one asks whether humanity dispenses with animality, that is like asking whether it dispenses with itself—because animality is part of the definition. This is different from the relation between essence and attribute, where the attribute is distinct from the essence. Therefore, the philosophers' argument that the First cannot have any multiplicity at all is flawed. Ghazali then raises a powerful objection: Why is it impossible for the chain of caused things to end in two distinct Necessary Existents? For example, one might be the cause of the heavens, another the cause of the elements, or one cause of intellects and another of bodies. These two could be distinct in meaning without needing to be composed of genus and species, just as redness and heat are distinct attributes in one substance without implying composition. Even if there is multiplicity, it is a type of multiplicity that does not impair the unity of the essence. Thus, the philosophers' claim that the First must be absolutely simple in all respects is not proven.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
ذات
صفة
كثرة
وحدة الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any multiplicity in a being implies composition into genus and species.
Correction: Ghazali shows that multiplicity can be of a type that does not impair the unity of essence, as in the example of redness and heat in one substance.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it possible for the chain of causes to end at two distinct necessary causes?
A) Because each cause is composed of genus and species
B) Because they can be distinct in meaning like redness and heat in one substance, without composition
C) Because necessary existence is common to both
D) Because they are identical in essence

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that multiplicity, such as two distinct necessary causes, does not require genus-species composition and does not impair the unity of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having parts or being composed, like a complex being? How does Ghazali's example of redness and heat in one locus help you trust that His attributes do not divide His essence? Commit to repeating 'Allah is One' with full conviction after this lesson.
?
Ghazali argues that two distinct necessary causes could exist without composition. Does your heart ever rely on secondary causes as if they were independent? This lesson challenges you: Are you truly depending on Allah alone, or do you assign creative power to other things? Examine your daily reliance and repent.
?
The philosophers feared that multiplicity would nullify God's unity. But Ghazali shows that some multiplicity does not impair unity. Is there any area of your life where you fear that adding something (like knowledge, power, or mercy) to your concept of God might reduce His oneness? Let this lesson liberate you from that fear and deepen your tawheed.
