Divine Attributes
advanced
8 min
Refutation of the Denial of Attributes
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument against attributes by showing their proof only cuts causal chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' proof for a necessary existent only establishes an end to causal chains, not the denial of attributes or absolute simplicity.

Core Idea
The proof of a necessary existent proves causality's end, not simplicity's necessity.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles the philosophers' claim that the necessary existent must be simple and devoid of attributes. He restates their argument based on composition and then counters it by showing that their proof only demonstrates the need for an ultimate cause, not its simplicity. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of Ghazali's argument.
Notice how he begins by quoting the philosophers.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Look for the conditional structure: if parts can exist independently then one is necessary, otherwise the whole is contingent.
This is the compound argument.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali says he already showed this is not impossible in cutting off causal chains, and the proof only indicates that, not the philosophers' invented attributes.
He distinguishes between proving an uncaused cause and proving its simplicity.
4
Recognize the key point
The philosophers have no proof for their specific description of the necessary existent as having no multiplicity.
Without that proof, their entire denial of attributes is unsupported.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا قولكم المحكى عنكم فى نفى الصفات ، وهو أن المركب من الجنس والفصل ، مجتمع من أجزاء، فإن كان يصح لواحد : من الأجزاء ، أو الجملة، في حال، وجود دون الآخر ؛ فهو واجب الوجود دون ما عداه ؛ وإن كان لا للأجزاء ، وجود ، دون المجتمع ، ولا للمجتمع وجود دون الأجزاء ، فالكل يصح معلول محتاج . وقد تكلمنا عليه فى الصفات . وبينا أن ذلك ليس بمحال ، في قطع تسلسل العلل ، والبرهان لم يدل إلا على قطع التسلسل . فأما العظائم التي اخترعتموها في لزوم اتصاف واجب الوجود بها ، فلم يدل عليها دليل ؛ فإن كان واجب الوجود ، ما وصفوه به ، وهو أنه لا يكون فيه كثرة ، فلا يحتاج في قوامه إلى غيره ، فلا دليل إذن على إثبات واجب الوجود ؛ وإنما الدليل دل على قطع التسلسل فقط ، وهذا قد فرغنا منه في الصفات وهو في هذا النوع أظهر
Except your statement reported from you regarding the denial of attributes, that the compound of genus and species is composed of parts; if it is possible for one of the parts or the whole to exist without the other, then it is necessary of existence without the other; and if it is not possible for the parts to exist without the whole, nor for the whole to exist without the parts, then the whole is an effect in need. And we have discussed it regarding attributes. And we showed that this is not impossible in cutting off the chain of causes, and the proof only indicated cutting off the chain. As for the great things you have invented regarding the necessity of the necessary existent being qualified by them, no proof has indicated them. If the necessary existent is as they described it, that there is no multiplicity in him and he does not need others for his subsistence, then there is no proof for establishing the necessary existent; rather the proof only indicated cutting off the chain of causes, and we have completed this in the chapter on attributes, and it is clearer in this type.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument against divine attributes. Ghazali begins by restating their position: a compound of genus and species is composed of parts; if one part can exist without the other, that part is the necessary existent; otherwise the whole is an effect needing a cause. He then responds that he has already discussed this in the section on attributes, and shown that such composition is not impossible in the context of cutting off the chain of causes. The philosophers' proof, he argues, only demonstrates that causal chains must end, not that the ultimate cause must be simple and devoid of attributes. Therefore, their claim that the necessary existent must lack multiplicity is unsupported. If the necessary existent were as they describe—simple and needing nothing—then their own proof would not establish it at all; it only establishes that there is an uncaused cause, which could still have attributes.

Misconception: The proof that there must be a necessary existent also proves that this existent is absolutely simple and without attributes.
Correction: Ghazali argues the proof only establishes an uncaused cause, not its simplicity or lack of attributes.

Genus-Species vs. Essence-Attribute Composition
Ghazali distinguishes composition of genus/species from composition of essence/attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali distinguishes between logical composition (genus/species) and real composition (essence/attribute) in the context of the necessary existent.

Core Idea
Species contains genus; essence and attribute are distinct.

In this passage, Ghazali critiques the philosophers' assumption that any composition implies multiplicity. He argues that the composition of genus and species is not like that of essence and attribute—the species includes the genus, so there is no real separation. This distinction is crucial for understanding how attributes relate to the divine essence without implying composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its translation to grasp Ghazali's argument. The passage is dense; take your time to understand each part.
2
Recognize Ghazali's key distinction
Identify how Ghazali contrasts genus/species composition with essence/attribute composition. He uses the example of 'human' as animal + rationality to illustrate that species includes genus.
3
Understand the implication for divine simplicity
Reflect on why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' analogy is invalid. This supports the Ash'ari view that divine attributes are not separate from essence.
4
Apply to the problem of multiple ultimate causes
Consider how Ghazali extends this reasoning to the possibility of multiple distinct causes. Multiplicity in meaning does not entail real composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن انقسام الشيء إلى الجنس والفصل ، ليس كانقسام الموصوف إلى ذات وصفة ، فإن الصفة غير الذات ، والذات غير الصفة ؛ والنوع ليس غير الجنس من كل وجه ، فمهما ذكرنا النوع فقد ذكرنا الجنس وزيادة ; فإنا إذا ذكرنا الإنسان ، فلم نذكر إلا الحيوان مع زيادة نطق ; فقول القائل : إن الإنسانية هل تستغنى عن الحيوانية ، كقوله : إن الإنسانية هل تستغنى عن نفسها ، إذا انضم إليها شيء آخر ، فهذا أبعد عن الكثرة من الصفة والموصوف ومن أي وجه يستحيل أن تنقطع سلسلة المعلولات ، على علتين ، إحداهما علة السموات ، والأخرى علة العناصر ; أو إحداهما علة العقول ، والأخرى علة الأجسام كلها ؟ ! وتكون بينهما مباينة ومفارقة في المعنى ، كما بين الحمرة والحرارة فى محل واحد ، فإنهما يتباينان فى المعنى ، من غير أن نفرض فى الحمرة تركيباً جنسياً وفصليا ، بحيث يقبل الانفصال ، بل إن كانت فيه كثرة ، فهى نوع كثرة لا تقدح في وحدة الذات
For the division of a thing into genus and species is not like the division of the described into essence and attribute, because the attribute is other than the essence, and the essence is other than the attribute; and the species is not other than the genus in every respect. Whenever we mention the species, we have mentioned the genus and added something. For if we mention 'human', we have only mentioned animal with the addition of rationality. So the statement of the speaker: 'Does humanity suffice without animality?' is like his statement: 'Does humanity suffice without itself? when something else is added to it? This is further from multiplicity than attribute and described. And from which perspective is it impossible that the chain of effects terminates at two causes, one being the cause of the heavens and the other the cause of the elements; or one the cause of the intellects and the other the cause of all bodies? And there is between them distinction and separation in meaning, as between redness and heat in one locus, for they differ in meaning, without supposing in redness a generic and specific composition such that it admits separation. Rather if there is multiplicity in it, it is a kind of multiplicity that does not impair the unity of the essence.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why the philosophers' analogy between generic/specific composition and essence/attribute composition is invalid. Ghazali first clarifies the nature of genus and species: the species includes the genus and an additional difference; e.g., human includes animal plus rationality. So the species is not separate from the genus but contains it. Therefore, asking whether humanity suffices without animality is nonsensical—it is like asking if humanity suffices without itself. This is far less of a multiplicity than the relation between attribute and essence, where the attribute is distinct. Ghazali then extends this reasoning to the possibility of multiple ultimate causes: why can't the chain of effects end in two distinct causes, one for the heavens and one for the elements, or one for intellects and one for bodies? They would be distinct in meaning, like redness and heat in the same subject—they are different but do not imply composition that allows separation. Such multiplicity does not undermine the unity of essence.

النوع
الذات والصفة
المباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing logical composition (genus/differentia) with real composition (essence/attribute) in the necessary existent.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that species includes genus logically, while attributes are distinct from essence. Therefore, the presence of multiple attributes does not imply composition in the divine essence.

Checkpoint

A) By noting that species can exist without genus
B) By giving the example of 'human' which includes animal plus rationality
C) By saying that attributes are prior to essence
D) By claiming that genus and species are identical

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's distinction between logical and real composition preserves divine simplicity while allowing for multiple attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does Ghazali's distinction challenge you to refine your understanding? What will you do to ensure your belief in Tawhid includes this depth?

Think of a time you confused logical categories with real ones in your understanding of Allah (e.g., assuming that because He is knowing and powerful, He must have parts). How does this passage correct that confusion? What will you change in how you reflect on Allah's attributes?

Ghazali uses the example of redness and heat in one locus to show that distinct meanings can coexist without separation. Is your heart at peace with the simultaneous unity and multiplicity in Allah's essence? If not, what doubt remains, and how will you actively strengthen your certainty using this argument?

Refuting the Claim That God Has No Quiddity
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument that God is pure existence without essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God having a quiddity does not imply that His existence is caused by an agent.

Core Idea
A quiddity does not make God's existence an effect.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' assertion that God is pure existence without essence. He demands proof and then deconstructs their reasoning, showing that conceptual dependence does not entail causal dependence. This passage clarifies a key distinction in Islamic theology about divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow of the argument. Note the philosophers' claim and Ghazali's response.
The text cuts off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Find the reasoning: if God had a quiddity, existence would depend on it, making God an effect. Understand why they think this is a problem.
3
Grasp Ghazali's distinction
Focus on Ghazali's reply: dependence can mean efficient cause or something else. He concedes conceptual dependence but denies causal dependence.
This is the core insight of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Ghazali insists on keeping quiddity for God. How does this protect divine necessary existence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٨] في إبطال قولهم : إن وجود الأول بسيط هو وجود محض ، ولا ماهية ولا حقيقة يضاف الوجود إليها بل الوجود الواجب له كالماهية لغيره والكلام عليه من وجهين : الأول المطالبة بالدليل : فيقال : بم عرفتم ذلك ؟ ! أبضرورة العقل أو نظره ؟! وليس بضروری، فلابد من ذكر طريق النظر . فإن قيل : لأنه لو كانت له ماهية ، لكان الوجود مضافاً إليها ، وتابعاً لها ، ولازماً لها ، والتابع معلول ، فيكون الوجود الواجب معلولا ، وهو متناقض . فنقول : هذا رجوع إلى منبع التلبيس ، في إطلاق لفظ الوجود الواجب ، فإنا نقول : له حقيقة وماهية ، وتلك الحقيقة موجودة – أي ليست معدومة منفية - ووجودها مضاف إليها ، وإن أحبوا أن يسموه تابعاً ولازماً ، فلا مشاحة في الأسامى، بعد أن يعرف أنه لا فاعل للوجود ، بل لم يزل هذا الوجود قديماً ، من غير علة فاعلية . فإن عنوا بالتابع والمعلول ، أن له علة فاعلية ، فليس كذلك ، وإن عنوا غيره فهو مسلم ولا استحالة فيه ، إذ الدليل لم يدل إلا على قطع تسلسل العلل ، وقطعه بحقيقة موجودة ، وماهية ثابتة ، ممكن" ؛ فليس يحتاج فيه إلى سلب الماهية فإن قيل : فتكون الماهية سبباً للوجود الذى هو تابع لها ، فيكون الوجود معلولا ومفعولا قلنا : الماهية في الأشياء الحادثة لا تكون سبباً للوجود ، فكيف في القديم إن عنوا بالسبب الفاعل له ؛ وإن عنوا به وجهاً آخر ، - وهو أنه لا يستغنى
Problem [8]: On refuting their statement that the existence of the First is simple; He is pure existence, and has no quiddity nor reality to which existence is attributed; rather necessary existence is for Him like quiddity is for others. The discussion of it is from two aspects: The first is the demand for proof. It is said: 'By what have you known that? Is it by necessity of reason or by its inference? It is not necessary, so it is necessary to mention the method of inference.' If it is said: 'Because if He had a quiddity, then existence would be attributed to it, dependent on it, and necessary for it, and the dependent is an effect, so necessary existence would be an effect, which is contradictory.' We say: This is a return to the source of obfuscation, in the unrestricted use of the phrase 'necessary existence'. For we say: He has a reality and a quiddity, and that reality is existent – i.e. not nonexistent negated – and its existence is attributed to it. And if they wish to call it dependent and necessary, there is no dispute over names, once it is known that there is no agent of existence, rather this existence has always been eternal, without an efficient cause. For if by 'dependent' and 'effect' they mean that it has an efficient cause, that is not so; and if they mean something else, it is conceded and there is no impossibility in it. For the proof only indicated the termination of the chain of causes, and its cutting off at an existent reality and a fixed quiddity, a possible?; so there is no need to negate quiddity. If it is said: 'Then the quiddity would be a cause for existence which is dependent on it, so existence would be an effect and a product.' We say: The quiddity in originated things is not a cause for existence, so how in the Eternal? If they mean by 'cause' the efficient cause; and if they mean another aspect – that it is not independent...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage introduces the eighth problem, where Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God is absolutely simple: pure existence with no quiddity or essence. Ghazali begins by demanding proof, noting that this claim is not self-evident and requires rational inference. The philosophers then present an argument: if God had a quiddity, existence would be attached to it and dependent on it; that which is dependent is an effect, so God would be an effect, which is contradictory. Ghazali responds by distinguishing meanings: he concedes that God has a reality and quiddity, and that existence is attributed to it. But he insists that this does not imply an efficient cause. The dependence here is not causal in the sense of an agent; rather it is a conceptual dependence. The proof of the impossibility of infinite regress only leads to a necessarily existent reality with a quiddity; it does not require negating quiddity. To the further objection that the quiddity would cause existence, Ghazali counters that even in originated things, quiddity is not the efficient cause of existence, so how could it be in the Eternal? If they mean something else by 'cause,' it is merely verbal. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: Ghazali denies that having a quiddity entails that existence is caused by an agent.

Refutation of Essence as Mere Necessary Existence
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' identification of God's essence with necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that identifying God's essence with 'necessary existence' reduces to a negation of reality itself.

Core Idea
Negative attributes cannot constitute an essence.

This passage is a sharp critique of the philosophers who claim God's essence is simply 'necessary existence'. Ghazali exposes the logical emptiness of this identification: it ends up negating any positive reality. Before we can accept any attribute as the essence, we must ensure it is not a mere negation. Let's follow Ghazali's argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ghazali's opening claim
Read the first sentence: The philosophers' discourse ends in pure negation. Understand why negating quiddity negates reality.
Note that 'quiddity' (mahiyya) means the 'whatness' or essence of something.
2
Follow the objection and response
The philosophers might say: His reality is that He is Necessary Existence, and that is the quiddity. Ghazali responds by analyzing the meaning of 'necessary'.
Notice that 'necessary' only means negation of a cause, which cannot be a positive attribute.
3
Grasp the final dilemma
Ghazali presents a dilemma: if necessity adds to existence, multiplicity results; if it does not add, it cannot be the quiddity. Existence itself is not a quiddity, so what does not add to it also cannot be.
This shows the contradiction in the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتنزهون فيما يقولون ، فانتهى كلامهم إلى النفى المجرد ، فإن نفى الماهية نفي للحقيقة ، ولا يبقى مع نفى الحقيقة ، إلا لفظ الوجود ، ولا مسمى له أصلا ، إذا لم يضف إلى ماهية . فإن قيل : حقيقته أنه واجب الوجود ، وهو الماهية . قلنا : ولا معنى للواجب إلا نفى العلة ، وهو سلب لا تتقوم به حقيقة حتى ذات ، ونفى العلة عن الحقيقة لازم للحقيقة ، فلتكن الحقيقة معقولة ، توصف : بأنها لا علة لها ، ولا يتصور عدمها ؛ إذ لا معنى للوجوب إلا هذا على أن الوجوب إن زاد على الوجود ، فقد جاءت الكثرة ؛ وإن لم يزد ، فكيف يكون هو الماهية ؟ ! والوجود ليس بماهية ، فكذا ما لا يزيد عليه .
They objectify in what they say, so their discourse ends up in pure negation. For the negation of quiddity is a negation of reality, and with the negation of reality nothing remains except the word 'existence', and it has no referent at all, if it is not added to a quiddity. If it is said: His reality is that He is Necessary Existence, and that is the quiddity. We say: There is no meaning for 'necessary' except the negation of a cause, and it is a negation by which no reality—not even an essence—is constituted. And the negation of a cause from a reality is a necessary concomitant of the reality, so let the reality be intelligible, described as having no cause and as not being conceivable to be non-existent; for there is no meaning for necessity except this—that if necessity adds to existence, then multiplicity results; and if it does not add, then how can it be the quiddity? Existence is not a quiddity, so likewise what does not add to it.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God's essence is simply 'necessary existence'. Ghazali begins by noting that the philosophers' approach ends in pure negation (nafy mujarrad). To negate quiddity (mahiyya) is to negate reality itself; only the word 'existence' remains, which has no referent unless attached to a quiddity. He then considers the objection: perhaps His reality is that He is Necessary Existence, and that itself is the quiddity. Ghazali answers that 'necessary' means only the negation of a cause—a negation that cannot constitute a positive reality or essence. The negation of a cause is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the reality, not the reality itself. The reality should be understood as having no cause and being necessary, but necessity is not a positive attribute added to existence. If it added to existence, multiplicity would arise; if it does not add, it cannot be the quiddity. Existence itself is not a quiddity, so that which does not add to existence also cannot be a quiddity. Thus the philosophers' view collapses.

النفي المجرد
الوجود
واجب الوجود
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Necessary Existence' is a positive essence defining God's reality.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'necessary' only negates a cause; it does not constitute a positive essence. The reality must be positively intelligible beyond mere negation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, what is the problem with identifying God's essence as 'necessary existence'?
A) It implies that God is composite.
B) It reduces reality to a mere negation, leaving no positive essence.
C) It makes God dependent on creation.
D) It contradicts divine unity.

Key Takeaway
Identifying God's essence with 'necessary existence' fails because 'necessary' is a negation, not a positive attribute that can serve as an essence.

The Muslim Proof for God's Knowledge from Will
Ghazali presents the Muslim proof for God's knowledge from will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Muslims proved God's knowledge of all things through the premise that whatever is willed must be known to the willer.

Core Idea
God's will to create proves His knowledge of all creation.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali explains the Muslim argument for God's knowledge. He shows that because everything other than God is temporally originated by His will, it must be known to Him. This leads to the conclusion that God knows Himself and all things. Let's trace this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Muslim passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'فنقول' to 'عرفوه بهذا الطريق' once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali builds the argument from the division of existence.
The passage is concise; follow the logical chain.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the premise that 'the willed thing must be known to the willer'. This is the foundation of the proof.
3
Trace the steps from will to knowledge
List the steps: everything temporal is willed, so everything is known; then from willer-knowing comes life and self-knowledge.
There are about six logical steps.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that God knows everything, including Himself. Why does Ghazali think this path is sufficient for Muslims?
Contrast with the philosophers' position in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

في تعجيز من يرى منهم أن الأول يعلم غيره ويعلم الأنواع والأجناس بنوع كلى

Problem [11]: On rendering them incapable who among them hold that the First knows other than Himself and knows species and genera in a universal manner.
We say: As for the Muslims, since existence was divided for them into temporal and eternal, and they had no eternal except Allah - exalted is He - and His attributes, and everything else was temporal from Him by His will, they obtained a necessary premise concerning His knowledge: for the willed thing (al-murād) – necessarily – must be known to the willer (murīd). So they built upon this that everything is known to Him, because everything is willed by Him and comes to be by His will. So there is no existent except that it is temporal by His will, and nothing remains except His essence. And whenever it is established that He is a willer, knowing what He wills, then He is necessarily living, and every living thing knows other than itself, so it is more prior to know itself. Thus everything became known to Him, and they knew Allah (1) by this path, after it became clear to them that He is a willer who originates the world.
As for you, if you claim that the eternal world did not come to be by His will, then from where do you know that He knows other than Himself? There must be evidence for it. The gist of what Ibn Sīnā mentioned in verifying that, in folds that go back to two arts:
(2) his speech.
The first art: That the First exists not in matter, and every existent not in matter is pure intellect. And everything that is pure intellect, all intelligibles are disclosed to it. For the hindrance to apprehending all things is attachment to matter and being occupied with it. And the human soul is occupied with managing matter – i.e., the body – so when its occupation is cut off by death, and it has not
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage introduces a new discussion about the philosophers' claim that the First (God) knows others. Ghazali first explains the Muslim position: because all existence is divided into eternal and temporal, and only Allah is eternal, everything else is temporally originated by His will. Since whatever is willed must be known to the willer, everything is known to Allah. Then, because He is a willer who knows what He wills, He is living, and every living thing knows others, so He knows Himself and everything. Thus Muslims know Allah's knowledge through this path. Then Ghazali challenges the philosophers: if the world is eternal and not created by His will, how do you know He knows others? You need evidence. He then summarizes Ibn Sina's argument, which has two arts. He begins the first art: The First exists not in matter; every such existent is pure intellect; and pure intellect has all intelligibles disclosed to it because the obstacle is matter. The human soul is occupied with matter, but after death it can know. The text cuts off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of creation is self-evident and needs no proof.
Correction: Ghazali shows that knowledge of God's attributes is derived from His actions—here, His will to create—and requires rational proof.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the foundational premise for the Muslim proof of God's knowledge?
A) God is pure intellect.
B) Everything is willed by God, so it must be known to Him.
C) God is living, so He knows.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Muslim proof for God's knowledge begins with the will: because God wills all creation, He must know it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every detail of your life is willed and therefore known by God, what secret area of your life are you most afraid He sees? How would your actions change if you truly lived with that awareness?
?
The proof says God knows Himself because He is living. Do you live as if you are fully known by a God who knows Himself perfectly? What would it mean for your prayer life to really know that the One you address knows you better than you know yourself?
?
Since God's knowledge of you comes from His will to create you, do you ever feel that your existence is arbitrary or meaningless? What does this proof demand of you in response to being willed into existence by the All-Knowing?

Ghazali's Challenge to Philosophers and Ibn Sina's First Art
Ghazali challenges philosophers and begins Ibn Sina's first art on knowledge via immateriality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's challenge to the philosophers regarding God's knowledge without creation by will, and the beginning of Ibn Sina's argument from immateriality.

Core Idea
Without creation by will, the philosophers cannot prove God knows other than Himself.

After presenting the Muslim proof, Ghazali turns to the philosophers who claim the world is eternal and not created by will. He demands evidence for their claim that God knows others. He then summarizes Ibn Sina's two-part argument, beginning with the first art: the First is immaterial, so He is pure intellect, and all intelligibles are disclosed to Him. The text cuts off, but we will examine what is given.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's challenge
Read from 'فأما أنتم' to the end. Notice how Ghazali phrases the challenge: if the world is eternal and not willed, how can you know God knows other than Himself?
This is a direct critique of the philosophers' position.
2
Identify the need for evidence
Ghazali insists that the philosophers must provide a proof (dalīl). This sets up Ibn Sina's argument.
3
Read Ibn Sina's first art
Read the first art: the First exists not in matter, so He is pure intellect. Pure intellect has all intelligibles disclosed because matter is the obstacle.
The passage cuts off while discussing the human soul.
4
Understand the premise
The key premise is that materiality hinders knowledge; immaterial beings are pure intellect and know all. This is the foundation of Ibn Sina's proof.
Think about why matter is considered an obstacle.

Ghazali Refutes Ibn Sina on the First's Knowledge
Ghazali examines and refutes Ibn Sina's argument that the First knows all things as pure intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of Ibn Sina's argument that the First knows all things because it is pure intellect, including the logical fallacy of denying the antecedent.

Core Idea
Absence of matter does not automatically imply knowledge of all things—the logic is flawed.

In this passage, Ghazali dissects Ibn Sina's claim that the First, being free from matter, knows all intelligibles. He first clarifies the terms, then identifies a critical logical error: the argument uses an invalid syllogistic form. Understanding this refutation is key to grasping the Islamic critique of Greek-influenced theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Ghazali's presentation of Ibn Sina's argument and his initial acceptance of the First's immateriality.
Notice that Ghazali begins by summarizing Ibn Sina's position.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'intellect'
Ghazali asks what 'intellect' means: does it mean 'that which knows all things' or 'that which knows itself'? This distinction is crucial.
If it means the first, the argument is circular.
3
Analyze the logical fallacy
Ghazali points out that the argument is a conditional syllogism of the form 'If P then Q; not P; therefore not Q' – this is denying the antecedent, which is invalid unless the converse holds.
The horse example illustrates the fallacy.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Without establishing that matter is the only obstacle, the conclusion does not follow. Ghazali's critique shows the need for rigorous logic in theology.
Consider why exclusivity (حصۃ) is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل ما ذكره ابن سينا في تحقيق ذلك، في أدراج يرجع إلى فنين. الفن الأول: أن الأول موجود لا في المادة، وكل موجود لا في مادة فهو عقل محض، وكل ما هو عقل محض فجميع المعقولات مكشوفة له، فإن المانع عن إدراك الأشياء كلها التعلق بالمادة والاشتغال بها، ونفس الآدمي مشغولة بتدبير المادة أى البدن، فإذا انقطع شغله بالموت ولم يكن قد تدنس بالشهوات البدنية والصفات الردية المتعدية إليه من الأمور الطبيعية، انكشفت له حقائق المعقولات كلها، ولذلك قضى بأن الملائكة كلهم يعرفون جميع المعقولات ولا يشذ عنهم شيء، لأنهم أيضاً عقول مجردة لا في مادة. فنقول: قولكم: إن الأول موجود لا في مادة، إن كان المعنى به أنه ليس بجسم ولا منطبع في جسم، بل هو قائم بنفسه من غير تحيز واختصاص بجهة، فهو مسلم. فيبقى قولكم: وما هذه صفته فهو عقل مجرد، فماذا يعنى بالعقل؟! إن عنيتم به ما يعقل سائر الأشياء، فهو نفس المطلوب وموضع النزاع؛ فكيف أخذته في مقدمات قياس المطلوب؟! وإن عنيتم به غيره وهو يعقل نفسه، فربما يسلم لك إخوانك من الفلاسفة ذلك؛ ولكن يرجع حاصله إلى أن ما يعقل نفسه يعقل غيره؛ فيقال: ولم ادعيت هذا؟! وليس ذلك بضروري؛ وقد انفرد به ابن سينا عن سائر الفلاسفة، فكيف تدعيه ضرورياً؟! وإن كان نظرياً، فما البرهان عليه؟! فإن قيل: لأن المانع من إدراك الأشياء المادة، ولا مادة. فنقول: نسلم أنها مانع، ولا نسلم أنها المانع فقط، وينتظم قياسها على شكل القياس الشرطي وهو أن يقال: إن كان هذا في مادة فهو لا يعقل الأشياء، ولكنه ليس في المادة، فإذن يعقل الأشياء؛ فهذا استثناء نقيض المقدم واستثناء نقيض المقدم غير منتج بالاتفاق؛ وهو كقول القائل: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنه ليس بإنسان، فإذن ليس بحيوان؛ وهذا لا يلزم، إذ ربما لا يكون إنساناً ويكون فرساً فيكون حيواناً. نعم استثناء نقيض المقدم ينتج نقيض التالى على ما ذكر في المنطق بشرط هو ثبوت انعكاس التالي على المقدم وذلك بالحصر، وهو كقولهم إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس ليست بطالعة،
The sum of what Ibn Sina mentioned in demonstrating this, in passages that revert to two arts. The first art: that the First is existent not in matter, and every existent not in matter is pure intellect, and everything that is pure intellect, all intelligibles are disclosed to it. For the obstacle to perceiving all things is attachment to matter and preoccupation with it; and the human soul is occupied with managing matter, i.e., the body. So when its occupation ceases through death, and it has not been defiled by bodily desires and base qualities transmitted to it from natural matters, all realities of intelligibles are disclosed to it. Therefore, he judged that all angels know all intelligibles and nothing escapes them, because they too are intellects abstracted, not in matter. We say: Your saying that the First is existent not in matter – if what is meant by it is that He is not a body nor subsisting in a body, but is self-subsistent without spatial location and direction, it is accepted. Then remains your saying: whatever has this description is an abstract intellect. So what is meant by 'intellect'? If you mean by it that which intellects all other things, that is exactly the required conclusion and the point of dispute; how did you take it as a premise in the syllogism of the required conclusion? And if you mean by it something else, namely that it intellects itself, then perhaps your fellow philosophers may concede that to you. But the upshot of it reduces to the claim that what intellects itself intellects others. So it is said: Why do you claim this? It is not necessary; Ibn Sina alone has singled himself out from the rest of the philosophers in this, so how can you claim it as necessary? And if it is theoretical, what is the proof for it? If it is said: Because the obstacle to perceiving things is matter, and there is no matter. We say: We concede that it is an obstacle, but we do not concede that it is the only obstacle. And the formulation of the syllogism on the form of conditional syllogism is that it is said: If this thing is in matter, then it does not intellect things; but it is not in matter, so it intellection things. This is an exception of the negation of the antecedent, and the exception of the negation of the antecedent does not yield a conclusion by agreement; it is like one who says: If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not a human, so it is not an animal. This does not follow, for it may not be a human but be a horse, and thus be an animal. Yes, the exception of the negation of the antecedent yields the negation of the consequent, as mentioned in logic, under a condition: the establishment of the converse of the consequent to the antecedent, and that is through exclusivity. For example, if they say: If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; but the sun is not shining...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage contains Ghazali's refutation of Ibn Sina's first argument for the First's knowledge of all things. The argument has two components: Ibn Sina's claim and Ghazali's detailed critique. Ibn Sina argues: The First is existent not in matter; every existent not in matter is pure intellect; everything that is pure intellect has all intelligibles disclosed to it. The sole obstacle to knowledge is matter; therefore, since the First has no matter, He knows all. Ghazali's critique proceeds in stages. First, he accepts the negative description (not a body, not in matter, self-subsistent). Then he focuses on the phrase 'abstract intellect' (aql mujarrad). He asks: what does 'intellect' mean here? If it means 'that which knows all other things', then that is exactly the point at issue and cannot be used as a premise. If it means 'that which knows itself', then one still needs a proof that self-knowledge implies knowledge of others. Ibn Sina's claim that self-knowledge yields knowledge of others is not self-evident (daruri) and is unique to him among philosophers; it requires demonstration (nazari). Then Ghazali addresses the objection that matter is the only obstacle. He concedes matter is an obstacle but denies it is the only one. He then analyzes the logical form: the argument is a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti) of the form: 'If X is in matter, then X does not know things; X is not in matter; therefore, X knows things.' This is an example of denying the antecedent (istithna' naqid al-muqaddam), which is invalid by general agreement. He gives the example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal; it is not a human; therefore, it is not an animal.' This does not follow because it could be a horse and still be an animal. He notes that denying the antecedent can be valid only if the consequent is exclusive to the antecedent (i.e., if and only if), as in the example 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; the sun is not shining; therefore, daytime does not exist.' The philosophers have not established such exclusivity. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the logical point.

Key Terms
عقل محض
عقل مجرد
قضية شرطية
استثناء نقيض المقدم
المانع
انعكاس التالي على المقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is free from matter, it automatically knows all things because matter is the only hindrance.
Correction: Ghazali shows that matter is not proven to be the only obstacle; the logical argument is invalid unless exclusivity is established.

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does Ghazali identify in the philosophers' conditional argument?
A) Affirming the consequent
B) Denying the antecedent
C) Undistributed middle
D) Affirming the antecedent

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that Ibn Sina's argument for the First's universal knowledge commits the logical fallacy of denying the antecedent, thus invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do you hold about God that you have never examined for logical coherence? Could they be based on faulty reasoning like Ibn Sina's, and are you willing to test them against the same standards Ghazali applies here?
?
If even a great philosopher like Ibn Sina could commit a logical fallacy about divine knowledge, how much more careful must we be? Will you commit, from this lesson onward, to scrutinizing your own theological claims for hidden ambiguities or fallacies?
?
This passage shows that a wrong understanding of a single term—'intellect'—can lead to a mistaken conclusion about God. How might your own misunderstandings of key Islamic terms (like tawheed, qadr, etc.) affect your worship or beliefs? Are you ready to challenge your cherished notions in light of what you've learned here?

Turning the Tables: The Nobility Objection
Ghazali responds to the objection that a knowing effect would be nobler than a cause that knows only itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali responds to the objection that a knowing effect would be nobler than a cause that knows only itself by redirecting the criticism back to the philosophers.

Core Idea
Ghazali uses a philosopher's own objection to expose flaws in their denial of divine will.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a final objection from his opponents: if God knows only Himself, then other beings that know more would be superior to God. This seems absurd. But instead of defending the position, Ghazali turns the argument around, showing that this absurdity follows from the philosophers' own denial of will. Though his full response is cut off, the direction is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: it argues that if God knows only Himself, other beings that know themselves, God, and others would be nobler than God. This is presented as a disgraceful conclusion.
2
Identify Ghazali's response start
Notice that Ghazali does not deny the absurdity. Instead, he says this disgrace is 'lazima' – incumbent upon the philosophers' own denial of will. He is turning the argument back.
The response is incomplete, but the key move is clear.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Ask yourself: why does Ghazali not simply reject the premise? By showing the objection follows from the philosophers' own view, he avoids defending a weak position.
This illustrates a common tactic in Kalam: using an opponent's assumptions to refute them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو قضينا بأنه لا يعرف إلا نفسه ، لكان ذلك في غاية الشناعة ، فإن غيره يعرف نفسه ، ويعرفه ، ويعرف غيره ، فيكون في الشرف فوقه ، وكيف يكون المعلول أشرف من العلة ؟ ! 
قلنا : هذه الشناعة لازمة ، من مقالة الفلاسفة فى نفى الإرادة ، ونفى
If it is said: 'If we judged that He knows only Himself, that would be extremely disgraceful, for others know themselves, and know Him, and know others, so they would be superior in nobility above Him. And how can the effect be more noble than the cause?!' We say: 'This disgrace is incumbent upon the doctrine of the philosophers concerning the denial of will, and the denial of...'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and the beginning of Ghazali's response. The objector argues that if God knows only Himself (as might follow from the previous arguments), then other beings that know themselves, God, and others would be nobler than God, which is absurd because the effect cannot be nobler than the cause. Ghazali begins his response by stating that this disgrace is actually a necessary consequence of the philosophers' own doctrine of denying will (and possibly other attributes). The answer is incomplete, but the direction is clear: the problem lies with the philosophers' position, not with Ghazali's critique.

Key Terms
الشناعة (disgrace)
المعلول (effect)
العلة (cause)
الشرف (nobility)
نفى الإرادة (denial of will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is endorsing the idea that God knows only Himself.
Correction: Ghazali is not endorsing that; he is showing that the objection's conclusion is a consequence of the philosophers' own denial of will, not a necessary outcome of his own argument.

Checkpoint
In the objection, what is the consequence of saying God knows only Himself?
A) It proves God's simplicity.
B) It would make other beings nobler than God.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It is necessary for emanation.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's strategy is to show that the objection actually proves the philosophers' theory absurd, not his own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that knowing God is the highest knowledge. But the objector warns that if God knew only Himself, others would be nobler. Where in your life do you subtly act as if your knowledge of God makes you superior to others, when in fact you barely know Him? Will you repent of that pride today?
?
Ghazali says the disgrace is incumbent on the philosophers who deny God's will. What are you denying about God's will in your own heart—perhaps that He wills your trials, or that He wills your obedience? Why do you run from that truth?
?
If the effect cannot be nobler than the cause, then your soul is an effect of God. Do you live as if you are nobler than your Cause? In what specific habit do you assert your own will over His? Write it down and decide now to surrender that area to His will.

First Objection: Sun and God's Knowledge
Ghazali presents and refutes the philosophers' distinction between sun's light and God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' distinction between the sun's light and God's knowledge, and how he reduces their position to absurdity.

Core Idea
If will is denied, God's creation is no different from the sun's light.

In this passage, Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers who claim that God's knowledge of the whole is essential for emanation, unlike the sun's light which emanates without knowledge. Ghazali responds by pointing out that other philosophers (the naturalists) hold that God's essence necessitates the world without knowledge, so if the philosophers deny will, they have no basis to reject this analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is necessary for emanation, and that this is unlike the sun's light which emanates without knowledge.
The objection tries to establish a unique role for God's knowledge.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Notice how Ghazali invokes the naturalists' view that God's essence necessitates the world without knowledge, to undermine the distinction.
Ghazali is not accepting the philosophers' premise but showing its consequences.
3
Trace the logical consequence
See how if will is denied, God's action becomes necessitated like the sun's light, making the analogy valid.
The key is the negation of will, which both parties (philosophers and naturalists) accept.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Understand that Ghazali is using a reductio ad absurdum: if the philosophers' position leads to the same conclusion as the naturalists, it is self-defeating.
This is a dialectical move, not an endorsement of the naturalist view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : بين الأمرين فرق ، وهو أن صدور الكل عن علمه بالكل ، فتمثل النظام الكلى هو سبب فيضان الكل ، ولا سبب له سوى العلم بالكل ، والعلم بالكل عين ذاته ، فلو لم يكن له علم بالكل لما وجد منه الكل ، بخلاف النور من الشمس . قلنا : وفى هذا خالفك إخوانك ، فإنهم قالوا : ذاته تعالى ذات ، يلزم منها وجود الكل ، على ترتيبه بالطبع والاضطرار ، لا من حيث إنه عالم به ، فها المحيل لهذا المذهب ، مهما وافقتهم على نفى الإرادة ؟! وكما لم يشترط علم الشمس بالنور ، للزوم النور ، بل يتبعها النور ضرورة ، فليقدر ذلك في الأول ولا مانع منه .
If it is said: Between the two matters there is a difference, namely that the emanation of the whole is from His knowledge of the whole, so the representation of the universal order is the cause of the overflowing of the whole, and there is no cause for it except knowledge of the whole, and knowledge of the whole is identical to His essence, so if He did not have knowledge of the whole, the whole would not come from Him, unlike the light from the sun. We say: In this your brethren have disagreed with you, for they said: His essence, exalted, is an essence from which the existence of the whole necessarily follows, in its order by nature and compulsion, not by virtue of His being knowledgeable of it. So what prevents this doctrine, when you agree with them on the negation of will?! And just as the sun's knowledge of light is not required for the necessity of light, rather light follows it necessarily, so let that be supposed in the First, and there is no impediment to it.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a response. The objection claims that the relation between God and the world is different from the relation between the sun and its light, because the world emanates from God's knowledge of the whole, and that knowledge is His essence, so if He lacked it, the world would not exist. The response argues that other philosophers (the naturalists) hold that God's essence necessitates the world necessarily, without requiring knowledge, so if you (the philosophers being addressed) agree with them in denying will, why not also accept this? The analogy with the sun is used: just as the sun's light follows necessarily without the sun's knowledge, so too can the world follow from God without knowledge, on the assumption of no will.

Key Terms
علم بالكل
فيضان
النظام الكلى
إرادة
طبع واضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali accepts the philosophers' premise that God's knowledge is necessary for creation.
Correction: Ghazali is not endorsing the premise; he is showing that if will is denied, the philosophers' position leads to the same conclusion as the naturalists, thus refuting their distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection's analogy between God and the sun?
A) God must have a separate act of will to create.
B) The sun's light emanates without knowledge, so God's creation can too.
C) The sun's light requires knowledge, so God's creation requires knowledge.
D) The sun and God are completely incomparable.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' distinction by showing that if will is denied, God's emanation is no different from the sun's light, which is necessitated without knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you tend to attribute them to 'nature' or 'habit' rather than to your will and intention? How does this passage challenge that tendency in your relationship with God?
?
If you truly believed that every event in your life comes from God's will (not mere nature), how would your response to difficulty change? What habit would you have to repent from?
?
The philosophers made a distinction to protect God's transcendence, yet Ghazali shows it fails. Are there any 'distinctions' in your own thinking that you use to keep God at a distance while still claiming belief? Will you examine them today?

Second Objection: Does God's Action Imply Knowledge of All Consequences?
Ghazali refutes the claim that God's action implies knowledge only of the first effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God's action implies knowledge only of the first effect, using the example of a stone rolling down a mountain.

Core Idea
Action does not require knowledge of all consequences — only the initial cause.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the second objection from the philosophers and then refutes it. He uses a clever analogy to show that even in voluntary action, knowledge of the immediate act does not entail knowledge of all subsequent effects. This refutes the necessity that God must know only the first intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument's structure come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhythm of objection and response.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers claim that action implies knowledge of the product, and God's only act is the first intellect. Therefore God only knows that.
Remember their emanation framework.
3
Spot Ghazali's analogy
Ghazali compares this to a stone rolling down a mountain. The person who pushes it knows the initial push but not all subsequent collisions.
This shows action does not require exhaustive knowledge of consequences.
4
Understand the distinction
Ghazali notes that the philosophers' reasoning fails in voluntary action and even more so in natural action. The stone is moved voluntarily, yet its later effects are not necessarily known.
This undermines the philosophers' claim.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how the analogy shows that action and knowledge are not as tightly linked as the philosophers assume. Their own system is inconsistent.
Why does Ghazali say 'they have no answer'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلم لهم أنه الوجه الثاني : هو أن صدور الشيء من الفاعل ، يقتضى العلم أيضاً بالصادر ، فعندهم فعل الله تعالى واحد ، وهو المعلول الأول ، الذي هو عقل بسيط ، فينبغى ألا يكون عالماً إلا به ، والمعلول الأول يكون عالماً أيضاً ، بما صدر منه فقط ، فإن الكل لم يوجد من الله تعالى دفعة واحدة ، بل بالوساطة والتولد واللزوم ، والذي يصدر ممن يصدر منه ، لم ينبغي أن يكون معلوماً له ؟! ولم يصدر منه إلا شيء واحد ؟ بل هذا لا يلزم في الفعل الإدارى، فكيف فى الطبيعى ؟ ! ، فإن حركة الحجر من فوق جبل، قد تكون بتحريك إرادى ، يوجب العلم بأصل الحركة ، ولا يوجب العلم بما تتولد منها بوساطتها، من مصادمته ، وكسره غيره ، فهذا أيضاً لا جواب لهم عنه .
We concede to them that the second aspect: that the emanation of a thing from the agent necessitates knowledge also of what emanates. In their view, the act of God, exalted, is one, namely the first effect, which is a simple intellect. So it follows that He is not knowledgeable except of it. And the first effect is also knowledgeable only of what emanates from it. For the whole did not come from God, exalted, at once, but through mediation, generation, and necessity. And that which emanates from that from which it emanates, should it not be known to him?! And there did not emanate from him except one thing? Rather, this is not necessary in the case of voluntary action, so how about in the case of natural action?! For the movement of a stone from above a mountain may be by voluntary movement, which necessitates knowledge of the origin of the movement, but does not necessitate knowledge of what results from it by way of mediation, such as its colliding and breaking something else. So this too they have no answer for.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is the second objection and its response. The objection states that if an agent's act implies knowledge of the product, then since God's only act is the first intellect, He would know only that. The response uses an analogy: a voluntary stone-throwing requires knowledge of the initial push but not its subsequent collisions. Similarly, even if God's act is natural (non-voluntary), it does not require knowledge of all consequences. Thus, the philosophers' own model—where only one thing proceeds from each cause—does not force God to know all effects.

Key Terms
صدور
الفاعل
معلول أول
عقل بسيط
فعل إرادي
فعل طبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali accepts the philosophers' premise that action implies knowledge of the effect.
Correction: Ghazali uses the premise to show an inconsistency in their system; he does not endorse it.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali use to show that action does not require knowledge of all consequences?
A) The sun's light.
B) A stone rolling down a mountain.
C) The first intellect.
D) The difference between voluntary and natural action.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's stone analogy shows that action does not imply knowledge of all consequences, refuting the philosophers' objection about God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action without knowing its full consequences, do you truly trust that God's knowledge covers what you cannot foresee? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on your own planning?
?
Think of a time you were certain of the outcome of your action, only to be surprised by unforeseen results. In light of Ghazali's stone analogy, how can you humble your confidence in your knowledge and rely more on God's omniscience?
?
If God knows only the first effect (as the philosophers claim), then His knowledge would be incomplete. How does your heart respond to the assurance that God's knowledge is infinite and not limited to immediate causes? Do you live as if you believe that?
